THE RED PAGE.
CONSECRATED BY A KISS
BRANDER MATTHEWS, writing “On
the publishing of plays,” declares that
“ theatric art may be sufficient unto
itself without the aid of literature.”
Invention and construction, the appeal to the eye and
to the ear, the ingenious utilization of contrast, climax
and suspensethese qualities are essential in the theatre,
and in the theatre they may suffice for our pleasure with
little or no aid from literature.
Adopting Matthews’ view, the Rev. T. H. Tay-
lor, asked by Mr. J. 0. Williamson to base a
religious drama upon Wagner’s opera of Parsifal
—with a “star” part for Miss Brune in the
character of Kundry—has subordinated literary to
dramatic interest, and has made of the legend a
play that is playable—with how much success the
gods may presently decide. The drama of four
acts and many tableaux is written in regular
blank verse; there is plenty of action; and neces-
sarily much of the action is composed of Kissing.
The symbolic strife of Good and Evil in the
legend finds practical expression in the crucial
Kiss which Parsifal gives Kundry. That tre-
mendous Kiss is the test of Parsifal’s purity of
soul, and a guarantee of Kundry’s salvation.
Parsifal triumphs through the ordeal in Act 111,
Sceneii— “The Gardens op Temptation.
Mr. Taylor has developed the Kiss lusciously.
“Upon banks of fallen blossoms recline in lan-
guorous attitudes, clad in diaphanous raiment,
the Sirens.” They explain that Kundry is wait-
ing for “ a pure, cold Kiss”—and laugh horridly.
Parsifal enters, and the Sirens “sway in voluptu-
ous movement.” Parsifal tells them that he has
never kissed anybody but his mother, and he will
even risk Kundry. Then their chaperone
addresses the Sirens indignantly :
Your charms have failed. Go— I Dance—display them
more!
Fill all the air with bodily vibrations 1
(Jusb here the audience stop talking and lean
forward in their seats for the “ Temptation Danoe
OF Sirens.”— aha!)
They swirl around Parsifal ; then with Kissing action
advance on him like waves and retire quivering like
foam.
During the last “movement ” of Dance, Parsifal
shivers—the sword shakes in his hand—he opens his
eyes—and looks at the Sirens. As the dance grows
more and more furious, he suddenly stands up and,
sheathing his sword, veils his eyes with his hands;
then, turning, goes up to Terrace, followed by the
dancing Sirens. As he reaches the bank of flowers,
he turns again and looks at them, and then wards
. them off with his hands. 4s the last note sounds
with a loud finale, and the Sirens stand with arms
outstretched, he raises his hands to Heaven.
Parsifal : Christ! Help me I—l am tempted 1
(Turns, and falling on one knee buries his face in his
hands.)
Fortunately Parsifal is able to resist the tempta-
tion to Kiss a Siren ; and Kundry enters with an
attractive song :
On joyous wing,
The song I sing
Shall greet my glorious King of Light,
Beneath whose glance
My blood shall dance,
And Body following Soul shall spring
A flying rose of blushing charms,
Into the Heaven of his arms ;
There —there to cling
Through, heat of noon,
In a swoon
Of bliss—-
And Kiss—and Kiss 1
Kundry explains to Parsifal that now she sees
his eyes she knows he is really good and pure, not
like those other naughty knights whose kiss
Was such pollution, that I always bear
A fresh red rose—to Kiss (Kissing it;—and Kiss again.
Hoping its sweetness may remove the stain
From these pale lips, pining for One Pure Kiss.
Here there is some uncertainty as to whether
Kundry’s kiss will prevail over/Parsifal’s kiss, or
vice versa ; and the chaperone urges the magician
to excite Kundry’s kiss to its highest or lowest
capacity :
Zana (clutching his arm) : Thou must!—thou SHALT 1—
by Satanas thou shalt !
Cause her to dance till all his senses whirl—
Then LEAP into his arms—and kiss—THE KISS 1
(The intense capitals are the author’s.)
(Klingsor proceeds to make great hypnotic gestures,
using Spear as a wand. Zana signs to those Sirens
who are visible. These commence to sway sloivly to
the music, which now begins very softly, increasing in
power and intensity.
Parsifal : Princess, Christ keeps me pure. Come, let
me Kiss.
Still Miss Kundry Brune hesitates coyly, until,
possibly perceiving signs of fatigue in Parsifal,
she invites him to “(very sweetly) heal me too —
with one sweet Kiss ! ” Parsifal attempts to take
her at her word, but she says
Ah, wait 1 Bliss grows by contemplating bliss
(beginning to dance quietly around him and throwing off
outer robe),
And like a butterfly before it sips
Some honeyed flower, I will flutter round
Until I dart on thee with thirsting lips—
(clasping Parsifal.)
And all my body clings to thine—like this 1—
And all my soul melts into ONE LONG KISS !
(Kisses Parsifal.)
All this pother about a Kiss! Still, one may
imagine that the skilful delay has roused wild ex-
citement in the audience, who are seen by the eye
of imagination clutching their chairs hard, and
heard by the ear of ditto breathing loudly and
emotionally. .It is really a highly dramatic Kiss;
and, if the Sirens do their voluptuous duty, that
scene alone should make the play.
Act IV is to some extent an anti-climax,
following on the great crisis of the Kiss. How-
ever, it rounds off the religious aspect of the
drama, which closes with all the characters kneel-
ing on the stage, while Parsifal alone stands
behind the altar.
He bears a distinct resemblance to the Christos; and
slowly elevates'me, Holy Grail, from ivhich streams
a great rosy radiance that gradually spreads over all.
Parsifal. Yet growing daily more and more—
LIKE HIM !
Mr. Taylor has accomplished a difficult task
well. He has made a coherent story with many
good acting episodes, which need only to be
handled adequately in order to touch the great
religious soul of the people, not to speak of the
great kissing body. Of Miss Brune’s adequacy
as Kundry one is moderately assured. But
Parsifal ? Who will interpret Parsifal ? The
shade of Wilson Barrett stretches vague, im-
ploring hands from the farther bank of Styx.
Alas !
ANOTHER GAY GOSSIP
OLD Isaac Pitman, being dead, yet liveth in
the considerable London firm of Sir Isaac
I ltman and Sons, Ltd., which has recently
commenced in the general publishing
trade. In On the Queen’s Errands, by Philip
Wynter, they give a really diverting book of
gossip that one can recommend gaily. Capt.
Wynter born a 839, wasson of a Vice-Chancellor
of Oxford University. He joined the army, went
to India, and served through the Mutiny.
Losing health, he returned to England, and
took employment as Queen’s messenger,
carrying despatches for thirty-five years at
a salary of 4CKM. a year and 11. a day expenses
w l , e '? , He tells the incidents of his
public life brightly and fluently, and can be read
throughout three hundred pages without a page
of tedium. His book presents good portraits of
~, Ly° ns > Hobart Pasha, and others, and has
a full index.
_
Wynter begins by regretting that his father
was no exception to the rule that all good clergy-
men have large families. “My father married
twice, and lam the eldest of twelve children by
his second wife.” His boyish reflections exhibit
the curious English selfishness by which notable
families are preserved intact.
I know that it is the opinion of some thoughtless
people that it is an advantage to have sprung from a
fertile stock and to be one of many. I cannot share this
feeling. I have a vivid recollection of the gloomy
apprehensions with which I viewed the repeated prepara-
a fr ® B . h arr >val, foreseeing too clearly that each
addition would curtail our luxuries and diminish my very
small share of patrimony. My brothers and sisters con-
tinued to succeed each other like the waves of the sea—
preceded always by the advent of a stern, tyrannical old
woman who required continual nourishment in the shape
of port wine, soup, beef steaks, etc. She seemed to take
entire command of every servant, and was (except as to
morals), a domestic Catherine 11.
After a time, I looked on her visits as a matter of
course, and when I sadly enquired why so many infants
should appear, she assured me they were sent as blessings
from heaven, and that they were a proof of the approba-
tion of the Almighty, and a reward for the piety of the
parents, and instanced the countless offspring of the
Hebrew patriarchs, who were the objects of a special
Divine favour. I learned, when older, that these same
patriarchs were hardly models of conduct, and observed
that, however pious people might be, they seemed rather
to repine than to rejoice as their children multiplied.
Anyhow, the old woman’s theory was doubted by me
at a very early age, and I ceased to consider myself as a
prize awarded to deserving parents.
The rest of the book expresses the author’s
opinions just as bluntly, and he is even mildly
“ O-shokking ! ” His garrulity is full of good
stories. He tells how an Archbishop of York
was out riding when he noticed a fox crossing the
road.
Looking round he saw, some fields off, the huntsman
casting his hounds, but owing to a bad scent, huntsman
and hounds were quite at a loss. His Grace, observing
this, waved his hat and gave a loud view halloa. The
huntsman came galloping up to the road, and seeing
from whom the sound came—recognizing the Archbishop
of York—he blew his horn and the hounds hit the line at
once. The huntsman muttered to himself, “That’s
Gospel, by G —d 1 ”
When Wynter lived at Oxford the Duke of
Wellington was University Chancellor, and had
the duty of. reading out at Commemoration the
honours awarded to various scholars; the list
being in Latin.
Thus, the Christian name of those christened
Charles was, of course, Carolus, with a short o. The
Duke persisted in reading this name as Carolus, pro-
nouncing the o long, until at last some Official, shocked
at his ignorance, broke in with “I beg your Grace’s
pardon, but at Oxford we always call this word Carol us.”
“Oh, very good, said the Duke, “I’ll remember in
future.”
Presently the name Jacobus occurred, which the Duke
boldly read as Jachbus, pronouncing the o short. Again
the don interposed. “ I beg your Grace’s humble pardon,
but we prefer to call that Latin name Jacobus.” “No !
damn it 1 ” said the Duke. “ You can’t have it always
your own way! If your reading of Carolus is Carolus,
Jacobus must be JacObus.”
Wynter has a soldierly penchant for a little
wholesome cursing. He says that at his first
school he had such a passion for learning that he
used to dream of the Latin grammar, and in a
somnolent state between sleeping and waking he
tried to decline the noun “dominus.” A stupid
nurse, overhearing the first syllable of this sub-
stantive, mistook it for the well-known British
expletive, and reported to his father that he was
damning her and the rest of the young family.
He refers to the slackness and want of enthu-
siasm shown by his senior brother officers in
India. As to Indian missions :
In common with the majority of those who have lived
among Orientals, 1 was never sanguine as to the result of
missions to Mahommedans and heathen being such as to
justify the enormous sums subscribed for them at home •
the converts being few, and not worth the trouble and
expense of their conversion. At any rate I am satisfied
that no real progress is ever made by married missionaries
or by those who become missionaries for a livelihood, and
to give them an income to marry on. . . . But the English
missionary societies continue, and will always continue,
to publish rose-coloured accounts of the progress of
Christianity, in order to obtain the subscriptions and be-
quests of large sums, which had much better be expended
in converting the heathen population of English towns
Wynter refuses to waste the patient reader’s
time :
I always think of the first Lord Ellenborough’s wise
move. He was sitting in the House of Lords listening to
a dull, wearisome harangue when he suddenly started up.
His neighbour said, “You are not going, are you
“ Yes,” said Lord Ellenborough with a sigh, taking up
his hat, “ I am answerable to the Almighty for the use of
my time.”
An anecdote of the late Lord Yalentia :
No ipan was more popular or had a kinder heart but
he had a very old-fashioned and highly spiced vocabu-
lary, and his language was of the strongest even when
wishing a friend good morning. Among his other pecu-
liarities was his dislike to tramps, and the story used to
be told of him that one day one of his daughters looking
out of the window at Bletchington, saw a begssai ap
proaching the house. She rang for the butler arid told
him to order off the mendicant before Lord Valentia
should catch sight of him. The butler drew himself up
and replied solemnly, “ Is Lordship ’ave damned ’ini
already, miss.”
Wynter knew Charles Lever, whose “conversa-
tion was so overpowering! y amusing that I was
literally tired with continual laughter.”
Lever told me that one day, having nothing to amuse
him, he strolled into a Dublin police court. A man was
being tried for stealing a watch, and on being asked what
answer he had to the charge, he simply sighed and said
\our honour, it is a very sad business, and the less we
say about it the better.”
Lever also related that when attending a funeral in
Ireland—the mourners being assembled in a dining-room
before the ceremony—the footman burst in, and address-
in" his master said, “Your honour, the Carpse’s cousin
would like a word with you 1 ”
J*
Wynter is satirical at the expense of people’s
ignorance regarding the duties and privileges of a
Queen’s messenger. After all, it is a rather tire-
some job. No matter how fond you are of travel-
ling, you get a little too much of it. Friends and
acquaintances are apt to add to the official
burden:
A gentleman once wrote to me evidently in perfect
good faith, “ What a calling is yours ! What an oppor-
tunity for spreading the light of the Gospel among less
enlightened Christians ! Will you accept a few tracts of
deep interest, and some copies of the Holy Scriptures for
distribution in Prance, Spain, and Italy, and thus gain
for yourself the glorious title of an evangelist?”
I answered him at once, thanking him for his offer,
but added that I had never heard or read of more than
four evangelists, and greatly feared that I had neither
the talents nor devotion needed to make a fifth.
Wynter didn’t mind taking charge of boys
going to and from school, as they were generally
well-behaved, and spent the time in sleep or (less
often) in subdued sobs when returning to their
melancholy fate ; but he always steadily refused
to take charge of a dog, though often asked in
very sweet tones. He also refused to take charge
of a Crown Princess’s midwife. One of his col-
leagues, being very anxious to stand well with the
Foreign Office, undertook the job of conveying
eight canary-birds as a present to the Sultan of
Turkey.
The Foreign Office official went on to say that my friend
would be further entrusted with a bag of birdseed, a
bottle of tonic, and a brush to clean out the birdcage. I
suppose (though he did not so inform me) that he was
further charged with a penny whistle for the enlivenment
of the songsters, and “ a little bit of sugar for the bird.”
... I never heard how the cages were cleaned out—but
perhaps this was done by francs offered to a deputy.
The canaries unfortunately could not be packed
in the usual Foreign Office sack, which, after four
or five years’ service, becomes plastered over with
polygpt railway labels, and is at once recognised
by foreign railway porters.
My late colleague, Capt. Haworth, used to affirm that
his sack, if properly packed and propelled by a sound
kick on its protuberant curves, would arrive at St. Peters-
burg without any traveller to look after it, provided that
he had previously whispered to it its destination 1
Occasionally Wynter quotes, and neatly. He
quotes Sarah Bernhardt’s compliment to Blowitz
in sending him a present with the message “La
plus fine de corps au plus fin d’esprit.” He quotes
Sydney Smith’s attractive alliteration: “The
same passion which peoples our parsonages with
chubby children animates also the Arminian and
burns in the breast of the Baptist.”
One receives the impression that Turkey, as
well as being a misunderstood country, is a country
where one is misunderstood.
I will conclude my remarks on Turkey with an account
of a distinguished English lady receiving a perfectly unin-
tentional insult from the Sultan. She was presented to
His Majesty, and won his heart by her praises of Turkey
and its capital. The monarch when taking leave pressed
into her hand a small case, begging her to wear its con-
tents in remembrance of her visit.
When outside the door, she opened the case and saw an
order with an unintelligible inscription in Turkish. Meet-
ing a Court Official on the stairs, she showed him her
treasure and asked for particulars. The functionary, with
a profound bow, explained that it was the “Star of
Chastity ” of the second class. The lady naturally felt
aggrieved, and pointed out to her husband that of such
order she was entitled to be a first class member, and she
could not understood the meaning of degrees in such a
distinction, and I am sure that it is equally puzzling
to me.
The Oxford of to-day Wynter never enters
without a mental shudder. He cannot get over
the complete change in the aspect of Oxford and
its population. The poetry of the place has
departed since (among other things) the introduc-
tion of the married fellow and the consequent
accompaniment of an endless procession of per-
ambulators, “varied only by the number of
female students now to be met with in the Uni-
versity precincts, some with spectacles and some
without, many of them badly dressed and worse
shod, whose learning hardly seems to me an
excuse for their appearance. ”
J*
A gruesome note. Palmer, the Rugeley poi-
soner, executed in 1856 for the poisoning of Cook,
had a brother who one day met a turf-handi-
capper, who tells this story :
Ue said to me, “ Mr. Merry, you are passing my house,
won’t you step in and take some refreshment?” In I
went and was offered some biscuits and sherry. Just as
a bit of fun I sniffed at the glass ana said, “ I hope it’s a
right ’un this time, Palmer.” You would hardly believe
what he looked like. I thought he’d have had a fit, and
he bustled me out of the house like a dog. He never
could have been able to understand a joke.
Some horsey stories. A gentleman going to
Symonds’ livery stables at Oxford had a large
number of horses paraded for inspection.
To every hunter that was brought out, this would-be
customer made some objection. One horse had upright
shoulders, another slack loins or curby hocks, or brittle
feet, till at last Symonds took the gentleman into his
office, and, placing him in front of a blackboard, offered
him, with a bow, a lump of chalk, saying, “ Now, sir, if
you will be kind enough to sketch the sort of animal you
require, I will try and find him for you.”
William Nicholl, the Nottingham bookmaker,
was an easy prey to subscription hunters.
Amongst others, one of those devoted women, the
Sisters of Charity, visited Nicholl, explaining to him her
object and soliciting his help. His sympathies were at
once roused, and in his rough northern accent he replied,
“Thou shalt. have a bit at once, I’ll give thee a pony.”
The religious lady, in her ignorance of turf slang, said dis-
appointedly, “That would be very kind of you, Mr.
Nicholl, but a pony would be of small use to us. We have
nowhere for it to stand, no one to look after it, and could
not afford to keep it. Now, if instead of a pony, you
would put your name down for ten pounds, we should
indeed be grateful.” “Doon with you, miss,” replied
Billy, as quick as lightning, remarking to a bystander,
“ See, I’ve shot the old silly for fifteen quid.”
Major Dixon was starting a large field of horses at
Punchestown, when owing either to the misbehaviour of
the horses, or the eagerness of the riders, there were
several breaks-away before the horses could be got off.
At last a jockey, to whom these delays caused the
greatest anxiety, shouted out to Dixon, “ Oh ! for the
love of God, Major, dhrop the flag. Sure the whisky is
dyin’ in me iverv minute.”
Capt. Wynter is excellent company.
MEMORANDA
John Murray publishes a third edition in one
volume of The Creevey Papers (10s. 6d. ), which
had such a success of entertainment upon their
appearance in 1903. The new issue makes a
handsome Bvo. with a score of portraits, and may
be commended again for the delectation of elderly
gentlemen and younger connoisseurs in literature.
In its present form no country library should miss
it. Everywhere you dip you meet some quaint
and charming reflection of our hard-drinking,
hard-living, hard-swearing ancestors —from the
lady whose husband broke open her boxes and
found twenty locks of hair, with a label on each
containing the name of the lover to whom it
belonged— to Sam Whitbread, who wrote :
My Dear Creevey,
To the usual occupations of hanging Mad Dogs,
swearing Bastards, convicting Poachers, and such like
country performances, has been added the amusement of
Hunting, which I have resumed to the great benefit of
my health, and the complete fugitation I hope, of all
critical Deposits in consequence of high living—
and a few years after cut his throat with a razoy.
Very few books nowadays are packed so full of
native human interest.
Possibly it is worth noting that Zadkiel’s Al-
manac and Ephemeris for 1906, published Dec.,
1905, at London, prophesies on p. 67 that
At San Francisco Mars and Saturn are on the fourth
angle or lower meridian. In the vicinity of that great
city underground troubles—probably a serious earthquake
—will be destructive about Christmas Day or the latter
half of February. The winter will be stormy and colder
than usual. Otherwise, the quarter will be a prosperous
one for California.
Now, if people believed in astrology—as people
do !
An agreeable commentator in Syd. Telegraph
revolts against the expression of “ Platonic friend-
ship ” applied to those disinterested alliances be-
tween persons of opposite sexes. “ How did the
absurd expression ever gain currency ? ” Well, it
originated with Plato. In his famous “ Banquet ”
the guests devote great attention to a love which
is not that of the senses—a celestial love —and
Plato as the editor of the speeches has been
credited with approving this, as no doubt he did.
In Shelley’s translation, Pausanias tells us :
Thus, not all love nor every mode of love is beautiful,
or worthy of commendation, but that alone which excites
us to love worthily. The Love, therefore, which attends
upon Venus Pandemos, is, in truth, common to the
vulgar, but presides over transient and fortuitous con-
nexions, and is worshipped by the least excellent of man-
kind. The votaries of this deity seek the body rather
than the soul, and the ignorant rather than the wise,
disdaining all that is honourable and lovely, and con-
sidering how they shall best satisfy their sensual necessi-
ties. This Love is derived from the younger goddess,
who partakes in her nature both of male and female.
But the attendant on the other, the Uranian, whose
nature is entirely masculine, is the Love who inspires us
with affection, and exempts us from all wantonness and
libertinism. Those who are inspired by this divinity seek
the affections of those who are endowed by nature with
greater excellence and vigour both of body and mind.
It will be, perceived that the cant usage has
adapted Platonic dogma to modern conditions,
and the width of Plato’s applications would
probably shock most of those who refer to him.
Probably the narrowness of modern applications
would surprise Plato.
The accidental death of Pierre Curie—crushed
by a dray in a Paris street —is the whole world’s
scientific loss. Possibly he was cycling (he was
an ardent cyclist)—and Paris boulevards are very
slippery after April showers. He was still some
years from fifty, and much was hoped from his
research. Now that is left to Mme. Curie, quite
as clever a chemist as was her husband.
“ Bowed by the weight of centuries,” The Man
With The Hoe leans no longer except in reproduc-
tions—his original destroyed by the San Fran-
cisco fire. This is another regret,; for from the
liberal harvest of pictures few such masterpieces
are gleaned. By the way, will Edwin Markham,
or another devotee of the Californian Muse,
recollecting earthquake emotions in tranquillity,
be able to display to us the tremendous destruc-
tion in which Millet’s canvas was a pinch of ashes?
J. S. Henderson, in The Athenceum , rectifies an
old error. Matthew Arnold and others have made
Heine the butt of Goethe’s remark about a poet
unnamed who “had every gift, but wanted love.”
Henderson proves that it was Count Platen whom
Goethe spoke of.
Bret Harte’s daughter says her father w'as half
a Hebrew—the son of a Jewish father and a
Christian mother. Then it must have been the
Jewish half that wrote the stories, and the Chris-
tian half that spent all the money and died cent-
less.
April Scribner's reproduces a series of interest-
ing etchings by Seymour Haden... “Mr. and
Mrs. Jack London are going around the world for
The Cosmopolitan in a 40ft. sail boat ’’—and to
Australia?... Ambrose Bierce pleads for a Ra-
tional Anthem, and indeed we need it.
Woods Hutchinson, in April McClure’s , has an
article to be studied by all food-cranks not per-
manently blasted. Hutchinson, of course, is a
first-rate authority, and not ten per cent, of his
argument can be controverted. He is that rare
swan in a region of quacks—a doctor who unites
experience, imagination, and sense. His con-
clusion is that “ so tnfCny varied elements and sub-
stances are needed in the ‘ perfect diet ’ that an
intelligent omnivorousness is our only safeguard.”
In reaching that, he demolishes delusions by the
score. For example:
Last of these delusions is the breakfast-food fad.
Pompous and pestiferous as its attitude is, it was born of
humble and highly respectable parentage, namely, Scotch
oatmeal. Its birth and conception would appear to have
been somewhat in this wise-: “The Scots are a great
people ; oatmeal is their principal food : therefore, oat-
meal is a great food.” It has nothing to do with our
argument, but it may incidentally be remarked in passing
that there is a fatal error in this syllogism. videKcit , that
the most convincing proof the Scotch have given of their
greatness has been their abilitj' to live on oatmeal at all.
The secret of their wonderful success, both mental and
physical, lies in the fact,that any nation trained to sur-
vive a diet of oatmeal and the shorter catechism, could
survive anything and flourish anywhere.
A.G.S.

A CASE at MARRICKVILLE
A Very Convincing Narrative*
THE CASE OF MRS. C COOPER.
(BY A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
In an interview with a representative, Mrs. Caroline Cooper, of No. 3 Fitzroy-street, Marrickville. gives
some information to the afflicted, which should help them considerably. Her statement runs as follows :
“ My health is altogether different now to what it was some years ago, as there was a time when I was nothing
but a total wreck, and now people are always telling me how well and strong I look. Yes, I am not like the same
person at all, for now lam always contented and happy, instead of being intensely miserable like I used to be. But,
heaven knows, I had enough to make me so, for the pains in my head were so great that for many a day I hardly
knew what I was doing. In each temple there was a nasty beating sensation, which caused me so much agony that
often I thought I would surely go mad.”
“You would welcome the arrival of bedtime, so that you could sleep and forget your afflictions for a while ? ”
suggested the writer.
“ One vvould naturally think so, but I didn’t, for the simple reason that I could seldom get any sleep : and, oh,
how those hours of wakefulness used to wear me out. Truly, when I got up of a morning, I felt worse than if I had
not been in bed at all, and I always had a bitter taste to make me feel sick for the rest of the day. Sometimes I got
so thoroughly exhausted that it would have been impossible to walk had I tried to, but knowing how weak I was I
used to stay indoors to be on the safe side, for when one is like that it is such an easy thing to meet with a serious
accident. And, another tiling, I was accustomed to taking fits of giddiness, and often I went right off, and did not
know anything that was going on, so you see I had to be careful. And wasn’t I nervous ! Goodness, I only had to
hear a little shout or a whistle and I’d actually jump again, and it would be a long time afterwards before my nerves
would steady down a bit. Why, my mind was so unhinged through excessive nervousness that I was afraid to be
left alone, and would not have stayed in the dark for a fortune'; besides, if I closed my eyes for a few seconds all sorts
of ugly faces could be seen, and those were the kinds of hideous sights I usually witnessed in my dreams.”
“ Have you any idea what caused your head pains and other ailments ? ”
“ I think it was all through receiving a sunstroke when a girl at my home in Goulburn. I can say with truth,
up to. the period when I purchased some Clements Tonic through a friend’s advice, and then, for the first time during
my long illness, I learned what it was to get some substantial ease from my torments. At first I thought the relief
would be only short-lived. It seemed too much to expect anything else after the disappointments of the past; but
Clements Tonic softn put an end to that pessimistic idea by making me feel better and stronger every day, and when
I realised that my improvement was making such regular strides, my heart was overflowing with gratitude. Con-
tinuing with Clements Tonic I lost the burdensome feelings that affected me after taking food, and soon the windy
spasms that had often doubled me up were quite cured. My eyes had always felt dull and swollen, and I used to be
irritable and anxious from morning to night, fearing that something was going to happen; but, strange as it may
seem, after using a few' bottles of Clements Tonic, my mind became as free from anxious thoughts as a babe’s. lam
glad to say that Clements Tonic left nothing undone, for it gave me a digestion that has remained sound for many
years past, and, in regard to nervousnf js, well, I hardly know how to express myself. At all events, I can say that
my nervousness was soon overcome, and if anybody suffers from sleeplessness there’s nothing in this world they need
take but Clements Tonic, to make their nights peaceful and refreshing. That remedy succeeded in giving me perfect
health, and I would rather have a bottle of Clements Tonic in the house than anything else. Well, I hope others in
need will read my story ; so you may publish it as you feel disposed.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Caroline Cooper, of 3 Fitzroy-street, Marrickville, In the State of New' South Wales, Commonwealth of Aus-
tralia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission to pub-
lish in any way my statements, which I give voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Sgd.) CAROLINE COOPER.
Subscribed and declared at Enmore this seventeenth day of November, one thousand nine hundred and four,
before me.
F. A. DRURY, J.P.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the People are Gray by the Sea.
Oh ! it hurts me to be by the murmuring sea,
When mosquitoes are looking for gore,
And the infants all yelp ’mid the weed and the
kelp,
While their parents prance round on the shore
In an infantile game with a suitable name,
In which Bert is allowed to kiss Flo—
Which they play when they roam—which they
wouldn’t at home,
And it’s much to their credit I know.
Then they sigh, and they nag, as they haul from
the bag
All the food that they’ve dragged from the train,
And they take off their clothes and repose on
their nose
In the sand—which is very inane.
But the missus, so cross, and the boss, and the
hoss,
They have sorrows enough, you’ll agree ;
And they toil and they fret through the year, so
they let
All their intellects rest by the sea.
It no doubt gives relief, and it soothes all their
grief,
And it leaves them with no one to thank,
Though it tends to annoy when I see them enjoy
Having all their minds perfectly blank.
Which I’m certain’s the case, for they’re able to
race,
Though they’re old, in the scrub, with great glee;
And they now and then sing, or play kiss-in-the
ring,
Which is really quite mournful to see.
But the shark winks his eye at the gay octopi,
And they drift closer in to the shore.
For the fish who is wise he may snap up a prize,
And the bather may feel very sore.
So the shark does his best, and he tries to digest
What he gets—but I wish to remark
That the infant so rough is a great deal too tough,
And I feel very much for the shark.
Then as Shakspeare, who’s dead, is alleged to
have said
In a play that he wrote : “ There’s the rub ! ”
When a couple that wrangle waltz round in a
tangle
Of kids —all their own —in the scrub.
And they pant and they bound and keep spinning
around,
While they threaten the that will squall
Like “a (something) young Turk”—and it’s very
like work,
And I wonder they do it at all.
’Tis a whimsical thought, though its value is
naught.
That the things I despise they enjoy,
And the things that / prize are the things they
despise,
And that each does the other annoy.
So I think I’ll retreat in the holiday heat
To a cave in a hill far away,
For it’s dreadful to be by the murmuring sea,
When the people take pains to be gay.
VIC.
KODAK.
At the Fall of the Leaf.
lii the solemn Autumn time, when the birds were
flying,
And about my pathway the red leaves fluttered
down;
Thus methought I heard a maid, and one to her
replying—
(lt was mournful Autumn time, and all the earth
was brown).
“Oh minstrel, minstrel, fold thy wing, and rest
beside my portal,
Sorely have I missed thee, ah, whither hast thou
been ?
Once thou sang’st me magic songs of youth and
joy immortal.”
“ That was long and long ago, when all the leaves
were green.”
“Minstrel, tarry yet awhile, and wake for my
recalling,
Silver streams that tell anew of joys we two have
seen ;
Summer days and springing flowers, and rosy twi-
lights falling.”
“ That was long and long ago, when all the leaves
were green.”
‘ ‘ Minstrel dear, hast thou forgot a nest amid the
branches,
Where thy mate sat peacefully through sunny
days serene ?
Still thy nest awaiteth thee, and still the sunshine
dances.”
“ That was long and long ago, when all the leaves
were green.
“ See’st thou not around thee, sweet, the withered
blossoms falling ?
Never comes the summer back, nor banished joys
I ween,
All I loved are thrown away, gone beyond re-
calling ;
I must seek another shore, where all the leaves are
green. ”
So he spread his wings for flight, and I, my tears
down-stealing,
Prayed, as on my pathway lone the red leaves
fluttered down :
“Oh, ye gods of Time and Change, list to my
appealing,
Of your pity call me hence ere all the earth be
brown.”
S.A.
A.M.B.R.

Prick Go,
TRYING TO GET IT ALIGHT AGAIN.
An ex-farm-burner wrote last week to the S.W. Herald suggesting the despatch of a contingent to help Natal to slay its nigger.
There has been a great silence since.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director,
llowto Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige, the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY. MAY 3, 1906.
Gr. H. REID’S ANTI-SOCIALISM.
G. H. Reid has at last been ill-advised enough to
announce the platform of his anti-Socialist party.
It was a foolish burst of candor, for it showed
with absolute conclusiveness that he knows
nothing of what Socialism means and couldn’t
identify anti-Socialism if it was in the witness-
box before him. There is a case for anti-Social-
ism. The general mass of civilised humanity
recognises that the social problems which
the quick rise of modern industrialism created
can best be solved by community action. They,
therefore, believe in Socialism—to a greater
or less degree. Opposed to them is a smaller
band, of which the main strength is recruited
from the descendants of the old CoBDEN-ites, the
brutal believers in the doctrine of laissez faire.
They admit—must admit—the evils which afflict
life; how so many toil merely for bread to give
them strength to toil to morrow for more bread ;
how yet others struggle feverishly, despairingly,
for even that much kindness from life; how all
beauty and poetry and grace of existence is denied
to millions that a few may wallow in barbaric
sensuality. They see these things—must see them,
for they exist, and cry every day to Heaven for re-
dress. And their remedy is : “ Let things be. In
time, matters will adjust themselves. Those who
must starve will starve. The surplus population will
be removed. The unfit will be eliminated. Let
things be. Freetrade and Individualism are
above all things—immutable laws which must not
be broken.” Allied in purpose, though not in
sympathy; with these soulless wretches are a few
visionaries of too much soul—generally men who
dream dreams, live on nuts, and wear their hair
long. They believe that jf Individualism or
philosophic Anarchy were established, at once
human nature would be transformed ; and, freed
from the shackles of compulsion, man would
do always the square thing by his brother
man. These two classes form the genuine anti-
Socialists.
* * * *
But Reid represents neither school. He has not
the brutal courage of one, nor the imagination of
the other. As an anti-Socialist, he is a more
glaring Yes-No than he was as a Federalist.
His Melbourne platform divided itself into two
sections —platitudes and suggestions for more
Socialism. Putting aside one or- two timid pro-
posals to repeal existing Acts, or parts of Acts,
Reid’s working platform, as announced, is this :
Platitudes.
To fight to the death against Socialism. This means
nothing. A general statement that G. H. Reid is
strenuously in favor of dishonesty, or is an uncompro-
mising opponent of fair play, would have just about the
same practical value as a political pronouncement.
The removal of the distrust and irritation existing
between the State and the Commonwealth authorities.
This is another large, shadowy platitude which assists
nothing in defining G. H. Reid’s position. He might be
in favor of removing distrust and irritation—presuming
that they exist, which it is not necessary to presume on
Mr. Reid’s mere Yes-No—by the Commonwealth giving
way to the States on all points of difference. Or again,
he might favor the States giving way to the Common-
wealth. He doesn’t say which, or how. He might also
want to split the difference, but he doesn’t sav where it
should be split.
A fiscal trace. This fiscal-sinking idea Mr. Reid has
stolen from the Labor party—the only difference being
that they wish to “sink the fiscal issue” in order to
do something' else ; he, in order to do nothing else. It is
not a policy, but a negation of a policy.
The substitution of peace and goodwill between labor
and capital for the existing lamentable state of strife and
ill-will. This platitude means, in brief, the Millennium.
But it doesn’t suggest anything practical.
Thoroughly efficient national defence. To this might
be added, as a political principle, thoroughly good bread
and lots of beer. There’s no need to be anything but
generous when throwing benefits around in this large,
vague way.
Socialistic Proposals.
Extension of the Braddon clause to admit of the imme-
diate establishment of old age pensions. Old age pensions
are rank Socialism ; and Mr. Reid follows the example of
many other Socialists in being careless as to the
means by which he finances his Socialism, for the
extension of the Braddon clause won’t supply any more
money than there is now.
Strenuous efforts to introduce Australian products to
the world's markets. More platitudinous Socialism. In
the individualists’ creed it is not the business of the
Government to interfere in such matters.
Strenuous efforts to encourage immigration to Austra-
lia. (It takes a very fat man to offer to be so strenuous.)
Again Socialism.
Formation of a great Agricultural Bureau. Socialism
with the name blown in the bottle.
Uniform Australian laws in regard to banking, in-
surance and insolvency. Socialism 1 The Anti-Socialist
should declare robustly against any such State inter-
ference.
The use of the powers of Government and Parliament
to promote Private Enterprise instead of destroying it.
Again Socialism. Anti-Socialism would decline to use the
powers of Government to either help or hinder Private
Enterprise. Its attitude is always “ let alone.” Private
Enterprise that leans on the State ceases to be private.
The establishment of Courts of conciliation to supple-
ment the Courts of Industrial Arbitration. Once more
Socialism. Always Socialism 1
Imitation of Canadian immigration methods in
developing the resources of Australia. Those methods
are radically Socialistic and comprise Government sub-
sidy of immigration ships, and general regulation of the
settling of the new population. An anti-Socialist’s im-
migration policy should be to let the immigrants come if
they want to, and stay away if they r want to, without
any State encouragement or help.
Resumption of the Northern Territory and a vigorous
policy of introducing European settlement and develop-
ing topical agriculture. Still Socialism 1
* * * *
So the anti-Socialism programme of G. H.
Reid boils down to five platitudes, and eight
proposals for more Socialism ! It takes the con-
summate impudence of Yes-No to put that for-
ward seriously. But Mr. Reid and Mr. Reid’s
followers recognise that there is no importance to
be attached to platforms or pronouncements so
far as their propaganda is concerned. Theirs is
the policy of stagnation, and, if possible, reaction.
They are prepared to support Reid, no matter
what Reid professes, for they have the Reid as-
surance that, if he succeeds, he will stand in the
way of the desire of the Australian nation to fill
the factories and till the fields of this
Commonwealth under conditions giving freedom
and fair reward to all. And, the pity of it, there
is, on the other side, as yet, no man who is stand-
ing out as the pronounced enemy of that policy of
stagnation on all points. The anti-Socialists, or,
rather, anti-Australians, are united and effe'ctively
organised. They will vote blindly for Reid, no
matter what his professions—Freetraders, Insect-
arians, Land Monopolists, all are united
by a common purpose. The Australians are
divided between a Labor party, which has no
fiscal faith (without which all other good works
are useless), and the Deakin party, which is only
beginning to definitely profess any strong faith
at all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Again, the Federal Capital.
A member of The Bulletin staff gets this letter
from a Monaro man :
I write to ask you if you can throw some light upon the
discussion of the Premiers’ conference upon the Federal
capital question. The Herald and Telegraph give only
a short and confusing report of same. They say that the
Premiers laid all the blame for delay and possible extra
expense upon the Federal Government, and that it was
“ stated” that the Federal Government would and could
have the matter settled when and where it choose.
What does all this really mean? In the face of the
refusal by N.S.W. Parliament to give the land at Dalgety,
do they mean that the Federal Government should
acquire by purchase, or is it that they could settle it by
backing down to Sydney ? The paragraph also says that
the feeling of the conference was adverse to the bush
capital, but would not recommend an alteration of the
Constitution. If you could give me some more definite
information regarding the meaning of the remarks made,
and who made them, I would feel obliged, as you know
I am very much interested, and I wish to know what
Deaiiin said, and if it is likely that anything will be done
next session to settle it. As the report is so brief, and
the Sydney dailies are without comments, it seems as if it
did not please them ; Tun Bulletin should give something
more intelligible about it.
Which is of sufficient importance to call for a
public reply. First, to produce the press report,
which read (exactly the same in both Sydney
papers)
The Conference discussed the question of the site of the
Federal capital, and there was a general expression of
opinion that the responsibility for all the delay and any
duplication of attendant expenses rested on the Com-
monwealth Government. It was stated that the Federal
Government would, and could, settle the matter where
and when it chose.
The feeling of the Conference was generally against
the present provision for a “ bush capital.” The Premiers
did not, however, feel it competent for them to suggest
an amendment of the Constitution.
The Conference ultimately decided upon a course of
action which means waiting developments.
As to whether The Bulletin can throw more
light on that report, it is doubtful. The Premiers’
Conference did not condescend to allow the public
to know how it conducted its business. Its
sittings were secret. The press had to rely
for its reports upon what Joseph Carruthers,
or some instructed minion of Joseph Car-
ruthers, told it. What the statements made—
by Joseph or Joseph’s minion—really amount to
is simply a sample of fiction which is not even in-
genious. The Australian Government, in the
first instance;' implorbd the State of N. S. Wales
to choose a capital site and offer it. N. S. Wales
steadily refused. Then the Commonwealth asked
for a number of sites that it might pick out one
for itself. N. S. Wales offered several, including
Dalgety, and reserved from sale the State Lands
around those sites. The Australian Parliament,
after making exhaustive inquiries, selected. Dal-
gety, which happened to be within gunshot of the
site which a N.S. Wales Royal Commission had
reported as the best. Now N.S. Wales refuses to
give up Dalgety, and is waiting for Deakin to
give up his affability and take it. That’s the real
position. In the inspired statement to the press
the Commonwealth is first blamed for the delay
(which is a lie, though a common enough lie in
Sydney). Then the opinion is expressed against a
“ bush capital.” If the Conference was averse to a
bush capital—the only one which the Common-
wealth is allowed by the Constitution to build-
why should it blame the Commonwealth for the
delay which prevents a “bush capital” being
built ? Anyhow, the “ bush capital ” can only be
averted by an amendment of the Constitution,
and these amazing Premiers don’t recommend an
amendment of the Constitution. Their sour
grumble only sets forth that they agree
to be dissatisfied, but do not agree on
any remedy for their own dissatisfaction.
It is possible that just what is reported represents
a true account; on the other hand, it is for many
reasons improbable. It is improbable that the
version of Joseph, or Joseph’s minion, is a
true one because it i 9 so inherently self-
contradictory. It is a branded-on-the-brow
lie—a sort of triangular Yes-No-perhaps-Yes
yarn which makes no straight statement and
is horribly mixed in its navigation. If it repre-
sents the actual truth of what the Premiers said,
then The Bulletin thinks a good deal worse of
Kidston and Price and—yes—it. thinks worse of
Bent. After a struggle, it finds it cannot think
worse of Cauruthers.
♦
Not Quite So Affable.
Prime Minister Deakin’s promise—at least it
sounded like a promise—made last week at Cam-
perdown (Vic.), that his Government will fight in
real earnest this coming session for a genuine Pro-
tectionist tariff is good news. It represents
Alfred minus his customary eloquence, diffuse-
ness, affability, circumlocution, and tendency to
ambie in pleasant circles round and round his
subject. It reads for once like the declaration of
a fighting leader, and if Alfred calls Parliament
together early, gets to work promptly, and puts
his foot down hard on every semblance of obstruc-
tion, then he must win. But to win he must
tread down obstruction as it was never trodden
before. The session will necessarily be short, for
this House of Representatives dies in December
at latest, and members from remote parts want to
get away by October in order to fly into the
uttermost parts of the backblocks and see their
constituents. There are many things to do in
about four months, and if anything is to be
effected in tariff matters it will be necessary to
explain clearly to the Parliament that all that can
be said on the subject has already been said
twenty times over, and that this time it must be
a matter of votes, not of talk. The majority must
rule; and it is as great an outrage on Responsible
Government if the measures that the majority
desires are talked out by the minority as it would
be if they were blocked - by physical violence.
Minority rule, whether it is imposed by a jaw-
bone or a club, is the same public injury, and the
same public disgrace. If Pumpkin Smith and
Wilks, and Fuller, and Johnson, and Kelly,
and all the rest are remorselessly cut off in the
bloom of their ditfuseness, after three minutes’
conversation, and debate is sawn off at the ground
every time to allow of the question being put,
and Parliament swears off talk and tattle, and
sticks to the only thing that really matters—the
work of discovering by a vote what is the will and
decision of the majority—then a great deal is
possible even in a session of four months. A
great deal may also be done to facilitate progress
if the Government introduces half its measures in
the Senate, and thus keeps both Houses busy from
the first. But the great essential is to lay the axe
to the root of the tree, and the tree is Gabble, and
the root of it is mostly Pumpkin Smith,
A Boundary Question.
Melbourne Age has lately thrown a bomb
or a clod or something into the middle of the
problem re the division of the Murray waters, by
raising again the question as to the boundary
line between N.S.W. and Victoria, and the Bent
Government seems inclined to take the matter up.
This problem lias simmered more or less in an
unofficial way for half a century, but it has never
yet been brought into court, though Irvine, in
liis cold, glacial fashion, was thinking of taking it
there just before he fell from the Premiership.
Bent possibly will take it there. Bent is a
fighting man ; he has fought with cabbages,
banks, building societies, drainage and manure
problems, land companies, liquidators and muni-
cipal councillors, and he is the most likely Pre-
mier Victoria has ever had to bring the matter to
an issue. The case seems, so far as all the known
evidence goes, sufficiently uncertain to provide, if
it comes into court, a very large fight and a very
great deal of costs. Victoria is commonly sup-
posed to be the old district of Port Phillip under
a separate State Government and a new name,
and the old district of Port Phillip certainly ex-
tended as far north as the Murrumbidgee. The
official description of Victoria in the Act of
Separation passed by the British Parliament was
this :
Bounded on the north and north-east by a straight line
drawn from Cape Howe to the nearest source of the Eiver
Murray, and thence by the course of that river to the
eastern boundary of the colony of South Australia.
South Australia, by the way, never was a
“colony”; it was, officially, a “province,” which
small detail shows the British Parliament’s loose
way of doing things. The N.S.W. authorities
were entrusted with the execution of this British
Act, and they drew the line from Cape Howe to
the Indi River (a distance of 110 miles)
and left things at that. The Indi is really
the uppermost end of the Murray. But the
distance from Cape Howe to the Eumeralla
River is only 75 miles; the Eumeralla is
really the top of the Murrumbidgee, and the
Murrumbidgee is a tributary of the Murray ;
therefore, if it were merely a question of taking a
bee-line from Cape Howe to the nearest tributary
of the Mutiny, and then following the water as it
flows, the problem would be quite simple, and
Victoria’s claim to the Riverina would be indubit-
able. And Britain has kept on officially describe
ing the boundaries of Victoria in the same words
in subsequent documents, which is something by
way of corroboration. On the other hand, how-
ever, there is the difficult statement (for Victoria)
that when the boundary has found the nearest
tributary of the Murray, it is to follow “ the course
of that river ” to the S.A. border, and this doesn't
seem to quite fit in with a that would
be about three-quarters Murrumbidgee and only
one-quarter Murray. Such a line would follow the
Murrumbidgee much more than the Murray. The
Victorian theory, in reply, is that the Upper Murray
wasn’t the Murray in the days of separation,
but the Hume; that the Murray only started
where the Hume and the Murrainbidgee joined ;
and that a line drawn from Cape Howe to the
nearest tributary of the Murray (the Murruni-
bidgee), and following that river all the way,
would also follow the course of the Murray so far
as there was, at the time the Act was passed, any
Murray to follow. Here the question seems to
get tangled up with the hideously careless nomen-
clature of early Australia. Certainly the Upper
Murray was once the Hume, and the alternative
name is not yet quite dead. Even on the map
which the N.S.W. .Railway Department hangs up
in its first-class carriages to-day the upper
river appears as the “Murray or Hume.”
Also part of the upper river was occa-
sionally the Goolwa. Also part of it was, and
still is, the Indi. And, apparently, there was no
fixed or definite date when the name Murray
spread up the river and partially superseded
the name Hume. In a N.S.W. publication of as
recent date as 1877 the great river is called the
“Gulba or Murray” away down near the S.A.
border; it is plain “Murray” between
Echuca and the mouth of the Murrumbidgee ; it
is “ the Goolwa or Murray ” between Echuca and
Albury; then it becomes “the Murray or Hume” ;
then “the Indi or Murray ’’; then nothing in par-
ticular. That showed the name “Murray” as
predominant but no more. It hadn’t a monopoly.
Heaton’s “ Dictionary of Dates ” speaks casually
of the Hume as “the boundary between N.S.W.
and Victoria,” and adds “ the name of the Murray
was subsequently given to its western portion,
which was discovered by Stubt.” There is no
date attached to this statement, but the book was
published in 1879. It is a horribly mixed river
and a horribly mixed case. If Victoria goes to
law, it must fight on the main issue that the boun-
dary was to begin with a line from Cape Howe to
the Murray’s nearest tributary, and on the side
issue that the old district of Port Phillip,
which was supposed to be made into Vic-
toria, did extend up to the Murrumbidgee. And
it must make the best case it can out of the awk-
ward proviso that the boundary line was to follow
“the course of that river” (the Murray), by
showing, if it can, that the Murray didn’t then
crawl any higher than about Euston. As for
N.S.W.’s 55 years adverse possession, and all the
vasttangleof State and Federal Acts of Parliament
and official doings which implied a recognition
of the present boundary, they will have to be
explained away as best Victoria can—with Costs.
There is a stupendous vista of Costs in view.
Speaking in a gambling spirit, and going to a
great extent on the fact that long possession
counts for a lot, The Bulletin just at present is
inclined to bet that the event, if pursued to a
finish, will end thus :
Costs 1
New South Wales 2
Victoria 3
After all, so long as the Riverina remains in
Australia, it doesn’t seem to matter much what
State it is in.
Labor Perfidy.
Sydney Wokker publishes, with an air of pride,
a special report of the lamentable proceedings of
the recent Victorian Political Labor Conference,
at which it was decided to oppose, with as much
resolution as the stolidest Tory, those men who
stood to the Labor party in its need, and kept the
Watson Government alive. The decision was,
without mincing words, infamous. Recall the
circumstances ! Watson had taken office with a
LabQr Ministry, to which was added Higgins
(whose throat now is to be cut by the Labor
Leagues, if they can), having the direct support
of about a third of the House, and assured of a
general sympathy from the Deakin party. Very
soon Deakin fell under the spell of Reid and
took up an attitude of armed neutrality towards
Watson. Thereupon certain members of
Deakin’s party took the bold step of break-
ing away from him and practically joining
Watson lsaacs, M auger, Hume Cook,
Crouch, Lyne, Chanter and Groom being
the most notable. These men, especially the Vic-
torians, by cutting themselves away from their
own party, exposed themselves to the bitter
hostility of the Tory interests in their electorates,
and to their opponents became “as bad as the
Labor members.” It was only reasonable that
they should expect, therefore, that to their friends
they should be “as good as Labor members.”
They didn’t stipulate for so much as direct Labor
support at the polls in return for the direct sup-
p at they had given to Labor in Parliament; but
it was arranged between them and J. C. Watson,
acting for his party, that they should have im-
munity from Labor opposition at the next elec-
tions. The arrangement was in every way
equitable, and was of such a nature as any party
leader was entitled to come to on his own authority.
But Watson took the precaution of consulting
the executive of the Labor League and securing
its approval beforehand. He further reported the
agreement to the last Inter-State Labor Con-
ference, and it endorsed that alliance whilst
deprecating future agreements of the sort. Now
the Victorian Labor League Conference decides
to repudiate the Labor leader’s action, to repudiate
all the endorsements which were given, and to
turn perfidious arms on the men who have come
into the Labor camp as friends and allies. There is
no other word but “ infamous ” for this. And the
Victorian L.L. Conference, as if to aggravate the
offence (if that were possible), came to its
decision with a cheerful and airy insolence. To
quote the Worker report
Mr. J. C. Watson’s appeal to give “consideration” to
certain men who stood to the Federal Labor party in its
hour of need was listened to attentively, its very impres-
siveness and, above all, its clearly-cut sincerity, demand-
ing for it weighty deliberation. That was fully given.
The great admiration which the men of the conference had
for John Christian Watson rang out in fervent phrase,
frequent and free, but
But they decided to put him in the position of
breaking his word. They admired him fervently,
but they would not observe the arrangements
which he had made. They loved him, but they
pilloried him as a disgraced and censured leader.
To quote the Worker again :
Mr. Watson was not present on the following day when
the vote was taken, but he came in shortly afterwards.
The decision was formally communicated to him. He
pointed out the seriousness of the position to him as a
leader that the decision involved and how his personal
honor had been at stake when he had promised certain
men who had stuck to the party all the consideration he
could get for them. He felt that he was at perfect liberty
to do so, inasmuch as he had had the executive’s sanction.
The conference had decided otherwise. Then Mr. Watson
spoke in words of tense earnestness. In a few phrases
he made it clear that the resolution was in the nature of
a knock to him, but whatever might happen in the future
he would never be found voting against the party. Such
a statement bore only one interpretation, and the hope
was generally expressed that Mr. Watson would not do
anything that savored of an extreme course as the result
of an honestly-arrived-at verdict by the conference. To
show that they still trusted Mr. Watson, the members
carried unanimously a resolution approving of his able
leadership.
They “approved of his able leadership,” but they
left him in a position in which it is difficult to


see how he can remain leader of the party and
retain his personal honor. The loss of J. C.
Watson is the logical outcome of the decision of
the Victorian Labor League Conference. The
Bulletin trusts thatsuch a calamity will beaverted
—it would be acalamity, for Watson stands highest
among the public men of Australia for capacity
and earnestness —but it is difficult to see how he
can find an honorable way out ot the present posi-
tion, and this paper believes Watson will not be
satisfied with any but an honorable way. It is
not surprising to learn, by way of P.S., from the
Worker report, that this Conference of faith-
breakers was much given to singing. The trail of
the “Awake, arise ! shake off your chains !” was
obviously there. The Conference, it is stated,
sang everyday, and one of its favorite songs ran:
Then, after trying to make “warning” rhyme
with “dawning,” it proceeded with its deeds of
perfidy.

The Understanding That Wasn’t.
The Tory press lie that there was an “under-
standing” among the makers of the Federal Con-
stitution that the Commonwealth Parliament
should not impose direct taxation, except in case
of such an emergency as war, is so mischievous
that it cannot be refuted too often. Herewith—
for the use of those who may meet the lie
and wish to refute it — a few references
on the point from the Convention debates. At the
Adelaide session of the Federal Convention on
April 17, 1897, clause 50, sub-section III. of the
Draft Constitution was proposed. It provided
,(customs and excise taxation had already been
(dealt with) for—
Raising money by any other mode or system of taxa-
tion ; but so that all such taxation shall be uniform
throughout the Commonwealth.
'The clause was passed without division. No one
hinted that there was any understanding that
tthese powers of taxation should lie in abeyance.
At Sydney (Sept. 22, 1897). an amendment sug-
gested by the N.S.W. Legislative Council that
the sub-section should be omitted was con-
sidered. Whereupon Mr. Barton, leader of the
-Conference, said: —
This is another matter in which the Legislative Council
•of New South Wales was under the impression that too
much revenue-raising power would be given to the Com-
monwealth, and that the result would be over-taxation.
1 urged without avail that if the Commonwealth had to
•raise a certain amount of money, if it had not the right
•to raise it by other sources of income than Customs, it
would still raise the same amount, and the aggregate of
taxation would be the same, but that argument did not
prevail.
On this statement there was no debate, no
suggestion of an agreement not to use the power
to impose direct taxes except in casesof emergency.
But the amendment of the N.S.W. Legislative
Council was thrown down the stairs with such
emphasis that it only bumped once on the way.
Later (Sydney 7/3/’98), Mr. Barton again spoke in
the Federal Convention on this point:—
My desire is that the Federal Parliament should be
unfettered in the exercise of its taxing power if it has to
use any direct taxation at all. Whatever my own
opinions may be as to the way in which that power
should be exercised, it is necessary that the authority to
which it is confided should have the power in full force.
And from the same debates scores of other
remarks can be gathered—some of them referring
directly to a land tax —which remarks show that
it was the deliberate and unanimous intention of
the members of the Federal Convention to give
the Commonwealth Parliament unfettered powers
of direct taxation. The lie that there was an
“understanding” to the contrary is hopeless. It
hasn’t even a toe to stand on.

Our Lamentable Defences.
Colonel Antill, who has the look of a good
soldier and the record of one, has retired from
the Commonwealth service—he was one of a small
band of permanent instructional officers—and, at
a farewell dinner, gave his comrades the reasons.
They were, in short, that the Commonwealth
treats its military servants with disgusting mean-
ness, and that the Defence system, under the
present regime, is drifting into chaos, so that
there is no hope either of making a good living or
•doing creditable work by staying in the service.
•Unfortunately, what Antill says is quite true—an
•under-statement of the truth, if anything—and the
persons who are responsible for military adminis-
tration are making a characteristic effort to dodge
the questions brought up by this public statement
•of unpalatable truths, by raising a point of dis-
cipline. There is to be a great pow-wow as to
whether Antill had the right to speak so boldly
on retirement, and as to whether his brother
officers should not have gagged him with the table-
cloth, or drowned him in the port, or left the room,
rather than listen to his “treason” against the
military administration. But Australians gene-
rally will refuse, if they are wise, to be
drawn aside from the discussion of the main
issue by any such triviality. It is not of real
importance whether Antill technically shook off
all the bonds of discipline when he spoke, or
whether officers, in uniform at a friendly dinner,
are actually “on duty” and under discipline.
What is of real importance is that the statements
of Colonel Antill should be fully discussed, for
they touch closely the vital problem of national
defence. In the first instance, the charge of mean-
ness in the treatment of professional soldiers must
be held sustained. The Bulletin favors a militia
system of defence which will abolish the
absurdity of paying a man to do what is his
elementary duty as a citizen—train for defence ;
but it recognises that there will always be need of a
small instructional cadre. And the men who are
entrusted with the work of instruction need to be
of the best type, and need to have the feeling
that they can safely devote their lives to the
Commonwealth. To ensure that, the instructional
officer should be well paid, and should have
pension rights on retirement. At present he is
ill-paid, has no pension rights, and is arbitrarily
retired, not when he is unfit for work, but when
he has reached a certain age, no matter how
efficient he may still be. Arl instructional officer
in the Australian service, a keen, capable man,
has a Son whom he designs for the military
life, and who will have the advantage of his
father’s store of knowledge. But the son is marked
for the British, not the Australian army, for,
incredible as it may seem, in spite of all its
traditions of extravagance and snobbery, the
British army offers a better “career” to the
professional soldier. The best the permanent
soldier in Australia can hope for is a living wage
until he is too old for any other occupation ; then
the sack without pension. Antill moves out
whilst he is still capable of getting into another
groove. He sees, as an awful example of staying
in, a well-known N.S.W. colonel, who, after
giving his life-time to the service, was summarily
retired without pension, and so far has
been refused even the small mercy of a
minor civil post under the Government to
keep him from poverty. If the Commonwealth is
to keep able men for the important work of
military instruction, it must pay them better.
Further, it must give them better opportunity of
doing good "work. Many a man is willing to do
responsible, trusted work at £5OO a year who is
not willing to lick stamps under an exacting boss
for £6OO. The present military policy of the
Commonwealth (initiated during that disastrous
time when G. H. Reid was Minister) is to
centralise all authority in a Board, which is
mostly made up of incapables, and a Minister
who in his present day is pathetically incapable.
In the service are some excellent men (Finn, for
example, who has won every step by merit
and force of character), but they have no real
Eower; they are hobbled and heel-roped and
altered. Their energies are wasted in conform-
ing to a harassing routine and chafing against an
intolerable red-tapeism. It is safe to say that,
even with the most generous pay, men of character
and ability would not be comfortable in the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth forces as at present
governed. Since the pay is not generous, is not
even adequate, hardly an officer is contented,
though many ape content for different reasons.
Colonel Antill has done a public service in
bringing these facts to the fore. The Bulletin
hopes that their discussion will not be stifled by
any petty row about the etiquette of the position.

The Sydney Tramway Muddle.
The conclusion to which the Sydney tramway
management was driven by its Easter holiday ex-
periences, that it will soon be necessaiy to dupli-
cate the tram lines along Pitt and Castlereagh
streets, is a deafening commentary on —among
other things—the foolishness of Owk’Sullivan’s
immense, unfinished railway terminus at far-off
Redfern. One great reason why the railway
should be brought into the city, with a central
station at Wynyard-square and arrangements for
a future continuation across the harbor to North
Sydney, is that a great deal of traffic would thereby
be taken off the streets. Then branch lines could
be constructed to the eastern and other suburbs,
which would take a lot more traffic off the streets
just where it can be best spared. A branch line
which dropped its passengers at Wynyard-square,
or at side stations on the way thither, would be a
great relief, but nothing can be done by means of
a branch line connecting the eastern suburbs with
a remote, bulky monstrosity at Redfern. The
business people who rush in every morning, and
rush out every evening, don’t want to go to Red-
fern any more than they want to go to Newtown
or Eveleigh, and if they did go to Redfern they
would simply have to load into another set of
trams to get back into the city, and there would
be the same old congestion. In the absence of a
city railway station, and a network of city and
suburban branch lines, there is nothing for it save
to build more and more and still more tram-lines.
But there is practically no place for any more
tram-lines to go at the city end except Circular
Quay, which .is already a tangled waste and
wilderness of lines, and about the best place for
suicide in the Southern Hemisphere. It is more
dangerousthanaSouthAmericanrevolution. There
are practically only the two old outlets at the
other end —the congested bottle-neck in Oxford-
street, and the still more congested bottle-neck at
Redfern. There are no streets to lay more lines
in except Pitt and Castlereagh streets. These are
both narrow. If they have to carry double lines
then heavy dray traffic must leave them ; and as
George-street and Elizabeth-street are already full
of tram-lines, the only streets available for heavy
traffic are College-street and York-street. But
College-street empties into the congested bottle-
neck at Oxford-street, where the tram-cars are
already as thick as flies on a hot day at Bourke,
and York-street empties into the bottle-neck
at Redfern, where tram-cars are as thick
as good resolutions in Sheol, so that the
alleged remedy is no remedy after all.
Some desperate attempts are being made to
ease the pressure by constructing little side tracks
to connect one main tram-line with another, but
that only means shifting part of the traffic from
one over-loaded street to another over-burdened
street, and adding new complications, new cross-
ings, and new possibilities of eternal smash. The
utter lunacy of the Owe’Sullivan railway idea,
which left the increasing traffic to be carried by
the narrow city streets just as of old, is glaringly
revealed before even the Owe’ person’s garish
station is more than half built, and the trouble is
already so bad that it would probably be worth
while to pull down that pretentious structure, and
write it off (out of revenue) as a bad debt, and
carry the railway straight into the city after all.
