The Yic. Farmer and the Vic. Railways.
Pbesumably to show that he is really doing a lot
of work for his money, Victoria’s imported Railway
Commissioner Tait has just issued a new Rate
Book. And an amazing thing it is. Simplicity
ought to be the guiding principle in goods classi-
fication. A plain and business-like system, with
as few divisions as possible, would enable the
Department to trade with the minimum of trouble
to itself and the maximum of convenience to the
public. But Mr. Tait has taken sweet Sim-
plicity by the neck, and strangled her fatally
dead. The classification is a phantasmagoria.
There are classes M, AP, AP special, A,
B, C, 1,2, and 3—a book full of
them. Asphalt in bags is in one class, asphalt
in blocks in another. Sheet zinc is in one class ;
if there are holes in the sheet zinc it is in another ;
and a great gulf yawns between clothes pegs and
clothes lines. Also, what is A when there are six
tons of it, is KZ when there are two tons of it,
and it is OY or PQ when something else happens.
But, through it all, the old swindle sticks out—
the vast, unrecognised subsidy to the Great Natu-
ral Industries, the farmer and the squatter (whose
representatives say that Protection isn’t needed
and that Socialism is scandalous) being fed by the
Socialistic railways at the e> pense of the manu-
facturer and the city taxpayer. Take a case, on a
plain, through line, where the rates to Melbourne
are unusually low, evidently with the object of
preventing any of the trade going to the out-
ports :
Freight Between Melbourne and Conlaii (Westfrn
District), 213 Miles.
Wheat, oats, barley, cha' T , straw, potatoes,
onions, per ten . . .. .. .. £0 14 4
Buckets, clothing, harness, tea, most groceries
furniture, per ton 2 0 0
Or another case, in a district where Melbourne
hasn’t to be protected from the poor struggling
little outport
Freight Between Melbourne and Natimuk (Wijimera),
241 Miles.
Wheat, oats, barley, chaff, straw, potatoes,
onions, per ton .. £0 14 4
Buckets, clothing’, harness, tea, most groceries,
furniture, per ton o u
So the unfortunate Natimuk hayseed has a mere
trifling “pull” of £4 lid. Bd. per ton over the
carrion storekeeper who supplies him with his
boots, harness, clothing, furniture, tinware, etc.
The Argus howls itself out of its boots at the
idea of giving preference to the brand of unionist
who carries a hod. Now let it do a different
shriek, anent the appalling injustice of giving a
bloated preference of 91s. Bd. per ton to the brand
of unionist that sits on the harvester seat. And
still nothing has been said anent the special
manure and wool rates charged on the Victorian
railways. No mention has been made of a score
of other reduced rates, simply because it would
take acres of paper to show all the different pre-
ferences that the country producer receives. It
is not argued here that the farmer has no right to
obtain any preference. But it is argued that he
has no right to a preference so huge that other
customers of the Railway Department are forced
to literally bleed money in order to square Tait s
ledger. It is argued, also, that about half the
clerks at Spencer-street could be given productive
work to do if the present ridiculously unequal
rates and complicated classification were abolished,
At present, the Department’s customers are lost
in a wilderness of tariffs ; and no man knows
what he will have to pay until the Department
sends in its insane bill.
A Fire Insurance Matter.
Tiie mention of State fire insurance, a subject
which has cropped up pretty often in recent years,
has always aroused a superior smile on the
countenance of the private enterprise insurance
“expert.” On these occasions he explains, in
simple words which he reckons are suited to the
limited comprehension of his hearers —people who
suggest such a thing as State insurance being
necessarily of limited comprehension that it
wouldn’t do. Fire insurance, the expert
laboriously expounds, depends for its success on
“ a wide diffusion of risks a far wider one than
can ever be secured in any one State; so State
insurance, being confined to one State, would
necessarily fail. The financial editors of the Tory
papers back up this explanation, and express their
sorrow for the limited intelligence of persons who
can’t see the point, and their leading columns
speak rather in grief than in anger about
the ignorance and incapacity of a certain class
of citizen. Meanwhile San Francisco is still
smouldering, and the New Zealand Insurance
Co., which went in for “a wide diffusion of
risks,” is wondering whether its loss will be
£250,000 or £350,000. The shock isn’t so bad as it
might be, because this co. recently took over the
N.Z, Accident Insurance Co.-—a concern which
didn’t go in for a wide diffusion of risks, and
didn’t lose its money paying for unforeseen
accidents to Bulgarian insurgents and the like—
and the combined funds of the two companies run
to very large dimensions ; still the bill is a heavy
blow. Most Australian companies at one time or
another took a hand at doing a great foreign busi-
ness, and those that did fall naturally into three
classes—the ones that were killed by the foreign
business, such as the once great Pacific Co. ; the
ones that got out of their large foreign business in
time and got back on the road to prosperity, such
as the Colonial Mutual and the North Queens-
land ; and one or two that are still in the foreign
business, and are feeling bad just at present over
its possibilities. With English companies the
experience is just the same as with Australian
ones. There is probably not one among the
British fire insurance companies that have
died in the last 30 or 40 years through
being burnt out (some, of course, have died
through never having had any business, but those
were swindles from the first) which didn’t perish
through its foreign business. The burning of St.
John’s, of Chicago (damage £39,000,000), of part
of Boston (damage £15,000,000), and the latest
San Francisco shock —these, and not the local
losses, represent the sort of thing which crumples
up the British fire insurance co. Just now three
English offices are looking gloomily at aggregate
losses of about £3,500,000, a sort of thing that they
couldn’t have met with in England since 1666, and
many others are struck as heavily in proportion.
It is at these times that the office which keeps all
its business close under its eye in one country—
especially if it is a country which hasn t earth-
quakes, revolutions, volcanoes, or shoddy wooden
cities—feels best. It may not be so bloated in its
figures as the one which is liable at any time to
be combusted in five continents, but its profits are
generally larger in the end. The superior insur-
ance expert rises to state now and then, as a reason
against State insurance, that the existence of an
insurance institution is impossible without foreign
business, b'ut the most steadily progressive offices
in Australia give that expert the lie in every bal-
ance-sheet.

The Imitation Icebeig.
A correspondent finds out that Irvine is only
an imitation Iceberg after all:
As I listened to Irvine my mind raced back to Robes-
pierre. The same cold, repellent, envious face ; the
same asperity, slowness of utterance, harshness of voice,
want of animation. I saw him, the Incorruptible One, as
he came up from Arras, where he had resigned his position
of Judge-Advocate, because his precious conscience would
not allow him to pronounce a death penalty, as enjoined
by the existing law. I saw him come to Paris, to be shel-
tered by Pktion, Roland and Brissot ; and to subsequently
return the favor by proscribing the first, driving the
second to suicide, and guillotining the last. I saw him
refuse —like Irvine recently—the proffered office of Public
Accuser ; because he aimed solely, like Irvine, at gaining
the reputation of being an incorruptible patriot. And
how gloriously did he succeed ! His former protectors
quivered in alarm whenever the great man’s corpse-like
eye rested on them. Everybody who disagreed with him
was a bad citizen. His own popularity he founded on the
blind passions and limited views of faction. His was the
faction of the sansculottes ; Irvine’s of the Federated
Fats. Marat, that terrible but far-seeing fanatic, had
but to see him once to sum him up. He found
Robespierre surrounded by busts and pictures of him-
self ; all portraying him as a calm, strong man, who alone
could save France. Marat blinked, then dragged himself
hastily-away. “ A nobody, sans ideas, sans everything
except the capacity for unlimited worship of self. He
certainly is not the man for whom I seek.”
Marvellous is the re-incarnation of character. After the
affair of the 10th of August, when the putty-willed Louis
XVI. surrendered theTuilleries practically without a blow,
the Incorruptible One howled harshly for a special tribunal
with sufficient latitude to reach everyone, and threat-
ened to bring all Paris roaring round the Legislative
Assembly, if the decree were not immediatey granted.
That was in 1792. In 1904 Irvine howled for a Strike
Suppression Act, to indiscriminately gaol and punish all
those who found no favor in the eyes of the latter-day
Incorruptible One. And, as Robespierre terrified the
Parliament of France into acquiescence, so Irvine bullied
the Victorian Parliament into placing such a perfidious
weapon in his hands. Robespierre declared that h.s
tribunal should be without appeal ; so also did the cold,
callous Irvine. Robespierre set up the guillotine, and
chopped off heads. Irvine set up h'.s Act for die-
franchising the civil servants, and chopped off votes’.
“No trial!" Robespierre screeched, horrified by the
possibility of the result being: favorable to a person
whom he saw fit to accuse. “ No trial, cried Irvine t
the railway men. And when the Labor party voiced the
wrongs of the railway men, how Irvine yearned for a
power to silence their tongues ! He indeed had one such
weapon. Public opinion, that shoddy creation of a
shoddy Press, was his to use as he might please. He
did please to use it; and clods of public reprobation smote
every mail who cried out that Justice had been muideiei .
How we forget, we Australians ! Here conies
the Incorruptible One again, seeking a place in out
National Parliament. Here he comes preaching Robes-
pierre's old gospel, that the mob can do no wrong.
Robespierre meant the Lean Mob; Irvine means
the Fat Mob. Covetous of flattery, lie has a swell fol-
lowing of women. So had Robespierre. Robespierre s
women made a god of him. His superannuated mai-
chionesses crawled at his feet. The National Women s
League crawls after Irvine to-day, imploring him to be a
calm, strong man some more, and stamp out this plague
of progress. So Irvine walks before all; holding con-
verse with none of his partners in bygone outrages ,
wholly wrapt up in the contemplation of his miraculous
self. His Society-cwm-political females fawn on him as
he goes. “ Dear Mr. Irvine, do stand. We will help you
into Parliament. Money, the press-every instrument is
yours.” The idol of the old women of both sexes,
he meditates in serene aloofness, pondering which
Federal electorate he will honor with his candidature.
Thiers put it well, when he said that a “ good ” reputa-
tion is requisite for fooling the orowd. But will any
crowd of electors, in Victoria or elsewhere, allow them-
selves to be fooled by Irvine’s cold, passionless, tomb-
stone-like reputation ? One wonders. Are we Austra-
lians the champion forg'etters of all time ?
Victoria’s Railway Accidents.
Looking back at that wonderful record of Vic-
torian railway accidents in the first quarter ot
this year (31 accidents were recorded by the press,
and it may have missed some ol the small ones),
27 seem clearly traceable to the awful economy ot
the man with the cigar in his mouth who came to
Victoria to “make the railways pay. there
were 15 derailments in the three months, to begin
with. All but the Gembrook affair were officially
investigated in secret, and a secret inquiry was
getting ready in the Gembrook case when the
press forced a public investigation. In most
cases, so far as is known, the road was proved to
be decayed at the scene of the accident, or the
points out of gear, and it seems that generally
some small railway servant was offered up as a
burnt sacrifice. But Tait, who has cut down the
maintenance staff so heavily that a general state ot
disrepair is only to be expected, was never the
sacrifice. In eleven other cases of accident the
engine broke down, and in one case a truck broke
up. These matters were also inquired into
officially and in secret, and presumably someone
was found responsible for not repairing the
vehicles, but the secret inquiry never seems to
have sacked Thomas Tait for cutting down the
cleaning and repairing staff to a degree that made
it almost certain that tja© rolling stock must
degenerate into a herd of rattletraps clattering
down a decayed permanent way to probable
destruction. Of course Tait was brought in “to
make the railways pay.” He hasn’t ventured
to make them pay by adequately increasing the
rates on the farm produce of the pampered
agriculturists of Kyabram and other places
—the men who got their produce carried
for next to nothing, and swindle the State
out ol its irrigation rates as well. If he did
so, rents (and land values) would have to go
down, for rents are already as high as the farmer
can afford to pay, even when he gets his produce
carried at a great loss, which loss used to make the
annual railway deficit. And if rents went down
owing to Tait, then Tait would become unpopu-
lar with the House of Rich Landlords and others.
Another way to make the railways pay would be
to reduce the debt against them—to pay off out
of revenue the loan money which represents mere
swindles done by the great landlords of Victoria
when they sold their property to the State tor
railway purposes at three to six times its value.
But if Tait proposed that, and mentioned these
swindles, he would again become unpopular.
The easiest way is to economise by euttmg
down repairs; but as the money saved
by lack of repairs comes off the capital
value of the railways—sometimes £1 saved
in repairs knocks £2 or £3 off the capital
value of the property —this following of the line
of least resistance is calculated to end in loss
rather than profit. The great bill for belated
repairs is almost due now, and when it arrives
jjf, Tait will certainly be unpopular. Mean-
while, he is pretty unpopular to go on with. „ But
he was imported “ to make the railways pay. In
the end, he will probably make the country pay
through the nose. Meanwhile, Tait talks through
the nose.

G. H. Reid, at Forbes (N.S.W.) :
If there is a monopoly which is injurious to the com-
munity, I am for strenuously putting it down ; but the
Socialists want them to continue as State monopolies, just
a substitution of one form of the evil for another ; and
whereas a private monopoly may be wiped out, a btate
monopoly is indestructible.
How is a State monopoly indestructible ? If a
State monopoly became “injurious to the com-
munity,” what is to prevent the community ( i.e .,
the people who own and direct the monopoly and
injure themselves by it) wiping it out ? And when
everybody has a share in a monopoly, how is it a
monopoly? And how does Reid propose to put
down the private monopoly that is injurious ex-
cept by the State taking it over ? Will he show
his draft of a Bill that will put down a monopoly
otherwise ?

Poor King Peter of Servia receives a lot of at-
tention in the cablegrams these times. He got to
the throne through the brutal murder of King
Alexander —the last of the Children of the Pig-
Dealer—and his wife. Now some of the Great
Powers won’t speak to Peter until he dismisses
the officers who butchered his predecessor, and
Peter dare not do this, because if he mentioned
such a thing those individuals would butcher lnm
also. Meanwhile they threaten to butcher him,
anyway, on the suspicion that he has a desire to
get rid of them, and propose to crown
Peter’s eldest son, aged about 19, in his stead.
The son may have been a respectable young
man before he became heir to a throne, but now
he is a drinky weed whose pursuit of Flossies at
night in his father’s capital has become a scandal ;
and it is commonly understood that he doesn’t
care much what becomes of poor old Pa so long as
he himself reaches the throne promptly and gets
the royal revenue to go on the tear with. Ihe
group of officers who murdered Alexander and
his queen have started a paper, and presumably
have put the new king on the free list so that
he may be kept posted up in their views.
King Peter has got together a small force of his
very own to guard his palace o nights in case
the officers who slew his predecessor should lead
his own army against him and leave another
bloodstain on the palace floor, but the reliability
of the new force is doubtful. Meanwhile the
ghosts of Alexander and Drag a are said to
haunt the royal building; and it must be a dreary
time for Peter Karageorgrvitch, shut up with
the spectres of his predecessors, not daring to go
out at night, and painfully aware that his
heir is whooping things up in the city out-
Hark, the battle-cry is ringing-.
Hope within our bosoms springing,
Bids us journey forward singing—
Death to tyrant’s might.
Though we wield no spear or sabre,
We the sturdy sons of Labor,
Helping every man his neighbor,
Shrink not from the fight.
Tyrant hearts take warning,
Nobler deeds are dawning,
Heroic deeds, sublimer creeds,
Shall herald Freedom’s morning.

“Itan”: Yes, the cedar forests of the N.S.W.
coast are practically exhausted. There are a few
trees left in the Dorrigo, but the end is in sight,
for that belt is soon to get into the hands of the
settler. What applies to New South Wales also
stands good for Queensland. The only country now
carrying cedar in Queensland is the rich stretch
of upland lying inland between Ingham and Cairns,
but the timber-getter has long been scouring even
those last reserves, and he has reduced the available
supply very considerably. The Government has
made an attempt at re-afforestation on a small
scale, but its little efforts look pretty hopeless.
If the cedar growths of many centuries along the
coast of Australia can be depleted in a few years,
what chance would a spot here and there have of
coping with the demand ? The cedar takes a
good many years before it is of any commercial
value, and it requires to be raised in a scrub to
force trunk growth or it will branch nut and give
nothing of a barrel. The Atherton country in
N.Q., however, is so rich that settlement is sure
to commence in dead earnest before long, and then
good-bye to the remaining cedars. Fortunately,
however, Queensland has a large number of soft-
woods in its list of 700 varieties of timber, which,
if planted out, would soon become of great com-
mercial value. A good deal might be done in the
western districts of Queensland and N.S.W. by
planting belts of timber, utilising prison labor for
the work, as is done in Maoriland. It would be
more elevating work, too, for prisoners than
making brushes and boots while huddled up in a
gaol.

It must appear that some branch of the Stinking
Fish family has now got down very close to plain
forgery in its anxiety to slander Australia and its
legislation. In this case the Western Morning
News (published at Plymouth, England) seems
either to be itself the forger or to have lent the
forger a hand. The paper lately issued a solemn
warning to the people of England to beware of
the Bannerman Government’s tendencies towards
secular education, and pointed out many alleged
horrors to which the secular system had led in
Australia. Some sample horrors come by cable-
gram :
The experiment has been tried in Australia. As a
result, the State schools are not merely un-Christian, but
anti-Christian. Another result is the empty cradle.
People here have no conception of the conditions pre-
vailing among Australian young people. Modesti’and
refinement have vanished. Australian girls and young
ladies are very different to those of the motherland.
The streets are filled with larrikins, who have no morals,
and who are a danger to the community.
A mighty revolution is setting in in the colonies.
Parents are demanding-a referendum in favor of definite
religious instruction in schools. Wherever this referendum
has been granted a threefold majority in favor of such
religious instruction has been obtained.
The Plymouth rag declared that this article
(including the incredible lie contained in the
last sentence of it) was the work of an
Australian Agent-General (name and ad-
dress not given). Thereupon all the Agents-
General repudiated it with emphasis, but appar-
ently they didn’t demand to know—as they
should have done—which of them had had his
name used for nefarious purposes, either by
the Western Morning News or by some corres-
pondent of that ill-conducted organ. The case
ought to be one in which some Agent-General, or
the Agents-General collectively, could get heavy
damages and thus make an example of the
malicious fool paper which gave one of them as
authority for its lamentable collection of English
lies. Mere repudiation of the authorship of the
story is a very weak expedient in such a case.
Some little time ago this paper expressed its
pleased astonishment at the fact that, so far, no
new lie or myth of any consequence had been
invented by the Tory party for use during the
approaching Federal elections. The Victorian
Female National Ass., however, has resurrected
one of the old myths, and finds it still in good
order. At the annual conference of the Farmers’
League, held a few days ago in Melbourne, Mrs.
S. T, Staughton —who possibly wouldn’t recog-
nise a farm if she fell over one—made a speech,
and this is part of what sh% said :
The Socialists found the healthy moral tone permeat-
ing the great mass of the people of Australia was not
capable of absorbing the monstrous proposals made on
the platform, and in their publications, issued and sup-
ported by the Labor Socialistic party. The Women’s
League had not lost sight of the original attacks on reli-
gion and the married state.
It would almost seem as if Mrs. Staughton, who
is a squatteress and a “ right-thinking ” person,
had lost her temper, or, at any rate, mislaid her
memory. It was an anti-Socialist lecturer who
made the attack upon “the married state,”
by a declaration that wages were not neces-
sarily meant to be high enough to enable
a man to marry. If Mrs. Staughton knows
of any statement, in the way of an “attack
upon the married state,” made by any of the
Labor leaders in Australia, The. Bulletin will be
pleased to reprint it. If she doesn’t, then she is
a delusion and a snare, and also a bearer of false
witness (which the “ religion ” she is championing
expressly condemns). The Tory cause must be in
a shocking state when such mouldy, old, decrepit
slanders have to be dug up for fresh use.
A quaint yarn from Melbourne :
The “potting” of Cornelius Crowe on a charge of
attempted blackmail is a queer story of criminal prosecu-
tion. Crowe is the Melbourne ex-policeman who for
years has been crankily attacking the force he formerly
belonged to, publishing, at his own expense, charges of
rascality and corruption against police and detectives,
and incidentally endeavoring to bring the “ tote ”
scandal to a head. Now, the odd thing about this
prosecution of the man is the fact that it wasn’t set afoot
by G. H. Bennett, M.L.A., to whom the allegedly
blackmailing letter was addressed. Mr. Bennett
certainly took the letter to the detective office,
but he had no deliberate intention, he says, of laying a
charge against Crowe. The summons against Crowe was
obtained in the name of a gentleman named “ Walker,”
and the expenses of the police-court prosecution (barristers
Purves and Coldham being briefed for it) were appar-
ently chargeable to the same Walker.” Bennett,
appearing as witness at the instance of “ Walker,” stated
that he had not wanted to get Crowe into trouble for
putting what appeared to be his signature to a type-
written letter. Crowe denied that he ever signed the
letter in the first place, or posted it. He stated that after
getting a draft letter type-written at a well-known office,
where other people than the typist were bound to see it,
he left it with a friend—unsigned—not having made up
his mind to post it to Bennett. Anyhow, it ivas signed,
either by Crowe or by some one else, and it got to
Bennett through the post.
Seemingly the prosecution saw possibilities as to
Crowe’s alleged “sending” of the document, for there
was a second charge in the indictment the
charge of “uttering.” The jury found Crowe not
guilty of sending but guilty of uttering, on the J udge’s
direction that the prisoner had been actually an utterer
if he left the unlawful letter on a friend’s table without
giving instructions for it to be posted. This definition
of uttering remains to be further considered as a special
case before the Judge’s sentence of 12 months’ hard labor
is confirmed or cancelled.

After pretty well every annual Easter Show,
Sydney Kvening News prints anything up to a
column reporting something that it alleged it
overheard in front of the prize pig or elsewhere.
It never mentions the names of the parties to whom
id plays the eavesdropper ; as for Blushing to print
what it has got hold of by hanging round and
“overhearing” things—well, it’s the Evening
News. This year it admits that it was lout
enough to listen while an alleged Australian was
saying to a friend what an alleged American said
to him when he was in Chicago or somewhere
lately. The reputed Australian told the alleged
American that in Australia a man wasn’t allowed
to work more than so hard ; the law' wouldn’t
let him, and there was a court provided to
see that every man loafed as much as
his neighbor. Whereupon the alleged American
said he reckoned Australia was a dodgasted fool,
and gave it a quantity of good advice—all of
which the Evening News printed without once
hinting that the alleged Australian was a beauti-
ful liar who, on his own confession, went round
the world traducing his country and filling the
foreigner with rotten notions about it. Still, when
an alleged Australian paper apparently thinks so
highly of the Stinking Fish liar of this sort as to
wander round a show ground after him, and listen
when he talks to a friend, and then rushes off to
print his remarks, there isn’t much to choose be-
tween the original liar and the paper that circu-
lates his remarks.

Just how well T. Bent and T. Tait have
managed the Victorian railways was shown by
the matters which the Amalgamated Society of
Railway Employees set down for discussion at its
annual conference. There were 25 notices of
motion. Of these, eight expressed a desire for
more pay, demanding a minimum of 7s. a day for
adult men. Five motions asked for a decrease in
the excessive work set the employees by the
department, and the removal of the penalty
which abolishes holidays in order that this work
may be finished somehow or other. Three reso-
lutions embodied requests for the classification
which has been constantly refused to railway men,
though approved by Acting-Commissioner Fitz-
patrick four years ago. Other notices requested
the restoration of travelling privileges, such as
even greedy private companies allow all the world
over. Then there was one motion on each of the
following subjects : Full pay for men suffering
from injury caused in the execution of their duty ;
inquiry into the extent of boy labor; permission
to the poorly-paid employees to help themselves by
selling milk and butter ; the restoration of the full
Parliamentary franchise ; provision for the safety
of travelling permanent-way men by giving them
tricycles with mirrors, so as to know when one of
T. Tait’s bolting trucks is swooping down on
them. These complaints and protests charge
Tait and Bent directly with sweating, injustice,
and tyranny. When the Victorian people can
spare time from reading thrilling newspaper
stories of the trains which fall off the line, they
might turn to examining the evidenco which lies
behind the 23 charges contained in the Amalga-
mated Society’s balance-sheet. The facts of
sweating and over-work are easily enough learned ;
the results, when the trains run along the sleepers
instead of the rails, are even more easily seen—
and felt.
“S.R.”: The average Defence Force man gets very
little rifle practice to make him proficient with his
rifle. Sixty rounds of ball cartridge are allowed
him free for practice in the year. In addition, he
must put in his course of musketry every year.
This makes about another 60 rounds. A grand
total of 120 rounds or so is an absurdly small
ration of cartridge. Is it any wonder that the
rifle club man (who for sport spends his hour
or two each Saturday at target practice) can
beat the soldier out of sight at shooting ?
I followed up rifle shooting for about four years,
and contrived in that time to accumulate a hearty
contempt for the shooting capacity of our soldier.
I found that the Lee-Metford rifle barrel was barely
capable of standing one year of steady practice
shooting. After that it became erratic, and its
owner would have no chance against the man
with a sound gun. From my experience I can
say that little or no care is taken in the testing
and proving of military rifles supplied to Aus-
tralia. In the American arsenal at Springfield
every rifle issued must have five shots fired at the
targets by the sighting staff to prove sights
correct, and in addition several shots are fired
from each rifle with times the usual cordite
charge. This is to find out any flaws in the
barrels. But America has a national arms factory,
and one doesn’t expect the Private Enterprise of
Brummagem (England) to take that amount of
trouble.

It is more than a thousand pities that
Greek so rarely meets Greek when a composer
and a librettist agree to send forth yet another
comic opera upon a world blown upon by popular
airs and overgrown with the jocular chestnut tree.
The composer is often born—Mr. Alfred Hill
is a born composer—and the librettist is
mostly made. Sometimes he isn’t even that
much. In “The Moorish Maid,” produced for
the first time in Australia at Sydney Palace on
Saturday night, Mr. Youlin Birch is responsible
for the lyrics and guilty of the libretto—he is
guilty only so far as the development of the plot
is concerned, for he deserves congratulation, and
a banquet winding up with “For he’s a jolly clever
fellow ” upon his fashioning of the plot; but he has
used the screw-wrench and the lever to develop his
humor. Mostly he has gone out to the high ways,
and bye-ways, and street corners, and ’bus-tops, and
lassoed his jokes, and dragged them in shrieking
from their comfortable old carpet-slipper-like ruts
saying, “ It’s all right, my chestnut; wait till an
old hand like Edward Lauri cracks you, and nary-
one of the audience save those of the wry-faced
citrus family will see that the moths are in you.”
And he says truly. Audiences frequently welcome
old jokes' as they do old friends seen in a
strange environment.
* * * *
Outside the palace of a Moorish princess
named Hashma the opera starts amid a blaze of
cruel orange, brown, and crimson. The place is
the market square of Sis, in Morocco, where the
recent diplomatic ruptures come from. The prin-
cess is about to be married to a neighboring
prince named Omar, who has rescued her from
brigands. The bridegroom is impersonated by
his steward Gastro, and marries the lady, but
that’s a trifle in what the Americans call “kammic
appera.” He doesn’t suffer * the picturesque
penalty of Death by a Thousand Oats, or
any other such horror. His comic master
merely tells him to leave the city for a week,
his absence securing the bride a divorce for deser-
tion. As he is going into elegant bachelor exile
the brigands seize him, under the delusion that
he is Prince Omar, whom their chieftainess,
La Zara, desires to marry. The second
act occurs in the brigandess’s marble halls
in the hills, and the tail of the opera badly wants
curling. It seems to have been bitten off, leaving
a precipitous stump. But each man gets the
damsel he wants, and the damsel herself is satis-
fied, though the precise basis of her satisfac-
tion might be more clear. Mr. Hill’s music
has all the brightness, freshness, and freedom that
lived in his former productions, “ Hinemoa ” and
“ Tapu.” Miss Rosina Buckmann, a new soprano,
has found in the part Of Zara a niche into which she
fits most songfully. Experience will do much far
her. Miss May Beatty, as a French prima donna,
is in capital form ; Miss May Garstang, Edward
Lauri and Charles M'Naughton deserve this ink-
drop of praise ; and one of the finest bits is Miss
Roland Watts Phillips’ outburst as Zuleilca.
** * *
Esther Kahn, who looks as if she’d been
fed on coffee and chocolate creams, so dark is she,
gave a concert in aid of Croydon Children’s Home
at the Centenary Hall on the 24th. She was
assisted by a number of good artistes, Mrs. C.
C. Lance and Miss Sara Hendy of the Brescians
being particular favorites. Miss Kahn played
her own Etude de Concert with Miss Nellie Kay,
and surprised her hearers by her originality, and
dainty and forceful playing.

It seems as safe to revive “ The Orchid ”
as it is to pour a moderately thick dressing of
praise over it after it has been revived. Saturday
night at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, facing the stage
was such a house as one might have expected if
the flower had been new instead of having
bloomed week after week, week after week,
only twelve months ago, while J. C. Williamson
was straightening out the tangle over the
authorship of “ The Cingalee.” And, though the
spidery bloom is, after all, only a pretty shallow
pretext on which to hang a variety programme,
there are four-and-thirty elements of a good-
sized success in it—about four-and-twenty of said
elements being in the chorus and the ballet. Some
of the other 10 are the music, which ripples
well enough ; “ Little Mary,” still sung with just
tbe right expression by Miss Evelyn Scott, and
better understood now since poor Robert Brough
jxpounded it at length (it was the last thing he
played in); Miss Florence Young, who (consider-
ing the matter from the outside, with the mind of
the mere man who knows little of these things,
certainly) will probably be glad when “The
Orchid ” has wilted, and she can get into
something looser ; and Miss Clifton, who, largely
because the whole house never misses a word of
her ditties, is obliged to hold at least four verses
up her sleeve, and pour them out drop by drop
after the main performance. Connie Milne is an
element, too, but the house misses all her wards,
and' pets her only for her sprightliness. John
Doran is the cheerful ass who gets married by
mistake to the right girl, and is an improvement
on the previous ass; but Lauri and the rest are
the same rest as previously.
“The Fatal Wedding” has proved a
fascinating title for playgoers, and the Sydney
Criterion some nights can hardly hold the audi-
ences. Under the circumstances naturally no
change is suggested.
** * *
At Sydney Royal on Saturday “ The
Prodigal Son,” by Hall Caine, will be staged
under Bland Holt’s auspices. And Bland Holt s
auspices are good things for a playwright to have
about the premises. The Caine drama is one of
much sensationalism, and of highly impressive
gloom. It is said to beat “Bast Lynne for
really interesting murk and weep. Bland Holt
will, without a doubt, do full justice to the spec-
tacles—of which there are many—and the Fatted
Calf and other properties will be on a scale of un-
paralleled magnificence, and the swine, which the
Son left to take care of itself, will doubtless be
seen inquiring about its missing husk in the dis-
tance.
** * *
Wirth’s Circus leaves Sydney this week,
driving its herd of elephants before it. Its de-
parture will create that blank which the country
paper always mentions when an alderman dies,
and will shed a gloom over a community which
is just recovering from its Easter, bun
indigestion, and has enough gloom in its
stomach without extraneous darkness falling on
it in this fashion. A bereaved community will
start to bear up as best it can next Sunday morn-
ing. Until then its camel will not be finally torn
from it, but unless that hairy baggy animal can
be persuaded to alter its arrangements the painful
separation can’t possibly be postponed any further.
** * *
Brescians and West’s Pictures are located
in Sydney Town Hall since Saturday night, and are
doing business in tune with their large environ-
ment. The fact that their ten nights of Town
Halldom will cost them £2OO rent hall-marks
them as popular successes.
#* * *
Miss Elizabeth Cash gave a concert at the
Y.M.C.A. Hall on Friday night, when the only
coin was in the cognomen of the young soprano.
She is a pupil of Miss Edward Deane. Miss
Cash has a good concert appearance, and though
her voice was a trifle hard, she sang intelligently
and in tune, which is not a common occurrence
with young singers. Miss Deane also sang, and
Miss Carr Boyd, a nervous but very acceptable
pianiste, played Liszt’s Polonaise and a nocturne
of Chopin. The concert amounted to a distinct
success.
#* # *
Homer B. Mason and Marguerite Z. Keller
are the only events of any consequence that have
happened at Sydney Tivoli since last week ; and
they have happened in a quick-time sketch in the
American style amidst great and mostly-deserved
applause. The lady's name is only plain Mar-
guerite on the bills; but as this paper, on
a preliminary inspection, thinks nearly as
well of the lady as of the musicianly burglar
who plays with her, it doesn’t see why Homer B.
should have the 8., while his partner has no
buffer, so to speak, between her Marguerite and
her Keller. Larola, who is nearly a whole music-
hall play-bill in himself, and Miss Raymond who
tootles, and numerous little ladies who dance
better than they sing, drop in between the items
that are printed in the big letters.
** * *
The charms of a sfcoushing match at the
Exhibition Building seemed to distract more
people from “Tambour Major,” last Saturday,
than from any of the other Melbourne shows.
From which it appears that the appetite for
comic opera of the Offenbach order is largely
associated with a thirst for blood, and the thirst is
mightier than the appetite. “ Tambour Major ”
quits Melbourne for touring purposes after Friday
night, but the favorable impression made by its
principal girls will not disappear so soon. There’s
a good deal of youth and winsomeness about the
girls, also they are genuine Australian products,
whose native worth deserves as much
encouragement as though it were imported.
In last year’s revival of this opera at the local
Royal, the Stella and Claudine were strangers
from abroad, and it may be remarked that Miss
Addie Campbell and Miss Birch, both of this
country, are distinctly brighter than their prede-
cessors in the same parts. As for Miss Thornton,
she is the same blithe and legsome Griolet as
before, with a somewhat improved voice. She
doesn’t look a very probable member of Napoleon’s
musical forces, but even a Maud Thornton may
have happened in a French army which was con-
ducted into Milan by twelve she- male lieutenants.

the Athenaeum Hall will be full of business during
the,brief season.

“ The Radium Dance ” has been added to
the Opera House programme, but the patrons of
Rickards are not deeply moved by that alleged
novelty. An audacious ’Murkan man with a
hideous nasal accent imparts an undne importance
to the Radium Dance by desiring the audience to
retain their presence of mind whilst the theatre
is plunged into blackness. Subsequently five
vague figures in white baggy pyjamas frivol
amidst the gloom, and when they have done
nothing that can be called graceful dancing they
go away, and the darkness disappears. Alto-
gether it is the sort of “turn” which might be
expected of the Voice that introduces it. In other
respects, the Rickards show is not disappointing.
The Meisters retired this week, bnt the banjoists,
the Carmos, and various other well-defined per-
formers are there, including Martinetti and
Grossi, whose musical instruments are mostly in-
corporated in a bicycle.
## * *
The Knight-Jeffries partnership has been
so well supported at Adelaide Royal that a special
performance of “Everyman ”is arranged. “The
Sign of the Gross” has been staged as a kind of
preliminary to the Morality play. Another bill
was made up of “ David Garrick ” and “ Comedy
and Tragedy,” giving about the finest possible
chance that actor and actress respectively could
have. The rest of the season is to consist of
various revivals. Frank Bullen (with the useful
assistance of limelight illustrations) dealt with the
Mighty Ocean from all points of view till his
departure. The Sanford Co., at the Tivoli, has
proved an unexpected success, and melodrama
reigns indefinitely.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The movement to raise a substantial fund for little
Leila Doubleday has drawn a fine testimonial from
Marshall Hall, Melbourne’s musical oracle, who rarely
speaks so warmly of a child performer. “She has a
musical temperament and talent astonishing in one so
young, and which deserves the most careful cultivation.
No more gifted child has been heard here since Percy
Grainger. I trust the efforts in her behalf will be
especially successful,” says Marshall Hall. Mrs. Double-
day, unfortunately, is just now an invalid, and can
take no active part in the arrangements for her
daughter’s concert on the 16th, but a big suc-
cess is promised by the working committee. After
the damsel has made her Melbourne appearance in
the double capacity of violiniste and pianiste (her share
of the programme will be five long numbers) she is to give
a concert in Sydney and another in Adelaide before leav-
ing for Germany. The note of interrogation in Leila
Doubleday’s case should make her an interesting subject
for musical criticism and public curiosity in each city.
Ought she to prefer the violin to the piano, or vice versa 1
Which ever way the public verdict goes, the cognoscenti
will probably find reasons for thinking differently. They
mostly think differently.
“0.K.”: Re the story told.in M.A.P. as to the founda-
tion of Frank Gardner’s fortune. Many years ago-—prior
to Carbine’s victory in the Melb. Cup—it was commonly
reported that Gardner had made a big rise by investing
in Broken Hill Proprietary shares. The mining story is
probably true. As for the racing yarn, that Gardner
landed a “ huge stake ” by backing Vengeance, The
Admiral and Carbine for the treble event, its improba-
bility lies in the fact that no such wager was recorded in
the sporting press of 1890. Which bookmaker or ’makers
did the huge stake come from ?
Melbourne Sporting and Dramatic News published a
wild misstatement of the names of Brough’s first comedy
co., mixing up some of the people who retired from it in
the ’Bo’s with others who joined it in the ’9o’s, and intro-
ducing other names that never belonged to it at any
time. The first Brough-Boucicault Comedy Co. con-
sisted of the two managers, Royce, Everard, Geo. Lash
Gordon, E. B. Manning, Miss Trevelyan (Mrs. Brough),
Miss Boucicault, Miss Bessie Major, Miss Wyndale, Miss
Florence Harcourt, Miss Emma Temple, Miss Romer and
Willie Warde, quaintest of dancers. All these people
(bar Boucicault) played both in comedy and burlesque,
and even Fanny Robina, the “principal boy” of the
burlesques, took an occasional part in comedy. Tither-
adge and A. R. Lawrence were later members of the B.
and B. Co. ; then Anson and Cecil Ward joined it some
little time prior to Pattie Browne’s engagement. The
first lady whom B. and B. imported from England after
they were a going concern was Miss Lilian Gilmore, a
charming actress of the light sentimental order, who
married and retired from the stage.
A Melbourne journalist makes known a small adven-
ture with Robert Brough. The man of letters had
written a play. He thought it a particularly fine thing
in the way of plays, and, after several attempts, succeeded
in arranging an interview with Brough, at which the
actor-manager was to sit in the operating chair, and have
the comedy read to him without gas. The playwright,
who was known to the people about the theatre as a
newspaper man, called at the appointed hour, and the
stage-doorkeeper told him that Mr. Brough would be
available presently. “ He’s just dodged out,” said the
faithful servitor, “ but I expect he’ll be back in a few
minutes. He’s giving the slip to some johnnie who wants
to read a play to him 1 ” The playwright said he’d call
again, and didn’t.
_ “Stargazer Julius Knight assures aS. Australian inter-
viewer, who put the question in the interests of the un-
attached Australian girl, that he is a bachelor, and
always has been. He leaves the future to speak for itself.
He tells the same inquirer that David Rossi in “The
Eternal City,” is the character he likes least. “ I have no
love for those talking Socialists. I am very conservative.”
But then the actor men who are not very conservative are
rare birds. The stage peer and the stage king and
the footlights “jook” wear much nicer clothes than the
stage working man, and generally get more applause,
and the actor pretends to be a member of the old nobility
till he almost thinks he really is one. If the English stage
were a vital reflection of the times and manners, it would
be more concerned with the aspirations of the democracy,
and actors might be radicals. As it is, they share their
conservatism with the mummy of Raineses and the
marble man.
The proposed drinking fountain to the memory of late
actor Brough will be a mistake. There is not a memorial
or public drinking fountain in Sydney or suburbs that is
in perfect condition. The gun-metal cups are broken off or
stolen or they mysteriously disappear, and other damage
seems to happen spontaneously. The Frazer fountain
and the Levy fountain in the Botanic Gardens are stand-
ing evidences, unless they have been “ re-cupped ” very
recently.
“ A. 8.” : Ran across an old, yellow English paper of the
seventies, the other day, and found in it a preface to Hol-
man Hunt’s “ Light of the World.” The paragraph told
how Hunt originally painted the picture to represent
“The New Diogenes”—the philosopher of latter-day
civilisation, garbed in plush and jewels, instead of rags ;
but, all the same, still intent on his old quest for the
honest man. Hunt, the journal alleged, had practically
completed the picture when Dante Gabriel Rossetti—him-
self a keen business-man, with all his pre-Raphaelism—
visited the studio and pointed out to the artist that
Diogenes, as a payable proposition, wasn’t a circumstance
to some subject which would reach the great heart of the
religious public. For a long time Hunt refused to alter
his canvas ; but eventually Rossetti’s logic prevailed, and
Diogenes was converted into Christ at the eleventh hour.
Julius Knight, moralising on stage hero-worship, says
that it is very much more pronounced in Australia than in
England. Nobody worships J. Knight in England. But
Julius does not claim that his divine talents and high
attributes provoke the adoration he receives. He says
the notoriety is due to the great amount of personal
journalism which exists in Australia, andwhich supplies
much gratis advt. to the actor. Mr. Knight says he gets
scores of letters from young ladies asking - for his photo-
graph. He used to sell his autographed photo, for half-a-
crown a-piece,- the money going - to a distressed actors’
fund, but “ found it was a lot of bother.” Which means,
probably, that the demand was too enormous for a busy
man to cope with. Julius Superbus settles all disputes
re his much-discussed understandings by admitting that
he measures “fifteen round the calf”—not round the
waist. These interesting particulars furnish more pabu-
lum for that personal journalism which won’t allow the
poor player to escape his weary not ricty.

“T. O’Fipp ” : Doting as I do on the antique, I am glad
to find that Haddon Hall, Dorothy Vernon’s ancestral
abode, is one of the oldest mansions in the Cold Country.
Any stray duke who goes about Britain boasting that his
particular Stately Bile was erected in the days of
Bill the Conqueror is soon silenced at Haddon.
In that hallowed spot even the cow-shed is of
the purest Saxon architecture (very much in the
style of that sweet ruin, the Melbourne City Court).
But the most touching fact of all I find recorded in a
“Sovenir from Derbyshire : For a Good Girl,” which the
vicar’s wife presented to my landlady before she migrated.
Haddon Hall’s “ State bed ” is still furnished with bedding
made by Dame Eleanor Manners in 1487. Several centu-
ries after that date George IV. slept in it. Say what you
will against George, I must after this believe that there
was something of the calmly heroic about him.
Gentleman pug O’Brien, of Philadelphia, is now ap-
pearing on the stage with sparring partners, and
pocketing 200 dollars per week by way of compensation
for loss of his valuable time. He travels with a valet, and
his wardrobe is a thing of beauty, and a joy for all
eternity. Recently the gentleman fellow from Phila-
delphia has grown somewhat bumptious in his treatment
of his sparring partners on the stage, and has bashed
them rather unmercifully. One of them grew suddenly
tired recently, and landed a shower of blows in the
middle of the gentleman fighter’s beauty that laid him
out, and did it so effectually that it u'as all he could do
to crawl on to his feet inside the ten seconds limit. The
sparring partner didn’t know' that he was to be sacked
instanter, or he would probably have laid O’Brien right
out immediately he resumed the perpendicular. In the
meantime O’Brien has been fluting in a marvellous
manner. If Mr. Jeffries should come back to the ring,
Mr. O’Brien. will be only too pleased to meet him. He
has his own system, and he is confident that it is good
enough to lay the mighty boiler-maker right out. His
system, it may be mentioned, is lightning-like speed of
foot, and he reckons he is light-toed enough to keep out
of Jeffries’ reach, and hit the ex-champion just whenever
he likes. There are no winged insects on Mr. O’Brien’s
opinion of his own prowess.
One night recently, according to a yarn pitched to a
Bulletin man by a door-keeper, a woman was found
sitting on the foot of the stairs leading to the gallery of a
Melbourne theatre. She was shedding copious tears into
a large handkerchief, and refused to go away. When
spoken to she sobbed hard enough to shake her hat off,
and declared in heart-broken accents that she wanted to
go in and see the play. She hadn’t the money to pay,
but, oh, she .wanted to see the play so much, and she
cried harder than ever, and implied that existence
wouldn’t be worth living if she didn’t see that play.
She said the play was just the story of her own sad life.
At this juncture a person in authority was called in, and
he was so touched by the woman’s dampness and her
genuine emotion that he let her have a seat in the back
stalls for nothing. Afterwards the woman is reported
to have worked off the same damp jest at other
theatres, and as long as she kept to houses where
tragic shows were being produced it was all right. But she
met her Waterloo when she let down her hair and wept
at the door of a theatre where the most irresponsible
musical comedy on record w'as being produced. When
she said she wanted to go in because this large-hatted,
short-skirted revel of mirth and song and champagne and
dance and jewellery w'as just the story of her own sad life,
the management said curtly that it reckoned she had
had a pretty good tinie and didn’t need sympathy.
J. 0. Williamson has mislaid a theatrical co. amid the
San Francisco earthquake. The Collier Company was
about starting for Australia under engagement to him,
but when last heard of it was a routed combina-
tion, and had lost all its baggage, and was exceed-
ingly glad to have saved itself. Its only chance
of reaching Australia in time lay in catching the Van-
couver steamer last Saturday, and as Friday found it still
trying to get its second wind at Oaklands (California),
four days’ train-ride from Vancouver, and with nothing
but what it stood up in, the case was hopeless.
“L.”: The recent acquisition of titled husbands by
tw'o more London actresses suggests a backward glance
at the British stage peerage. Omitting the informal
brand of blue-blooded match exemplified by Nell Gwynne
and many others, a chronologically-arranged list, so far
as I can discover, of actresses who have allied with titles,
and incidentally their mostly unintelligent human appen-
dages, runs like this: Anastasia Robmson, Countess of
Petersborough (1724); Lavinia Fenton, Duchess of Bolton;
Elizabeth Farren, Countess of Derby; Louisa Brunton,
Countess of Craven ; Mary Bolton, Lady Thurlow ;
Elizabeth O’Neill, Lady (1819); Susannah Paton,
Lady William Lennox; Harriet Mellon, Duchess of St.
Albans (widow of millionaire banker Coutts, who was
grandfather of Irving’s financial backer, Baroness Burdett-
Coutts) ; Maria Foote, Countess of Harrington ; Katherine
Stephens, Countess of Essex (1838): Louisa Mordaunt (or
Nisbett), Lady Boothby; Helena Faucit, Lady Martin
(1851); Miss Robinson, Lady Smith (good old Smith) ;
Emily Sanders, Lady Don (acted with her husband in
Australia in the sixties) ; Miss Fortescue, Lady Gardner ;
Belle Bilton, Countess of Clancarty (1889); Connie Gil-
christ, Countess of Orkney (1892) ; Fanny Stirling, Lady
Gregory ; Mary Augusta (“ May ”) Yohe, Lady Francis
Hope (1894—since divorced); Rosie Boote, Mar-
chioness of Headford (1901); Anna Robinson (can’t
remember title) ; Eva Carrington, Lady de Clifford (1906);
Frances Belmont, Lady Ashburton (1906). Mabel Scott,
who married Earl Russell, had not been on the stage up
to the time of her marriage (1890), but afterwards got
variety engagements on the strength of her title. Then
she divorced her husband and married a flunkey, who
had made her believe he was a prince. She has since
divorced him also. So Britain has known at least 25
titled actresses—three in the 18th century, 18 in the 19th,
and four in the 20th. Three of them were probably
driven to the rash act of titled matrimony in the endeavor
to escape from the name of Robinson, but it is hard to
account for the unwisdom of the others.
“ T.D.” : While the Australian concert artist in England
usually finds it convenient to drop any mention of Aus-
tralia, people on the halls evidently find it a very paying
thing to declare that they are Australian-born, whether
they are or not. Went last night to the Metropolitan in
the Edgeware Road, where one item was supposed to be
given by “ the Australian comics.” Their accent would
have been welcomed with delight by any denizen of the
New York Bowery. A little later “ the Australian tenor ”
came along, and proved to be a full-blooded buck nigger
from way down in Dixie or some other old place in" the
States. I fled in case the next item might also be Aus-
tralian, and next day learned sorrowfully that I had just
missed about the hottest Continental turn (by a smart
little Spanish lady) that has been seen on the London
boards for years.
“Hamer”: The mistakes that pass on the stage un-
noticed by the audience are marvellous. I once staged a
piece, under necessity, at short notice, and from the jump
things became mixed ; but, being all fairly experienced,
we pulled through. Whenever a “wait” became immi-
nent, the hero informed the villain that he’d see him
d d first, or the leading lady announced that she would
rather seek a grave in yonder river (there was no river)
than yield to his terms, or the villain shrieked that he
would be even with them all yet. The audience cheered
these confused proceedings. It was the last act that
caused the most trouble. The hero had asked me hur-
riedly what became of him, and, mistaking him for the
villain, I told him to get off and kill himself. He rushed
off and banged a stick on the wall to represent a pistol
shot ; the old man announced that he had stabbed him-
self, and was arrested by the comedy policeman ; the
leading lady, who should have married the hero, but
couldn't as he was dead, annexed the soubrette’s light-
comedy sweetheart ; and then the villain muttered
“ Foiled !” for the last time, and the faded curtain came
down to considerable applause. Yet that night several
individuals informed me it was “ all right.”
“Roma”: Heredity is hard to combat. A friend of
mine had a nursegirl who was highly recommended to
her as being religious, retiring, etc., etc. One day, she
went out, leaving her baby in charge of the treasure. On
her return, some hours later, she found the treasure gone
and the howling infant almost in convulsions. On the
table was a note:—“ Exkuse me leavin’ yer so sudden
like but ’ive gorn to jine the cirkus wich come to-day, me
mother before me eld a igh persishun in the cirkus an
when the band played to-day i new it was in my blud
w'hen ’ive ris in the cirkus ill come an see you som day,
with love, Mary Ann Smith.”
“ O’F.” : A considerable number of Bendigonians bask
in the reflected glory of relatives who have achieved a
certain amount of vocal fame. Inquiry as to the reason
why some chit puts on an extra amount of swagger in the
Saturday night promenade down the Mall usually results
in the information that she is the cousin of the young lady
who was highly commended for the over-18 soprano solo
at the competitions some years ago. One inhabitant of
the “city of wonderful (sometimes fearful and wonderful)
voices” ran up big accounts (he afterwards scooted and
left them unpaid) on the strength of the allegation that
a world-famous vocalist thought his granddaughter’s
voice “ might do credit to Covent Garden.” After hearing
the voice in question, I believe that if the great singer did
utter this opinion, she made a mental reservation to the
effect that it was the watermelon department of Covent
Garden she was thinking of.
“ A.S.”: Over 20 years ago, when the old Paddy’s Market
Square (Sydney) used to be filled with side-shows on Satur-
day nights, there was always present a curious performer—
Prof. Kolb, snake charmer. Kolb always worked with
Australian snakes, and he contended that in handling the
Australian varieties he was dealing with the most deadly
snakes in the world. I was interested in his work, and
frequently went to his house to see him feed his pets.
Kolb used to handle the tiger and black and brown snakes
with the greatest freedom. Kolb was bitten many times,
but he didn’t let the public know' anything about that.
He had his reputation as a charmer to keep up. He said
snakes with their teeth drawn were no good to him. They
didn’t eat or digest their food well, and became too
sluggish for show purposes. So he preferred to take the
risks. The worst bite he ever got was on the tongue,
while putting a tiger’s head in his mouth —a thing he did
every Saturday. His tongue swelled to immense pro-
portions, and hung down to near the bottom of his waist-
coat, but he never lost consciousness, and in the end
pulled through. When the markets were altered and the
show's w'ent elsewhere, Kolb and his bag of reptiles
drifted aw»ay.
One Australian theatrical manager has been heard to
ascribe the dull times in theatrical business—meaning
the dull times in his own business, of course —to “ the
increased drinking habits of the men.” He says that
men are no longer satisfied with long intervals for
drinks. They want it to be all interval.
A company playing “ Ghosts ” struck Nevada (U.S.A.)
recently, and there was a serious outburst from the robust
local press. “The play is certainly a moral hair-raiser,”
said the critic, W'hodescribed Mr. Aveling as a “spavined
rake ” and Mrs. Aveling as a proud beauty who keeps her
chin in the air and “gives an old tar 300 plunks to marry
the hired girl whom Aveling gets gay with.” The critic
continued : “ Oswald, the mother’s only boy, is sent to
Paris to paint views for marines, and takes kindly to the
gay life of the capital, where the joy of living is the rage;
and families are reared in a section where a printer run-
ning a job office solely on marriage certificates would hit
the poorhouse with a dull thud.” The incident of Regina
and Oswald is mentioned. “Pastor Manders has a spasm,
and Egstrand, w'ho runs a sort of sailors’ and soldiers’can-
teen, sets fire to an orphanage, and the boy, who has inheri-
ted a sort of mayonnaise-dressing brain from his awful
dad, funs amok, tears about the stage a spell, breaks some
furniture, and upsets the wine. He finally takes rough-
on-rats, dies a gibbering idiot, with his mother slobbering
over him and trying to figure out in her mind that he
was merely drunk and disorderly'.” It is remarked that
the company “ handled the sticky mess as well as could
be expected, although Miss Rozeto built up her bustle too
high.” The critic concludes: “As a sermon on the law
of heredity the play is great, but after seeing it we are
glad to announce that Haverley’s minstrels will relieve
the Ibsen gloom on Monday night.”
Mildred de Grey, an American, who dances with bare
feet, has lately' heen taking the eye of London theatre-
goers. As the idea will probably be imported to Aus-
tralia, another shock is in store for good, kind evangelist
Varley.
The Imperial Federation Defence Com-
mittee (whatever that may be) got at the ear
of John Forrest—John’s ear was in London at the
time—with a scheme
whereby the Empire would have one fleet, every part
contributing- to its support, and being represented on
Imperial Councils which could be arranged on a mutually
satisfactory basis.
But the Imperial Federation Defence Committee
didn’t offer any sample of a “ mutually satisfac-
tory basis,” so we are no further forward.
“ O’F.” : The jubilee of Melbourne Uni-
versity brings into prominence once more the
name of its first chancellor, Redmond Barry.
Among all the gossip about him there is no men-
tion of the curious fact that the chief villain of
Thackeray’s “Barry Lyndon ” is called Redmond
Barry. It would be interesting to learn how
Thackeray came to use the uncommon double
name.
A REAL PICK-ME-UP.-“ Talk about cham-
pagne,” said Byron, “there is nothing which
cheers your spirits up like a dose of salts.” But
had he known Hunyadi Janos he would certainly
have preferred this, the best and safest natural
aperient.*
An English barrister named Roth has
been appointed fourth Supreme Court judge in
Westralia. If Westralia can’t grow its own
Supreme Court judge, there are stacks of good
local men in Adelaide, Melbourne, Sydney and
Brisbane—men who know much more of Aus-
tralian law and conditions than any freshly-
imported person is likely to do. But Westralia
has got the British Johnny disease in a very bad
form.
The extended litigation between E. P.
Field, barrister and street preacher, of Sydney,
and late general secretary of the English Evan-
gelical Alliance, and Lord Kinnaird and other
bosses of the Alliance, has been ended. Mr. Field
gets £5OO and a written assurance that there is no
charge against him beyond incompatibility of
temper; in other words, either the ex-street
preacher or the Alliance is too cantankerous.
Last Tuesday (May 1) Richard Seddon
entered his 14th year as continuous Premier of
Maoriland. It is a record that will probably not
be reached again in this century. As a general
thing five years is regarded as a very long spell of
office without a break, and only nine Australasian
Ministries have turned the critical corner into the
sixth year. Here is the list up to this day of
publication :
Years. Months. Days.
Seddon (Maoriland).. ..14 0 2
Forrest (Westralia).. ..10 1 17
Kingston (S. Australia) .. 6 5 15
Herbert (Queensland) .. 6 1 22

A cry from “ D.H.R.” :
I wish to inform you of the utter absurdity of your re-
marks about importing a railway commissioner from
Canada or Britain when Japan is so close, and we all know
what gallantry they displayed during the recen i war with
the Russians, and your idea of employing an Australian
is consummately preposterous, and, I suppose, is the out-
come of your Socialistic ideas. Anyhow, about Socialism
7- don’t you see what dire calamities have lately 7 occurred
in Italy and San Francisco ; and to any 7 reasonable or
thoughtful man or woman, there can be no doubt that
those calamities are the result of the hideous machina-
tions of the Labor party. I hope that these matters will
cause you to see the error of your ways, and that from
this 1 ut you will cease to be a Socialist and advocate the
Brotl erhood of Man, and when the gentle Chinaman
takes your eldest daughter, you will present him with
your second one, and that you will advocate the sending
forth of armies to redeem the poor black person from
heathenism, and not hunt capital from your country or
violate the sanctity of the marriage tie any 7 more.
N.S.W. railways were launched, so to say,
under a Board of Three. Ward, Barker, and
Keinp were appointed Commissioners in 1854 —
Ward with the style and title of Chief. He and
his associates pulled together indifferently, and
Ward resigned after a seven months’ struggle.
It was then thought better to have a single Com-
missioner, and Captain Mann was given the job.
He barely met expectations. IN ext, engineer
Martindale was invited from England. He was
the first imported railway genius, but he was the
worst disappointment of all, and returned to
England after three-and-a-half years. Then the
authorities fell back on John Rae. It was the
custom at that period to call on Rae when any-
thing of moment went wrong with the public
service. Rae stuck to the job for 17 years, and,
although better as a Town Clerk, was yet a very
successful Commissioner for Railways. When Rae
moved off Goodchap stepped in as sole Commis-
sioner. After him came Eddy, with Oliver and
Fehon, and next Oliver, with Fehon and Kirk-
caldie. Now another arrangement impends.
What could more eloquently support the
contention that the world’s spirit of progress
waxeth stronger than the fact that more than 12
million gallons of D.C.L. whisky are distilled
every year? Inasmuch as it is’true that the
wisdom of the imbibers increases at the same rate
as the consumption of D.C.L. there is some hope
for this old world yet. Man is what he drinks.*
Justice G. B. Simpson (N.S.W.) invites
the public to help him when they know,
or think they know, anything which would,
if known by him, prevent him granting a
divorce. During the reign of the Judge’s pre-
decessor an individual who took that public
duty on his shoulders had a very bad time
with the presiding Judge. But the applicant for
divorce on that occasion was a prominent
politician.
“ A. D.” :. Earthquake and volcano sites
have a peculiar, fascination for the human build-
ing ant. Passing over a lava-strewn gutter on
my way from San Guiseppe to Vesuvius, once, I
asked an old guide why so many villages were
clustered within hen-crow of the crater. “Can’t
they find a more solid resting place than the rim
of a volcano ? ” I demanded. The old man snarled
at me over his shoulder as though a slur had been
cast upon the quaking, breathing hell-heap.
Then in wrath he asked what I considered a good
place for honest people to live. “Dio mio !” he
rasped, “ the voice of Vesuvius will never drive us
away. We love her—the pulsing monster. She
groans and roars to us. It is good to feel the
earth belch and quake at night, to smell the
crater-smoke at dawn. By Cristo ! you English
think you are safe on your wet island. The sea
will smother you some night—mark, Signor, it
will smother you in Atlantic mud and dead fish.
Then we shall see Jim Bull looking for a hencoop
m the Bay of Ireland.” In the same locality I
met an Australian chemist sniffing the crater
fumes. He was certain, he said, that a month
spent within the lip of the volcano would cure
phthisis in an elephant. Up to date I haven’t
seen any bottled Vesuvius on sale at the local
chemists’.

“ Lil ” : A swaggering young Greek, in his
curious national costume (apparently a new
arrival), attracted attention the other day in the
neighborhood of the numerous Swanston-street
(Melb.) fish and fruit shops run by his country-
men, The Greek found loose thereabouts does
not often show off the red cap, white ballet-skirt,
and tightly-pinched waist which even the brick-
laying Theotokis Plasteropoulos displays in his
native country. It is doubtful whether Constan-
tine Barracoutos doffs his petticoat in Australia
because'his long hours leave him no time to laun-
der it, or because the beauteous Highland soldier
threatens to prosecute him for infringement of
copyright. Anyway, he still seems to view his
greyhound waist with pride. I heard one floun-
deraserver boasting to another that he never wore
braces or belt, as bis big hips and record-breaking
small waist filled the bill ! Don't talk of female
vanity while the Grecian fried-fish hero is about.

The headmaster of a Maorilancl school—
the Whangarei technical college—the other day
got the shock of his life along with a letter from
the scholar who had won the prize in the English
Literature class. In the course of a lengthy essay
on the effects of corn petition, the youngster told the
superintendent that “prize-giving is calcul ited
to foster a mercenary spirit—the spirit of doing
your work only for what you can get.” Therefore
the youth’presented his apologies and declined
the prize. Now one wonders whether the youth
who won the prize played that essay off his own
little bat, or. whether in the background of the
great self-denying ordinance there was a Stern
Parent with ideas and a rod.
“ Albert D.” : The recent unforetokl
earthquake at San Erancisco has cast a depression
over the seer and the clairvoyant. Not one of
them ever had the power to predict an ordinary
fall of gaspipe at the right moment. I called on
a Sydney “ second-sighter ” the night after the
Californian cataclysm, and found him in a state
of rum and dejection. Waving me to the medium’s
chair he breathed into a half-empty bottle and
wept. “Don’t speak to me about ’Frisco. I
saw it all here, in this room, at 7.30 t’other
night. I felt there was something in the air,’’
he continued; “a grand momentuous uplifting
of the spheres. Closing my eyes, I took a grip of
things, and let myself into space. Then I felt a
hot sea wind blowing over my naked body as I
was hurled through the Starry Way. The earth
lifted and quaked, my friend, like a whale’s back.
I saw myself in a strange city, shut in by tall sky-
scrapers and a mountain-locked bay. The air was
full of crashing walls and the groans of the
dying.” The “second-sighter” sat up in his
chair, and put a silent grip on my wrist. “ Why
didn’t you publish your experience?” I ventured.
He glared at me like a sheep with toothache.
“ Publish h 1! I couldn’t locate the place,” he
cried. “At first I thought it was Lisbon, then
Sydney or Teneriffe. 1 saw Chinamen flying
here and there ; I saw the looters at work among
the dead, saw the soldiers firing upon the gangs of
robbers prowling among the ruins of warehouses
and banks. I was afraid to publish my vision
because my geographical bearings are not sound.”
He raised the bottle with the air of a man who
had missed a bonanza. I departed into another
office in the same building, where my friend Ram
Singh carries on business as crystal reader and in-
spired spook. Before I could say “Frisco ’ lie
began to claw the tablecloth with his yellow
fingers. “Ah, dam!” he shouted, “I lose one
bletty gold mine ! My dead father gif me the
office only the other night. ‘ The bletty world is
all up, Ram,’says lie to me. ‘Smash! Bang!
to-morrow at 5 o’clock.’ Then I tie myself in
the chair and get my pencil to work. ‘ What part
of the world goes bang first, my father?’
I say to him. And I hear his body movin’
among the banyans of Brahm. ‘ Molong
and Parkes,’ he say in a far, far away
voice. Then I feel to myself that someone
had pulled my father from the highest Mahatma
on to a pile of potatoes.” The Hindu stood up
suddenly and wrung his hair. “ All my bletty
information goes bang whenever dat bletty Jew
upstairs goes past hero with Iris beer bottle. I
call cm my dear father again to tell me where the
earthquake was going to open, and he only answer
‘ Crojingolong and Jimmy Kawolski.’ Then I
send a circular to my clients, telling them that all
the bletty sheep and sand would be shaken off the
back, of Molong and Parkes.” The Hindu beat
his breast. “ See how 1 fall in because the dam
peoples carry the beer over me when I speak with
my father !” 1 left him. There were other seers
and spielers in the same building who mistook the
great earthquake for something else.

Re ignorance of Australian geography and
history, referred to by “Ped.” (B. 15/3/’O6). Most
of the blame for this can justly be debited to the
account of teachers of private
schools. Many of them are
English, Scotch, or Irish born.
Their knowledge of Australian
geography is the limited amount
contained in a printed-in-England
text-book, compiled by an English
geographer; and Australian his-
tory is a subject of which the
greater number of them never
heard. It is quite common to
meet private school pupils who
know every county in England.
Ireland, and Scotland, and are full
of information regarding Transyl-
vania, Andalusia, and Mesonota-
mia, but who are quite ignorant
of the whereabouts of the chief
Australian towns, except, per-
haps, the State capitals. They
know the names of all the English
nionarohs from William the Con-
queror to Queen Victoria, with
dates of accession and death, and
don’t know the name of the Pre-
mier of their own State. A know-
ledge of Australian geography
(very thorough) and history (very
fair) is imparted in N.S.W. State
schools; but it is possible for
teachers in these schools to pass
their exams, without answering a
single question upon Australia.
Also, the Sydney University
junior history paper should include
Australian history, a branch totally ignored by
the Uni.
“The Government should he ready with
generous assistance ” (S.M. Herald, 24/4/’O6).
And this appeal for Government assistance is in
behalf of a memorial to a dead person. When a
bonus to establish an industry is suggested, the
Herald yells about “pampering,” “pauperising,”
See., &c. An industry helps to keep a live person
alive ; a memorial doesn’t make a dead man any
deader. So why not give the “generous assist-
ance” to the living? And why pamper defunct
persons with tombstones and pauperise cadavers
with blocks of granite ?

ANOTHER BIRTH-RATE ITEM.
THE REGISTRAB (forgetting the date): “Let me see , this is the tenth."
THE MOTHER (indignantly): “The first!”
On April 12th, Charlotte Bernard, aged
54, was knocked down by a cab in George-street,
Sydney, and was removed to Sydney Hospital.
Two stitches were put in her head, and she was
then taken off to the Central Police Station on a
charge of “drunk.” A little later she appeared
very ill, and was hurried off to the hospital again,
and placed in the observation ward for some hours.
Next morning she was fined for being drunk, and
turned loose. That night the long arm of
the Law saw her acting rather strangely, and
sagaciously ran her in once more as a
“drunk.” Next morning the matron at the lock-
up had a look at her, and, growing suspicious,
packed her off to the hospital for the third time.
Then she was brought back to the police station,
and locked up till next morning, when the Court
remanded her for medical observation. At the
gaol hospital the Government Medical Officer,
Dr. Paton, examined her, and diagnosed, not
alcoholism, but a brain injury, and sent her to
Sydney hospital some more. By this time she was
getting quite used to being sent to the hospital.
There she died on the 19th, and a post mortem re-
vealed that the cause of death was a fracture of the
skull received in the cab accident on the 12th.
Now, will the Law do the honest thing by the dead
woman’s memory, and change the police court
record to read that Charlotte Bernard was fined,
not for being drunk, which seems to be a lie, but for
having a fractured skull? Even if it does nothing
to the ridiculous people who seemed to think fine,
imprisonment, and frequent and furious cab
exercise from the gaol to the hospital and back
were the best remedies for a broken head, it
might do that much.
* * » *
The British House of Commons will
shortly be asked to consider a Bill which will
make it a felony on the part of any trader or
manufacturer to stick the royal arms, or
any other emblem of the English or any
other reigning family, on his labels.
The Bill will also suppress the testi-
monials from the Princess Olga of
Dad bag about sending at once six
more bottles of your invaluable pre-
paration for removing hair, or the
like. This sort of public deception
has become very rife lately, and so
many things are on the markets of the
world with the royal arms of some
State emblazoned on the labels, that
people are beginning to wonder how
many millions of royalties there are.
In Australia the use of the big cat
and the spiked horse has become an
abuse, and one class of time-payment
fiend has put the royal arms on top
of his final notice threatening to im-
prison people who have not paid up or returned
the clothing in the same order and condition in
which they received it.

A highly-academic crowd of persons sat
down to eat a University dinner in Melbourne
Masonic Hall on Tuesday night last week, and
when filled with good wine soared into the regions
of lofty culture, and made'academically-satirical
remarks about “ the practical man,” and bee line
merry over the beastly ignorance that prevents
him from accepting the culchawed enthusiasts at
the valuation of their own vanity. Bent, with
his chin arranged in two or three folds on his
chest, waited till everybody had finished, ai.d
then bellowed: “Let me tell you that if tie
practical men hadn’t come forward with their
money, you wouldn’t be able to say all these
pretty things about yourselves to-night.” Then
the vain ones began to wonder whether polit#
learning hadn’t marie them forget politeness.
Romeo was up before Melbourne Magis-
trate Panton last week—Romeo Trudo. He
desired Juliet Gwendoline Frances Smith, or
Brown, or it may have been Jones, to con-
fine her affections to him alone; Juliet
Gwendoline was not so disposed; wherefore
Romeo promised to shoot her, or, failing that,
“to get all the blackfellows in Melbourne to get
her out of her abode and then to saw her in six
pieces with an axe.’’ Magistrate Panton, who
begins to be a tired old man and regards all these
“ love ” episodes as mere spasms of cucumber or
insanity, remarked that it was a trivial case, fined
Romeo 405., and bound him over to keep the
peace towards his Juliet Gwendoline. So Romeo,
instead of having it out with the fiery Tybalt, will
doubtless proceed to raise the 40s. by pawning his
revolver with the fiery Cohen.

“The Manor House” is now be-
coming a fairly common residence-title in such
places as Victoria’s Western District, where the
land-grabber lurks when he is not spending his
Australian revenue in London. A standard dic-
tionary defines “manor” as “lord’s estate in
lands kept for own use.” Now, how’ long has, say,
Mr. Percy Chirnside (who keeps a “Manor
House”) been a lord?
This constant effort to
ape the squire and the
bucolic baronet, and to
look like a sort of bush
peer, is a very dreadful
thing.
