The British hired fleet in Australian
waters is certainly ruled with very little sense.
Time and again the use of a warship has been
refused to search for a missing vessel—though
this is a recognised part of a warship’s business in
limes of peace—and last week the request that
one of the vessels should help in the Captain
Cook celebration was denied. Seeing that the
warships are always ready to lend themselves to
ceremonial pageantry on behalf of any British or
foreign royal festival, it would have shown tact to
havs helped along this Australian celebration.

“K.” : Surely it is time Bendigo Easter
Fair committee obliterated the Chinese element
from its procession. For many long weary years
the yellow person has dragged through the public
ways his long uninteresting dragon with the same
eternal invariableness. It always looks much the
same dragon. Ahead of it slouches a disorderly
crowd of pig-tailed brethren. Behind it there
potters and shuffles and toddles and slithers along
another disorganised mob of pig tailed heathenism.
Here and there in the scramble is a tom-tom.
Here and there is something like an ice-cream
cart possessed of a devil. Here and there a
banner hangs anyhow. This year, a Chow
herbalist, in a buggy, decorated the procession
with a group of white girls walking in front
and another behind. That made me tiled. For
the rest, there were the dragon, the ice-cream
cuts, the tom-toms, and the shambling mob as
usual. lam told that the Chow procession gets
£5O or -£7O appearance-money for its paper wild
beast and its barbaric din. If this is so, Waffor?

Victorian Railway Commissioner Tait has
replied to the judgment of the Court of Inquiry
into the Gembrook smash. The Com t said the
accident was due to the bad state of the per-
manent way, and that a subordinate cause was
the rate of speed. Tait says that driver Sheehan
will be dealt with at once, and so will the ganger
in charge of that section of the line. He neatly
avoids the obvious inference from the'judgment—
that he, T. Tait, is the responsible man, because he
set six men to do maintenance work that could not
be done by sixteen. He passes that phase of the
question over entirely. And there is no hint that
the maintenance of the road will be any better in
the future.
“ Tam bo ” : Re par. anenfc Devonport,
Tasmania (B. 5/4/’0(5). It is quite correct as
regards the success of the town’s Socialistic muni-
cipal undertakings ; yet so inconsistent
is the average Devonport man that he
would faint in horror on the bank of his
river at the mere whisper of the word
Socialism. Devonport, however, does
not look on Launceston as a “devil of
a place”—at least, not in that sense.
Similar feeling exists between Laun-
ceston and Devonport as between Mel-
bourne and Sydney, and about once
a month Launceston and Devonport
newspapers (tlie latter town runs two
dailies) get on the subject, and the way
Potts of Devonport speaks of Laun-
ceston, and the way Slurk of Laun-
ceston alludes to Devonport, is some-
thing terrible and insidious. No two
towns on earth are more grieved at the
other town’s backwardness. Launceston
papers have a pull over Devonport, as
they supply the Tasmanian correspon-
dence for the mainland dailies, and they
take care that that correspondence
never mentions Devonport except as
a bygone and a backslider. Yet Devon-
port is the second port in Tasmania
(Hobart is first), and its outward trade
last year ran into nearly a quarter of a
million. An important piece of infor-
mation, which Launceston carefully
suppressed and is still sitting on hard,
is that Postmaster-General Chapman
is considering the advisability of
changing the centre of Tasmanian mail
distribution from Launceston to Devon-
port. Poor unfinancial Tasmania is
paying £ll,OOO per annum for a fatt
mail service, .which it doesn't get
because the much-talk ecl-of turbine
steamer Loongana encounters fogs and
low tides in the forty odd miles of
winding Tamar, and can’t keep its time-
table in consequence. Devonport is
much nearer Melbourne, and the Loon-
gana could make an extra trip per week
by running there. The feeling at
present points to civil war if the change is
made. Hobart, which doesn’t love Launceston
either, never loses an opportunity of barracking for
“ the city of the Mersey.” Launceston is think-
ing of going over some day and burning cobble-
stoned Hobart.
* * * *
“ Melb.” : One thousand being the mem-
bership of Tom Mann’s Social Questions Com-
mittee, it is becoming a factor. After being
turned out of its cellar, it is turned cut of the
Queen’s Hall, Bourke-street, on Sunday nights.
Whereupon it leaps to the Bijou Theatre, at £6 a
night. The red flag is adopted. Mrs. Mann
renders assistance as choir leader, with Labor
hymns. An orchestra is formed. Funds are
coming in. For a little thing, the society cleared
£lO net profit from a bit of a coffee stall at
the Eight-hours fete. Members of the Political
Labor Council, associated with the Trades Hall,
are taking steps to prevent its members
from joining Mann’s society, and there
will be a fight. The society has propaganda
open-air meetings in the various Melbourne
suburbs on Saturday nights. Mann is sedulously
training speakers, both men and women, and quite
a number are learning his own style of splasl y-
whirling, irrelevant oratory. The Sunday pro-
paganda is as busy as it can be. Open-air meetings
in the morning where they can be fixed. Sunday
afternoon meeting at Yarra South Bank, the
familiar pitch below Government House. Sunday
nights, Bijou Theatre. Every night in the week
is occupied. Of course the prospective election
Euts the society in a hurry. Two numbers have
een issued of the society’s paper, the Socialist,
price one penny.
SIC TRANSIT.
IST MUSICIAN : “ How's your new piano going,
Von Bibbler ?”
VON BIBBLER: “Got in Himmcl! he went off
yesterday. The deni bailiff got ’im!"
In Melbourne the Law is getting itself
into a frightful tangie over the matter of extradi-
tion. In November last a man was arrested on a
charge of getting away from Natal with h s
creditors’ money, and P.M. Panton ordered his
extradition. lie appealed on the ground that
Victoria was not a British possession, but only a
part of one, and therefore Panton, being only a
Victorian P.M., was not competent to commit
him. After grappling strenuously with this
point, the Full Court decided that Victoria,
though only part of the Commonwealth, was still
a jewel in the blessed Crown, and upheld the P.M.
Now the man from Natal appeals to the High
.Court. All this bother might have been avoided if
some Federal Ministry had passed an Order-in-
Council appointing State police magistrates to
be Commonwealth officials. Another case, that
of Smith, or Griffin, is even more tangled
up. Smith was arrested in Albany (W.A.)
on August 11, 1905, charged with forgery,
alleged to have been committed in Natal.
That charge was abandoned and another,
of falsification, substituted. He was committed,
but appealed, and was discharged. In Adelaide
he was re-arrested on the old forgery charge, and
once more discharged. He thought he would be
left at peace in Melbourne, but again the old fal-
sification business was brought up, and P.M.
Panton ordered his extradition, but delayed the
execution of the warrant to permit of an appeal.
Naturally Smith is asking whether this Australia
is one country or twenty.
* * * *
The beneficial effects of the “Light of the
World ”at length become manifest. Last Satur-
day a Palmerston North (M.L.) man journeyed
to Palmerston to see Holman Hunt’s canvas.
Afterwards he went out and shot his wife dead,
and then tried to kill himself. The same day, at
Auckland, a man shot himself dead while listening
to a Salvation band—instead of slaying the band.
* * * *
Fitzroy (Vic.) police court, with its bench
of ancient honoraries, was knocked endways last
week by the incomprehensible action of a Quaker.
The Quaker was a hawker, and while he was
round at the back of a house saying “thou ” and
“thee” to the householder in his own broad-
brimmed language, a heathen with a record of 15
prior convictions walked off with his outfit. The
police recovered the stolen property and arrested
the thief, and the honoraries gave him three
months. Then they started to make out an order
for the restoration of the property, but the Quaki r
explained that his conscience would not permit
him to take back an article that bad been stolen
from him, and the magistrates could do what they
liked with the truck, vegetables, and scales. The
magistrates gasped and fell against each other,
but in the end they told the sergeant to act as he
thought best. The sergeant accordingly presented
the truck and scales to an industrious old chap
aged 74, who had yearnings after the bold, free
life of a hawker. The Quaker went out to buy a
second outfit.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The ■‘Householders’ League,” London, announces that
if certain considerations are shown by the Australian
Governments the League will send out hundreds of
voung women. The desire of the young women is to form
k settlement round the towns and raise poultry, fruit,
arid other agricultural products. The? will also be
WILLING TO MARRY.
A remarkable message ! nor is it too clear
It will please the sagacious minority l
There's a fear
The idea
May appear
Pretty queer,
Though it comes on the highest authority 1
And, moreover, ’tis plain
It has managed to gain
A position approaching priority ;
And, to judge from the various rumors we hear,
It might possibly please the majority I
The majority, that is to say, of young men.
’Tis an outrage no lady could palliate 1
We may possibly find
It will make them inclined
To directly and promptly retaliate 1
For it isn’t a matter to palliate,
And on every ground
To be possibly found,
They are morally bound
To retaliate !
Do these impudent Londoners really suppose
A young Austral maiden’s timidity
Would dispose
Her to those
Who might close
If they chose
On her palpable rights with avidity?
If they do they will find
She possesses a mind
Of a clear and surprising lucidity 1
For she isn’t the sort to be led by the nose
As a person of arrant stupidity I
The Commonwealth damsel possesses a vote ;
Is a force in this model community ;
Competition to bring
In her sphere is a thing
That is not to be done with impunity 1
No, it’s not to be done with impunity 1
For the sake of Imperial unity
Send a lad with eacli lass
And the project may pass
As a very first-class
Opportunity

W. T. GOODGE.
HEALTHY GIRLHOOD.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
Healthy girlhood is the only path to healthy
womanhood. The merging of girlhood into
womanhood lays a new tax upon the blood —and
nothing can pay that tax but Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, for they alone contain the elements that go
to make new blood. It is the overtaxing of the
scanty blood supply that makes growing girls suffer
from all those headaches, backaches and sideaches
—all that paleness, weakness, and weariness—all
that languor, fainting spells and nervousness—the
perverse appetite, the despondency, and the
constant ill-health.
Unhealthy girlhood is bound to lead to un-
healthy womanhood and a life of misery. No-
thing but the blood-building qualities of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills can save a girl when first she
undertakes the tasks and trials of womanhood.
That is the time when Nature makes new demands
upon the blood-supply. But Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills actually make new, pure, rich, red blood
which meets those new demands with ease. In
this simple, scientific way, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
fill a girl with overflowing health and strength.
Without them, no young woman can be fully
fitted for the duties and delights of wifehood and
motherhood.
“Soon after I turned 14, my health began to
fade,” said Miss Roughley, 422 Jones-street,
Ultimo, Sydney. “My face was pale and sickly,
and my lips and gums were almost bl ue. Round
my eyes were heavy, black rings. When I held
my hand up to the light you could almost see
through it. I had hardly any blood at all. I was
too weak to dust a room. Someone had always to
be kept in the house to do the work that I should
have done. I always wanted to be lying down. I
couldn’t be bothered with anything or anybody.
“I grew to hate the sight of food,” Miss
Roughley added. “Every mouthful lay on my
chest like a weight of lead, and gave me a terrible
cutting pain. Night was what I dreaded most.
Often I would lie awake till daylight. When it
was time to get up I had not the energy to dress
myself. I sat on the bed for an hour or more,
trying to struggle into my clothes. Every day I
grew worse. My feet swelled up as bad as if I
had Dropsy.
“ Every day I went from bad to worse. I
was irritable, and everything got on my nerves. I
couldn’t bear the least sound. I would cry my
eyes out for nothing at all. If I walked a hit fast
my heart felt as if it were coming up in my throat
—and, for two or three minutes, my breath was
gone. If I went as far as the grocer’s, a terrible
pain caught me in the back, and a horrible faint
feeling came over me. I felt so weak and dread-
fully ill that I used to wonder how ever I would get
home again. At last I got so bad that I bad to go
to the Hospital in Sydney. They did their best
for me there—but I came home as bad as the day
I went in.
“As the weeks slipped by, I grew weaker. I
thought I would never be strong again. Then one
day I read how Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills had
cured a young girl suffering almost the same way
as I was. I sent straight to Pattinson’s for some
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. After a box or two I
felt a little better, and picked up heart,” Miss
Roughley concluded. “Everyday I noticed the
difference in my appetite. Soon I was eating as
well as anyone in the house. Gradually the blood
came back to my lips and cheeks. My old energy
and spirits returned, and I gained in weight. I
have been well and strong ever since I finished my
last box of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills—and that was
three or four years .ago. Since my cure. I have
not had one hour’s pain or sickness. Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills saved my life.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills don’t tinker with mere
symptoms. They don’t act on the bowels. They
do only one thing, but they do it well—they
actually make new blood. They won’t cure any
disease that isn’t caused originally by bad blood.
But when Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills replace bad
blood with good blood, they strike straight at the
root and cause of all common diseases like head-
aches, sideaches and backaches, kidney trouble,
liver complaint, biliousness, indigestion, anaemia,
neuralgia, sciatica, nervous exhaustion, failing
powers, locomotor ataxia, and the special secret
troubles that every woman knows but that none
of them like to talk about, even to their doctors.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by re-
tailers and the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Sydney—2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d.,
post free. Medical advice free.
N.S.W. Divorce Justice Simpson has made
many couples uneasy with his declaration that
only a conscientious objection to being married
by a clergyman legalises marriage by a Registrar.
Hundreds of people get married by the Registrar
as a matter of convenience, though they have no
objection to being married by a parson. But, of
course, any little irregularity, such as a false
declaration of conscientious objection or a neglect
of the Registrar to ask for a declaration, would
not in any way invalidate a marriage. Angelina
has her Percival still tied as firmly as before.
Public policy, however, should recognise tl e
Registrar’s marriage, and only the Registrar’s
marriage; it should make all couples go through
the civil ceremony, leaving them at liberty to go
through any church parade they please afterwards.
Such a reform would, amongst other benefits,
abolish the disreputable business of the broken-
down parson who runs a marriage-shop on cheap-
and-nasty lines.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Somebody writes again to the papers complain-
ing- of the life insurance offices’ charge of 7 or 8 per cent,
interest on overdue premiums. The usual reply is made
most of the amounts are so small that it would not be
worth while charging up less. All the same, it is ridicu-
lous to be charging one’s own members 7 or 8 per
cent, on absolutely protected loans, while the public
is being lent money for as little as 4 per
cent, on security which involves more or less
risk. Take the A.M.P., for instance. Even of its mort-
gages 75 percent, carry under 5 per cent., and only 6
per cent, carry 6 per cent, and over ; yet of the loans on
policies and overdue premiums no less than 48 per cent,
carry 6 per cent, and over. The objection that most of
the amounts are small, and that it wouldn’t be worth
while charging them up if the rate were made, say, 5 or 6
per cent, could be easily got over by making a minimum
charge—whatever the management thinks is necessary to
cover the wear and tear of the staff’s intellect.

Though this bank’s net profits st ill sag, most of its other
figures show a large increase:—
Mar.,’os. Mar.,’o6.
Notes in circulation. £129,968 £157,846 Inc. £27,878

Deposits .. .. 2,334,238 3,145,970 Inc. 811,732
Liquid assets .. .. 1,668,129 2,506,275 Inc. 838,140
Advances .. .. 1,700,946 1,913,016 Inc. 212,070
Premises .. .. 71,483 70,299 Dec. 1,184
The growth of deposits is something extraordinary ; they
have increased during the year by over one-third, and
there have been large increases of other liabilities like-
wise. The accounts do not distinguish between deposits
bearinj; interest and those not bearing interest; but
there it no doubt that the increase of deposits at interest
has been vastly greater than the increase of advances;
and as the accumulated mone3' has either been put out at
short call in London, at a very low rate of interest, or
kept in the vaults, a shrinkage of profits is easily accounted
for. Nevertheless, the business has been so profitable
during the year that it would show' even better
results than it did in the previous vear but for the
writing-down of premises by £2OOO. Going on its
figures alone the Bank of Adelaide is one of the surprises
and puzzles of Australian banking. It show's a profit
(after writing off £2OOO from premises) at the rate of
£6 18s. 4d. per cent, on shareholders’ funds; and yet it
maintains in liquid assets no less than 66 per cent, of its
liabilities to the public. The possible explanations of
how the thing is done are several. (1) S. Australia is
pretty clannish, and has a very good opinion of its own
honesty ; and the Bank of Adelaide is itself. More than
a third of all the bank-notes in S. Aus. are issued by the
Bank of Adelaide. (2) It has about 1000 shareholders—
small people mostly, and not professional investors, and
they are, therefore, so many unpaid canvassers for the
bank. (3) It doesn’t poke round into corners of the earth
that it knows very little about; but of the State to which
it confines itself no body of men know more than its
directors. For investment, the fact that the bank works
not under the limited liability Act, but under a charter’
prejudices the shares. And, no doubt, the larger the
popularity of a bank, and the more deposits it can get
the bigger the risk of shareholders in case of a slump!
But the Adelaide directors, recognising this, maintain
what under other circumstances would be an absurdly
needless proportion of liquid assets ; and correspondingly
they so limit advances that the w’hole of the latter could
slump 36 per cent, before the shareholders’ funds would
be exhausted. The dread of something unknown hap-
pening because the co. isn’t “ limited” hasn’t, therefore,
for the present, anything very solid to go on. All the
same, it is something that makes it necessary for share-
holders to keep awake. Yet Adelaide, in spite of the
risk, never lets the shares go begging. On the present
dividend they return under 5 per cent., and even a rise
of dividend to 9 per cent, would show on present prices
no more than £5 6s. per cent.

This great bank, whose worst fault is that its profits are
mostly distributed outside Australia, continues on its way
of solid prosperity ; nevertheless, like most of the rest of
them —especially those that are not inclined to take up
business that has only a speculative bottom to rest upon
it shows the effects of a glut of money and a lack of
means to profitably use it. Take this comparison
Oct., 1904. Oct., 1905.
Notes .. .. £471,783 £450,854 Dec. £20,929
Bills, &c. .. 2,011,391 2,319,698 Inc. 308 307
Deposits .. 15,186,394 16,329,565 Inc. 1,143,171

Advances., .; 14,368,061 14,294,304 Dec. 73 757
Premises .. 306,527 285,581 Dec. 20,946
Thus in the 12 months there was a net increase of
£1,430,549 in the public’s money held by the bank ; on
the other hand, advances actually shrank £73,757, thus
throwing altogether £1,504,306 into the hands of the bank.
Of this amount over £600,000 is in quite unproductive
specie ; another £BOO,OOO is out on short call in London,
probably at 2 per cent.—lent out to the banks that lend
to the men who either lend back to Australia, or else
make the goods that Australia ought to be making for itself
with the help of the same money, without all this finan-
cial circumlocution.
It is an amazing tiling under the circumstances
—with over an extra million and a half doing either
nothing at all or very little—that the Bank of Australasia
should sustain its profit even as it does. But how does it,
or any other Australian bank, doit? By cutting down
interest on deposits ; by charging high rates for accom-
modation to farmers and others; by, competing with
insurance offices, trustees, &c., for gilt-edged mortgage
business which is hardly banking business at all; and by
under-paying its staff (though in this respect less com-
plaint can be made of the ’Asia than of some other banks,
albeit the standard of bank-clerks’ pay at the best is
wretched). The absurd idea that only the artisan would
benefit by the establishment of effective Protection, and
the establishment of Australian industries—which would
mean Australian securities—dies hard, nevertheless. By
clipping and competing in the ways just mentioned, the
Bank of Australasia has been able to keep its profits (on
the basis of shareholders’ capital and reserves) as fol-
lows
Apr., 1902.. £ll 1 5 p.c. I Apr., 1904.. £lO 19 9 p.c.
Oct., 1902.. 10 6 9 „ | Oct., 1904.. 10 1 9 „
Apr., 1903.. 11 15 0 „ I Apr., 1905.. 914 0 „
Oct., 1903.. 11 8 3 „ | Oct., 1905.. 913 9 „
But, in spite of all that the bank hgs been able to do, the
fall in earnings from £ll 15s. per cent, to £9 13s. 9d. per
cent, is pretty severe; on the present funds it means
£59,142 a year. In other words, if this bank could make
profits at the same rate now as it did at the end of 1902,
it would be making, on its present funds, £59,142 a year
more than it is doing. The three fine seasons which Aus-
tralia has had, along with the record metal outputs at
almost record prices, should have made business hum.
Instead, however, of being a blessing, the extra money
has been actually, in a sense, a burden to the banks—all
because the industries that ought to have spurted, and
called for more capital at good rates, have been carried
on, not in Australia with Australian money, but in some
foreign country with foreign money—helped a little by
Australian money, shipped away, and lent out at ridicu-
lously low rates. Under such circumstanees the strain
to pay a 12 per cent. div. is liable to become severe ; and
it.isn’t altogether wonderful that acting-superintendent
Cowper lately declined to take on the job permanently.
The Bank of Australasia has now in liquid assets 39 p.c.
of all its liabilities to the public ; and, though that is a
heavier holding than is necessary under anything but
very special conditions, still many other Australian banks
find themselves with a much larger accumulation. The
debts to the public total £19,100,117; liquid assets
amount to £7,483,788 ; so there remains £11,616,329 of
public debt to be covered by £14,294,304 of advances and
£285,581 of premises—book-value ; the reai value is cer-
tainly greater. In view of the manifest quality of the ad-
vances (the earnings prove that), it is a position which
ought to give the depositors much comfort.

to block further remarks regarding the management’s

These undoubtedly are good figures ; but most of the
gain in profits looks as though it might have come from
foreign exchange business, and that, to a great extent,
isn t a business that sticks through good seasons and bad.
Note, as to this, the very large increase in “bills in circula-
tion ” ; andamong theliquid assets “ billsand remittances
in transit” account for £1,403,708, as against £923,603
twelve months before. The National has been so ex.
tremely fortunate (or well-managed possibly) as to be
able to make profitable use of money at a time when
others have seen it accumulating; at the same time, from
all appearances, the National’s money has been in use in
operations which don’t necessarily mean the same sort of
business or profits for the future. It isn’t, therefore,
safe to regard the recent rate of profits as normal ; still
less safe would it be to expect the rise to continue. Cer-
tainly, it is possible that previously the bank was steadily
writing down bad and doubtful debts, and that it hasn’t
need to do so now ; hence the sudden spring. But there
is no sign that it is so. On the contrary, it is pretty
certain that this bank can still find very good use for
surplus profits in writing down book values; the line
“Bank premises (at cost to the bank)” doesn’t, for
instance, look as though the last possible word had been
said on the subject of writing down. And the return on
shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves) is low enough
(considering the size of the deposits) to support that
view :

But if on its books the bank still has over-valued assets,
the public’s position is comfortable enough, the liquid
assets representing now about 43 per cent, of all public
liabilities ; while all non-liquid assets might shrink in
value 24 per cent, and still leave the public’s money
intact. The position certainly has not been visibly
strengthened as it ought to have been during the
past five prosperous years, chiefly because the
lay-by has been far too small, proportionately. In
the half-year after the last locked-up deposits were
got rid of in 1901, the shareholders’ funds represented
nearly 26 per cent, of the non-liquid assets (in other
words, those assets could shrink 26 per cent, and leave
the public money intact) ; the figure for to-day, as shown
above, is 24 per cent. From the depositors’ point of
view, the movement, such as there has been, has been
the wrong way; but still the National seems to offer
ample margin of safety.

How much has been written off for depreciation and
charged to revenue is not disclosed. Improvements have
recently been made at the refineries, and other works are
constantly in progress ; yet refineries, mills, lands &e
are in the balance-sheet at £2,461,999 as against £2 474 165
as at September, 1905, and £2,561,231 as at March, 1903.
Possibly some few farms have been sold meantime’; but
they don’t amount to anything worth mentioning. That
means therefore that more than enough is now being
written off and charged to current revenue to pay for all
the additions and improvements and purchases
not to mention the heavy law expenses which
the co. has incurred in losing a fight (which
didn’t end till it reached the Privy Council)
against the Commonwealth. For practical purposes it
isn’t therefore much use working it out that the earnings
on shareholders’ funds are at the rate of £8 11s. 3d. per
cent, per annum, because it is a mere guess what the real
earnings are. More or less apropos this, chairman Kater
M.L.C., whose remarks were not Quite as mournful as
usual, read out at the meeting some figures to disprove
that the co. consists of a few fat monopolists. He showed
that there were over 1300 separate holdings of shares
700 of them in the interests of widows, children, and such
like; professional men (113) owned £120,000 of stock-
business men (375) owned £305,000 ; and the Fat Man (05)
the capitalist not m a trade or a profession—owned
only 23 per cent, of the stock. What all this proves
isn t clear. Most times the widow and the orphan
don’t, admittedly, make much out of the monopoly.
No 10 per cent, comes their way. They buy £2O shares
for £5O, and get a return of 4 per cent. The co. is then
expected to earn a fair 4 per cent., not only on the £“>o
which it has received, but also on the £3O which some
speculator has received ; and, squeezed itself, it squeezes
the farmers, &c. That is where dealing' with the State
has the advantage. The State sinks a capital of £1,000,000
in an undertaking ; and even if it succeeds wonderfully, the
amount on which it attempts to earn interest remains at
£1,000,000 —it isn’t forced up to £3,000,000. The people
who put in the million get all the interest they bargained
for ; while the State’s customers (the people who have to
provide the interest) get the advantage that in the other
case flows to the speculator in shares. It may be owned
up at once that the private concern is often better
managed than the public concern, though there is no
reason why it should be ; but even so the public which
the private concern serves doesn’t get the advantage.

“ After making provision for bad and doubtful debts
and depreciation,” £5OOO per half-year is appropriated
for “ depreciation of plant and machinery.” How much
the former provision amounts to can’t be said —
the published profit and loss account is a mere
skeleton. It is probably something substantial, though.
Tooth’s, according to common belief, have added more
than a trifle to their holding of properties during the past
few years ; certainly they have made large additions to
plant. Yet 10 years ago brewery, plant, freeholds, and
goodwill were valued for book-keeping purposes at
£619,946, and to-day at £631,167, or only £11,211 more.
Six years ago the item stood at £647,831. Making the
same appropriation for depreciation in the two years, it
is found that in 1895-6 the earnings showed £6 15s. 4d.
per cent, on the whole assets at the middle of the year ;
in 1905-6 they showed £7 9s. 6d. per cent. Moreover, a
larger proportion of the assets is at present in public
securities, &c., which give only a low return. Possibly
brewing is a more profitable business now than it was 10
years ago, though it isn’t supposed to be. A more likely
explanation of the improved rate of profits is that a pro-
fitable internal reserve has been built up through the
severity of the writings-down. But it is Impossible to
say for sure whether the same severity is now being
practised. Some other details :

this co. is accumulating something handsome to console
itself with. “ Sundry debtors ”is still a heavy item, but
has been reduced a trifle during the last three years—
another satisfactory feature of accounts which, in fact,
passing over their scrappiness, don’t present features of
any other sort.

In addition to the reserves shown above there is a “Re-
pairs suspense and contingency account,” £2500. Of the'
other reserves £7921 is insurance, and that isn’t the most
sure sort of reserve, if it is a true reserve at all. Apart
from the amounts added to this insurance fund, and
necessarily reckoned above as profits, the profits for the
past 12 months have been £4728, and the dividends have
been £5228, or just £5OO more. It is clear, therefore,
that dividends are being paid out of the fund which is
supposed to be put aside to cover losses by shipwreck,
&c. That being so, the 7 per cent. div. is excessive. Before
the capital was increased some time ago, the 8f per cent,
div. had been barely earned for some years; and a divi-
dend in the same proportion on the increased capital would
amount to well under 6 per cent. As no new capital
was brought in, and as the old rate of dividend was barely
earned, and as the writings-down presumably had not
been excessive (anyhow, it was found necessary to have
some special writing-down), it seems clear enough that
even a 6 per cent. div. would have been too much for the
re-constructed co. to begin with. Certainly it is possible
that the co. is writing-off so much for depreciation that
it is establishing a hidden reserve—-in which case the 7 per
cent, dividend, would not be so objectionable. But it
isn’t likely. Why should a co. write off more than a fair
thing when by so doing it reduces its visible profits'below
the amount necessary to pay its regular dividend? The
co.’s financial position is strong; besides ship and free-
hold and leasehold property, it has £44,303 in public
securities, deposits, and cash balances ; its ship property
(completed) amounts to only £39,800. It isn’t surprising
that the co. seems unable to rightly pay the present 7 per
cent, div., seeing that more than half the assets areearn-
ng an average of only perhaps 3J per cent.

Something over 12 months ago the society had a cleaning-
up, and wrote down its assets, consisting mainly of ad-
vances, by £10,380. The immediate result of this piece
of honesty was, as might have been expected, a consider-
able withdrawal of deposits ; in 1903 they totalled
£73,700, and in 1905 they were down to £57.903,
And one result of this again was that a com-
fortable credit balance at the bank was changed
into an overdraft of £1768 ; but certain fixed deposits re-
mained at £14,926. During the past 12 months, though
in Brisbane there must have been many calls for advances,
the society appears to have set itself‘the task of wholly
restoring confidence. The overdraft, therefore; has beeii
wiped out and a credit of £1596 established ; the loan
which was contracted a few years ago to build new pre-
mises has been reduced to £2400 ; and the fixed deposits
have been increased from £14,926 to £16,641. Twelve
months ago money held on deposit was down to £57,903 ;
it is on the up-grade again, and the total stands at £61,431.
On the other hand, with a view presumably to strengthen-
ing the position, the advances have been reduced from
£122,254 to £120,617. The interest earnings show
within a small fraction of 6 per cent. _on
the average of total assets. For a building society
whose assets are nearly all advances (£'20,617 out of
£146,350) the rate is by no means high ; the advances
themselves appear to earn 7 per cent, on the average.
But the deposits carry 4 per cent. ; and, as the manage-
ment costs are about li per cent., the margin for profit
and bad debts is not large. No doubt through proper
salary increments, the expenses have been slowly mount-
ing up while the advances have been steadily slipping
down ; in 1901 the advances were £147,921 and the ex-
penses £l7lO, while now the advances are £120,616 and
the expenses £1917. The situation seems to call either
for more business or less expenditure.

So, reckoning in with the expenditure the money spent
on development, the half-year shows a loss of £494. The
co. seems now, however, to have about readied the turn.
It has had a lot of bad luck. First, it didn’t get
quite all the capital it should have had. Of
80,000 shares only 42,573 were got off. That
meant, among other things, the hiring of plant,
which is a costlv business at the best of times.
With the help of £lO,OOO of debentures (nearly all held by

and kept the largest interest in it) the co. might never-
theless have pulled through if it hadn’t been for a fire
which broke out in the north side workings in February
last year. That not only cut down the output when a busi-
ness was being worked up, but cost money for sealing,
and for development which could otherwise have been
deferred. Four or five months ago matters reached more
or less of a climax, and to save the situation 25,000 pref.
shares had to be created; practically the whole of these
have been issued, except 4000, which are in reserve to meet
£4OOO of debentures due early next year. Six months
ago the co. owed (including debentures) £21,431 ; as the
result of the new share issue it has added £7450 to its
Elan t (doing away with the hiring), and its debts have
een reduced to £10,782, including the £4OOO of deben-
tures before mentioned. It isn’t such a fair start as could
be wished ; but as the sealed workings are being gradually
opened out, and developmentis finished, the co., handling
a coal of excellent quality, should be able to pull through.
But in the matter of the old ordinary shares, which are the
only ones on the market at present, the man who may
feel inclined to look that way will find it of interest to
first read the articles and consider the privileges attached
to the pref. shares.
* * * *
Mount Morgan (Q.) is now fairly going as a
copper producer. It had the great advantage of having
its furnaces, etc., designed by R. Shepherd, who has had
a wider Australian experience in this class of w T ork than
probably any other man, and, though an engineer to
begin with, can now be reckoned also among the front-
rank Australian copper smelters. The first furnace was
blown in towards the end of January, and the adjust-
ments w'ere practically complete and a smooth run
obtained before the end of March. An output of from
320 to 360 tons of blister copper per month can now be
maintained. There are three furnaces, each with a
capacity of 5000 tons a month, and it is already safe to
say that that tonnage will be exceeded. Only two of
them will, however, be kept in blast; the third will
be a stand-by—for the present, anyhow. As to
costs, nothing has, of course, been yet made known ;
but, as nearly as can be judged, they will pan out
at from 255. to 275. per ton over all, of which
mining and delivery will account for a trifle over one-
third. This would bring out the cost of producing blister
copper (charging nothing for the gold contents) at about
£44 per ton. At Mt. Lyell (Tas.), whose blister copper
output last half-year for which accounts have been issued
was 4306 tons, or just about double the expectation from
Mt. Morgan, the costs (mining, smelting, and converting)
ran to £32 2s. 6d. per ton. At Australia’s other big copper
producer, owned by a public co., the Wallaroo and
Moonta (S.A.), the mining, dressing, and smelting costs
ran to £59 19s. 4d. per ton of fine copper. But the Wal-
laroo and Moonta's costs for mining and dressing are, com-
paratively, very heavy, being two and a half times
greater than the Bmelting costs. At Mt. Lyell,
mining costs only ss. 2d. per ton; smelting and
converting 7s. 2d.—a total of 12s. 4d. per ton. As
to values, since the present Lyell co. started
(Aug., 1903) the average value of the ore has been—
copper 2’35 per cent., silver l - 840z., and gold a minute
fraction over ldwt. Mt. Morgan copper ore available
is estimated at 1,100,000 tons, containing 3J per cent,
copper and Bdwt. gold. At the rate now provided for,
this ore would last about nine years.
* * * *
With copper at £GO, and a recovery of 3 per
cent, of that metal and 6dwt. of gold, Mt. Morgan
should, from its new works, show about £12,000 a month to
cover refining, realisation, depreciation, etc., and provide
profits. Every £1 per ton for copper above that should
mean, of course, an additional £320 to £360 per
month.
* * * *
Mt. Morgan new works are, so far as one can
judge now, a credit both to the men who designed them
and the mostly-Queensland firms that supplied them.
They are a model of ; compactness. The blast furnaces
are the central feature in a_ building 350yds. long. On
one side are the blowers; on the otliertlie converters. The
bin accommodation runs to 10,000 ton#, and the sampling
is automatic. A feature of the furnaces is the vaporiser,
designed mainly to economise water—a matter of import-
ance at Mt. Morgan, where they know what it is to have
to cart water by train. The blowers are worked (as most
machinery is now at the Mount) by electric motors,. The
matte (copper, iron and sulphur) is passed into a furnace-
well, with the possible addition of a little of the most
silicious ore. The electric crane is the striking feature
of the converter house. It works a travelling ladle, with
which the matte is taken from the furnace-well and
emptied into boiler-plate Bessemerising drums, Bft. by
6ft., with a 2ft. lining of silica, to slag out the iron, which
is tilted out, and a blast of air is forced through the mass
in the drums to get rid of the sulphur. The copper (and
gold) are finally poured off, and the silica lining is re-
placed as it is lost—a process in which the crane is again
an economiser. The fumes pass off in a chamber, with a
rise of 115 ft., and the stack rises another 250 ft. A furnace
which belches sulphur fumes has its disadvantages ; but
Mt. Morgan 00. has done more than most owners to get
rid of the worst of the nuisance.
* * * *
O.K. copper mine (N.Q.) is still busy on its new
converter plant, which ought to be at work some time
next month. The job has been much delayed through
want of bricks, due to the wet season. Meantime the
other furnaces are closed down, as there is a large
accumulation of matte already awaiting the converter-
say, £40,000 worth ; and the co. has also a thousand or
two cash in hand.
Opinion is divided as to what the O.K. Co.’s
policy should be pending the building of the badly-needed
railway ; but there is no doubt in the directors’ minds—
they ought to go straight ahead, and turn out. all the
metal they can while the market is up where it is ; and
this they will do. As for the railway, nothing has been
decided as to the route or the means by which the line is
to be paid for. There are two proposals as to both things.
As to route, a line could be laid about 45 miles to Mun-
gana, on the Ohillagoe Co.’s line, which would mean a
fine lot of traffic for the Ohillagoe Co. ; or it
could be laid about twice the distance to Biboohra,
which would join the Cairns line below the
Ohillagoe Co.’s terminus. The Biboohra survey is
not finished yet; but as far as it has gone no
great difficulties have been struck ; moreover, this line
would run alongside the Mt. Molloy, which is a- good deal
in its favor. Nevertheless, if the Ohillagoe Co. comes
along with any reasonable freight proposals, there is
little doubt that the Mungana route will be adopted. As
for the means, the co. will soon be a big profit-earner
again, and it is most likely that a good part- of
the money will be put into the line; the accumu-
lated matte will be a large help in this way.
The immediate effect of this will be to depress the
market for the shares ; but in the end it will probably be
better than to issue enough new shares to build the line.

The starting of the desulphurising process which
the Chillagoe Co. (N.Q.) is installing wili re-open a few
more mines in that district; for hitherto, when silver-lead
sulphides have been struck, there has been no option but
to quit, as the Chillagoe Co. hasn’t been a buyer of sul-
phides. The Kuddigore will at once re-open now that
this has been changed. Kuntze, formerly of Tasmania,
being now in charge of the co.’s metallurgical depart-
ment the Huntingdon-Heberlein desulphurising process
has of course, been adopted—which, boon as it has been
to Australia, is a pity, seeing that in the Carmichael-
Bradford process Australia has a method of its own,
proved capable of doing at least equal work.
Ravenswood Deep Mines, Ltd. (N.Q.), which is
mainly Bull, and nearly all of the Cohen branch of the
family, tapped Premier Kidston for a prospecting vote
durin"- his recent northern tour. Lawrence Wilson
urged°that the co. had spent £25,000 in development; and
Kidston, confronted with the surface Work, was im-
pressed. Wilson, however, did not stop to explain how
much of the £25,000 had gone in buildings and plant,
so his modest request for a State advance of £5OOO or
£lO,OOO fell on sympathetic ears. Kidston’s sympathy is
all right. He believes in State organisation and State-
aid and such Socialistic things. But how can the share-
holders square the request with their anti-Socialism ?
At latest report water in Bennion’sshaft, Croydon
(N.Q.), had been lowered to within a fathom of the reef.
Early this month, therefore, some work should be done
on the stone. Efforts to overcome the water difficulty
have been so prolonged that many of the local theorists
have ceased to take a breathless interest in the discovery.
. * *
Glanmire Reserve Tribute, Gympie (Q.), had a
shareholders’meeting' for a novel purpose on Thursday
evening, 48,000 shares being represented out of 60,000.
The meeting was called (1) so that directors might explain
why the miners’ wages had been reduced, and (2) to con-
sider changes in the board. Directors replied that the
reductions were made because other companies had made
them. As that was all they had to say, the meeting
ordered the wages to be raised to the original rate of
£2 10s. per week. Then it was moved that one director
be removed from the board. This was done ; and Gym-
pie’s senior M.L.A., who had been the moving spirit in
the meeting, was elected in his stead. This show some
time back looked rather promising, with a reef formation
of 3ft. to 4ft., carrying fair gold ; but more recently the
discovery has not come up to expectations.
The Commonwealth Oil Corporation, Ltd., has
got another inspired column in the papers ; if it can’t
produce oil, it can turn out gas at any rate. “ The pur-
chase, concluded this (last) week, of the properties and
stocks of the N.S.W. Shale and Oil Co. gives to the pur-
chasing co. the basis for an immediate commencement of
operations.” Then follow particulars to show what a
great and profitable concern the N.S.W. Shale and Oil Co.
has been : “ The purchase price was £35,000, the Oil Cor-
poration taking over all stocks at cost as at March 31,
from which date the properties have been worked as for
the Oil Corporation. The New South Wales Shale and
Oil Company has won aggregate profits of £330,000, ex-
tending over a period of 32 years. Until 1902 those profits
were earned by Hartley Vale shales, which then became
exhausted ; the profits derived during 1904-s—roughly,
£lO,OOO per annum —were earned from the Torbane mines,
west of Capertee.” Now, who is pulling the public’s leg in
a scandalous manner—in plain Australian, who is shame-
lessly lying to the public, and what is the object V Anyway,
here are the facts. From June, 1903, to June, 1904, the
N.S.W. Shale and Oil Co. made, according to its own pub-
lished accounts, a gross profit of £3827, of which £3OOO was
written off for wear and tear, leaving a net profit of £827.
From June, 1904, to June, 1905, the co. made a gross pro-
fit of £5172, and £6OOO was written off for depreciation,
leaving a net loss of £B2B. Therefore, for the past two
years (to June 30, 1905), the gross profit was £8999, or an
average of £4499 per annum, and the net result was a
loss of £l. Yet in the interests of this huge London
blister, the Commonwealth Od Corporation, Ltd. —it is
printed in Sydney, where the facts could easily be ascer-
tained, that “the profits derived during 1904-5 were,
roughly, £lO,OOO per annum.”
The Bulletin has previously stated the history
of this Commonwealth Oil Corporation (capital £800,000),
and has proved its utter hopelessness in the conditions
under which it proposes to work; and its general
character can most likely be judged from the foregoing.
The figures and statement made in general regarding it
have been just about as accurate as those pointed out
above. And its conclusions have been just as danger-
ous. Consider the remark —“The N.S.W. Shale
and Oil Co. has won aggregate profits of £330,000
extending over a period of 32 years.” That
statement doesn’t hint that in the past six years, during
which it has been continuously at work, with the ex-
perience of over a quarter of a century behind it, instead of
making one farthing profit, it has made a net loss of £904.
What the co. was alile to do years ago isn’t of much con-
sequence ; its profits (though the figures quoted are
probably wrong) only prove that the co. knewits business
and could make money out of shale while the shale and
the market lasted. What it hasn’t been able to do for
six years past is of vastly more consequence.. And that
damning fact isn’t mentioned. Again it is mentioned
that the purchase price is £35,000. The paid-up
capital of the co. is £85,000. But at June 30
last the co. owed £36,147. Possibly that has since
been reduced a trifle. Clearly, then, though the pur-
chase price is £35,000, if the oo.pays 20s. in the £ it
won’t have a penny left for itself. In other words, share-
holders lose their whole capital of £85,000. And that is
the concern upon the strength of which the Common
wealth Oil Corporation, Ltd. (capital £800,000), is
boomed, and its purchase “ gives to the purchasing
co. the basis for an immediate commencement of opera-
tions ! ” Gradually, no doubt, shares —boomed on the
strength of such yarns as these—will be let out to J.
Bull. Later on, when J. Bull finds out things, he will
say more things about Australia. And for its almost
universal silence on the subject, Australia will almost
deserve what the angry Bull will say.
* » * *
After water had been pouring into the northern
Broken Hill mines for over a week, some Sydney share-
holders, pricked by Palmer and Sons, woke up,
held a meeting, and asked, among other things, if
the flooding was necessary. So they proved over
again that the capacity of Sydney mining specula-
tors for waking up after the ’bus has left is amazing.
The meeting decided, however, to be there at the start of
something, anyhow. It had heard rumors about an
amalgamation with the Junction, and it decided that the
Junction North “ should be worked as a separate pro-
perty by the present co., which is possessed of the neces-
sary money to make any repairs required to the present
shaft, or even to construct a new shaft, if necessary,
the mill appliances being in a highly satisfactory
state, and the ore bodies disclosed being of large
dimensions and high value.” Money, highly-satisfactory
mill, big and rich lodes—what more do shareholders want?
Dividends, perhaps. But don’t mention such things.
Just 20 years ago the Junction North was taken up, Zeb.
Lane being its first barracker. Off and on it has been
worked ever since, never being wholly neglected ; and all
it has done in the way of dividends in those 20 years was
4s. per share, paid in 1896 and 1897, out of a bunch of rich
ore, which has now been worked out. Not a cent has been
paid in the past nine years, though during that period
there have been two metal booms. The process (mag-
netic), on the strength of which the shares were lifted out
of the mud 12 months ago and made popular, failed ; yet
the stock did not fall back into the mud again. . Possibly
it didn’t deserve to fall back to quite where it had been ;
for ore developments have been satisfactory, and even
without the magnets the mill looks as though it should
make profits. But, on its record, Junction North share-
holders are not entitled to hold their tails quite as high
as they did at this Sydney meeting.

Part of the market had been expecting a half-
crown div. from the Broken Hill Prop. ; but the usual
quarterly eighteenpence comes instead, and the balance
can be debited to the fire. If the mine can’t pay more
than eighteenpence, it is overvalued at even 725. Six
shillings a year—-that means a return of S.\ per cent,
only ; and 8£ per cent, isn’t good enough when the eo.
has to swallow itself in order to earn it, leaving finally
nothing but a name and a hole in the ground. And the
market can’t be kept sky-high for ever on the strength
of what the zinc is going to do. The co. is treating
already about 5000 tons of zinc material a week ; and
even if, at some time or other, more may be put through
and a better recovery made, that is likely to be offset by
a weaker zinc market, for the present prices are quite
abnormal.

Pinnacles mine, Broken Hill, despite appar-
ently reliable reports to the contrary, is still in statu quo.
Twice during the past two years it has been announced
that a newly-formed London co. would take over
the mine, and erect a concentrating mill to satis-
factorily deal with the ore. The new co. has not
loomed up yet, however, and a valuable ore body
is having its eyes picked out in the interests of the
Queensland Smelting Co. The hand-dressed ore no
longer goes to Aldershot; it is being sold to the B.H.
Prop. Co. Meanwhile, mine-development is suspended,
only enough men being employed to comply with labor
conditions. Recently there was registered in Sydney a
mortgage of the Pinnacles mine from the Queensland
Smelting Co. (which is an English concern) to Hon. N. C.
Rothschild. This does not look as though a new co. were
insight. # „ . *
Expected that the big deal over Great Cobar
will be settled up within a fortnight or so from now. Also,
it is said to be unlikely that the Cobar Gold Mines will be
included in the pending transaction. The co. didn’t feel
inclined to take the 10s. a share or so offered. Expert
Rolker has been commissioned to examine mine and
report as to best means of treatment.
Result of the assay of borings from the C.S.A.
has not outwardly shaken the new-found faith of Cobar
shareholders ; they say, “ Wait till the shaft goes down ”
and pass remarks about drills, which would have been
very well-timed if they have been passed when bores
were first mentioned. But to pretend that the bores have
not disappointed them is rubbish; if they had had a
ghost of an idea that the drills would have given these
results, the drills would never have gone down. Some
barrackers talk now as if bores were always misleading.
Well, that is comforting, but it isn't true. When Mt.
Morgan (Q.) found out that it had copper in the bottom,
manager Richards was worried by the drilling people ;
and with their concurrence a test was agreed
upon. Bores w'ere put down in a _ straight line,
then a drive was run through in order to
ascertain both the deviation of the bores and the
real value of the drillings. The drive proved the varia-
tion immaterial, and an average of the drillings gave
practically the same results as the sampling of the drive.
On that result Mt. Morgan was satisfied to go in for
wholesale boring ; and, by the way, it was to a great ex-
tent that experience at Mt. Morgan which lately decided
the O.K. copper people, in North Queensland, to presently
go underfoot with bores. No doubt the bores at C.S. A.
can’t be accepted as conclusive ; the mine still has a faint
hope of holding other rich shoots ; but to pooh-pooh the
bores altogether is ridiculous.
Shareholders in Queen Bee, Cobar (N.S.W.), by
a majority of 33,220 votes, vetoed the proposed sale to an
English co. on the terms offered. Directors, on this
decision, will now set to work to get in more plant and
increase the output. Bee South, a syndicate-owned
show with gambling chances, has lately reached encourag-
ing country ; and its shares have jumped. Queen Bee
North has got on to the market—a little float, 120 shares
of £l5 each. It is a long shot, but the locality is popular,
and that will probably pull the float through. Unfortu-
nately, popularity doesn’t pay mining dividends, though.
The purchase monev (£1750) for three blocks
south of C.S.A. Central (N.S.W.) has been divided among
the first little syndicate. The deal was contracted for in
December last; Frank Gardner is supposed to be the
most interested party.

' Will some good soul please put the stopper on
Vic Geologist Dunn before he does any real harm ? His
fads over saddle formations existing from the Grampians
to Cape Borda can be passed by ; but when he scampers
out in the following strain, because a single lot of quartz
taken from the formation at 4524 ft. in the New
Chum Railway Mine, Bendigo, went an ounce to
the ton, it is time for some cold reasoning
to come in “ This remarkable specimen of auriferous
quartz is evidence of the vast stores of wealth in gold
possessed by the State. It is a guarantee of the stability
and progressive prosperity of Bendigo that such rich ore
exists at such great depths. The winning of such ore
from a vertical depth of over three-quarters of a mile from
the surface is an achievement of which Bendigo miners
maybe j usoly proud. To the last sentiment everyone takes
off his hat. But when a scientist and a practical mining
man writes that a small crushing provides evidence of the
stability and progressive prosperity of a field, then—rats !
Prudence should have suggested waiting till the New
Chum Railwav “ saddle ” had been driven on, and proved
to be of extent and payable. Even when that is done, it
is not irrefragable testimony in the direction he indicates.
One mine does not make a field. Good as Bendigo is,
there is no need for it to go about with a swelled head, in-
duced by metropolitan press puffing, and Dunn’s
“ illimitable ” spread-eagleism. Let anyone who wants
to establish the fact that Bendigo so far has
given very profitable returns below 3000 ft. take the
mines that have sunk beyond that depth, and see what
they have paid in divs. Let him also work out the cost
of getting the gold won below that level, and he will have
something that will effectively-dispel the least tendency
to such frothiness as Geologist Dunn’s.
Alex. Jobson, who was formerly the A.M.P.’s
actuary in Melbourne, and, later on, the Equitable’s
actuary for Australasia, has, upon the scattering of the
Equitable’s staff, gone into business as public accountant,
auditor and actuary in Sydney ; and The Bulletin doesn’t
know anybody who is better entitled, by training, ex-
perience and natural bent, to hang out a signboard to
that effect.

Mining co. balance-sheets are strange fish. The Wickham
and Bullock Island Coal Co. (N.S.W.) keeps a worked-out
and abandoned mine in its balance-sheet as an asset
against the figures £Bl,OOO ; the Long Tunnel Co which
five years ago took over a mine that had paid
in di'vs. and looked good for a fine lump more after some
more development had been done, and which last half-
year made a profit of £16,554, or £11,171 after paying all
capital charges—this col has its mine and plant (apart
from electric pumping plant) in its balance-sheet at only
£4389. As to its liquid assets the Long Tunnel has im-
proved this way:—March, 1905, net overdraft, £3902;
September, 1906, credit, £1153 ; March, 1906 (before pay-
ing the dividend), credit, £13,860, of which £1024 was put
aside to wipe out the balance due on the pumping plant
contract.
The Long Tunnel manager’s report of stone
crushed gives instructive data, as it illustrates what a
bump-up was given to the finances through the last
crushing of 2145 tons, averaging over 22dwt. The highest
point touched before that was 16dwt. in February,
while for the first three months in the half-
year the dirt went only 9dwt. The improvement
has come from the upper stopes; but the old
practice of relying on dish prospects makes it impossible
to say if the increased yield really represents the average
of the stone in sight, or is only a rich leavening. There
is a delightful piece of disingenuousness about the state-
ment of the stone in sight. What is said is : “ A total of
10 972 tons of quartz has been added to that developed,
without calculating any of the stone above the
1900 ft. level.” If this means that the co. has only
10,972 tons in its southern ground, apart from
that above 1900 ft., there is not much to justify shares
being at £7 ; for with the battery crushing 2000 tons each
month, there is in that case less than six months’ supply
in sight. In six months the co. will probably have got to
the north-west stone through the 2625 ft. crosscut. Accord-
ing to the old estimate, there is about 60,000 tons of
9dwt. stone in the stopes there. These latter figures
must, however, be taken with some reservation, as the
crush’ings prior to work being stopped had gone down
gradually in value. Therefore it is not wise to count on
much margin of profit coming from that quarter. Never-
theless, throwing the stone in, there is nothing like ten
years' purchase in sight, although, that is the market
rating of the co.’s shares. Considering that the co. has
only just got back to divs., it was rather a grab on the
part of the board to accept a jump-up in fees from £2OO
to £5OO without shareholders having prior notice.

Duke of York, Meredith (Vic.), looks as sickly
and as sad as another Duke of York used to do. Like that
other one, too, it had a vigorous youth, and early reached
manhood. Then it had a little scandal over the famous
“corner.” Both are alike in the sense that they are
supported by the money of the State but the human
Duke of York is thrifty, and, no doubt, has a bit in the
bank • whereas the other Duke of York can only show
a balance ,on the wrong side. It is a very solemn fact
that the fashion of naming mines after royalty or the
“aristocracy” has been punished. At Meredith, from
the Duke of York and the Duke of Wellington down,
there isn’t a dividend-payer. At Maryborough the
various Duke shows are as feeble as the Lord Brassey
mines at Newstead ; and the Prince of Wales group, at
Bendigo, despite a little spurt, have never put up much
of a ...
East Eingall, Day Dawn (W.A.), was much
boomed some months ago, and gaudy reports were pub-
lished about alleged developments. On the strength of
these varns, it was stated that the 241,585 reserve shares
had been placed at par, which meant £60,000 odd, and that
the co. was in a very strong position financially. In
November last crosscutting was started at 500 ft: to find
the reef which in the shaft at 413 ft. was said to be worth
61dwt. lOgr. for a width of Sft. Since then they have kept
on groping in the dark, but nothing better than ldwt. to
2dwt ore has been found, except in a miserable leader
6in. wide. The people who run the show are also in-
terested in the Murchison Associated, a wild cat of many
years’ standing, and about which glowing reports periodi-
cally appear. Anyone who loves his money will keep off
both of these.

Cosmopolitan, Kookynie (W. A.), seems doomed.
During March the value of the ore treated was only
21s. 7d., and costs panned out at 21s. 9d. per ton, leaving
a loss of 2d. per ton, or in all £5O. For months past only
a few pence have separated profit from loss, but the lower
levels have proved so poor that co. appears to be beyond
salvation. For some inscrutable reason 8., M. and Co.
have not acquired a fresh property to take the place of
the present derelict, as was done in the case of the Oroya,
which is now pinning its faith to the Oroya Junior at
Black Range.
' *
The March yield of Westralia Waihi, Davy-
hurst was 1010 tons for 4120 z. bullion, worth £1242, or
only £3 per oz. On this basis the ore averaged 245. 7d.
per ton, and costs 355. 7d., thus registering a loss of
11s per ton, or £570. Co. has now been in existence five
years and has merely drained the pockets of shareholders,
and seems likely to continue to do so. The lenses of ore
are so short and erratic, and so liable to cut out at any
time that it is impossible to count on them, and the
country is so hard that working is very expensive.
Manager Watt did fairly well, but, as he gave it best, it
is hardly likely that his successor, Davenport Cleland,
who jogged along complacently at Bayley’s, will set the
Thames on fire.

Very little attention is devoted to mining news
by the Perth (W.A.) newspapers. In their issues of
16/4/’O6 the morning papers had just 12 times as much
turf news as mining - , which suggests to outsiders that
horse-racing is of very much more importance than the
goldfields. The West Australian published IS mining
items, all but a couple being reprinted from outside
papers some of them more than a month old. One of
the two allegedly original items was a message con-
cerning a Kalgoorlie mine, cabled to the London directors
six weeks before; and this ancient scrap of dead
intelligence was also reproduced in the Herald. The
latter paper had a stale article from a London financial
publication, and only three pars under “ Mining.” The
proprietors of both papers never did take kindly to
mining, which they still regard as something to be
viewed with suspicion. A big haul of turnips or a hefty
catch of cabbages, or even an unusually large c od of
earth, is much greater in their eyes than all the
dazzling wealth of the Golden Mile.
Blue Tier (Tas.), which was to restore Mt. Lyell
to all its former glory, was evidently worked out during
the Commission that sat in Hobart to find out who ought
to have it, as it, so far, only employs Luke Williams, two
men and a boy.

Hobart (Tas.) proposes to do- something more
for the tourist whom it mentions in its prayers in the
place where most men mention their daily bread. The
something more is a railway up Mount Wellington—the
mountain whose top can only be reached at present by
an effort that is beyond the fat tourist with the most
money. Between throwing Bills upstairs and downstairs
and finally into the dust-bin, the Tas. Parliament last
session gave a gentleman named Wertheimer a con-
cession, and the gentleman named Wertheimer is now
promoting.a co. with a paid-up capital of £13,125, ana
striving to show how, charging 3s. 6d. for the ride up and
down, shareholders will make 9 per cent. Most pro-
moters show how anything from 67j to 219 per cent, can
be made, and his 9 per cent, is something to the credit of
Wertheimer and his scheme, anyhow. Otherwise it
doesn’t look too captivating.

Queensland doing- business in soft woods with Westralia

stock lots brought as ballast from Europe and Amenca ( •
21/4/’O6). The inter-State freights at present asked for are
about 6s. per 100 ft.! But Director of Forests MacMahon has
hopes of such concessions, combined with an mcreasea
duty on imported woods, that Queensland will be able t
supply all the wants of Westralia and every other Aus-
tralian State which has now got the habit of looking
abroad for soft-wood supplies. If the Australian Parlia-
ment can’t And time to raise the tariff, Queensland sees
the alternative of helping the timber people by wiping
out the harbor dues, as it did lately with coal cargoes
of 500 tons and over. This plan is working very well, and
Kidston is the man to extend an idea when he sees the
State getting an advantage. „
One of Victoria’s under-valued shires—Upper
Yarra—is just now struggling to get away from the anti-
cipated bailiff. A contractor lately broke into a council
meeting, and remarked that the council’s cheque for £iO
had been dishonored, and that he would like to hear
reasons why the municipal fathers should not be g a °l e< *
for passing valueless cheques. Investigation showed that
this precious shire council had drawn cheques for £L>9,
to meet which there was only £3O in the bank ; that the
overdraft had reached the legal limit, and that nothing
more would come in till June or thereabouts. It was re-
solved to go down to the pawnshop and plead earnestly
for another loan. Yet this shire values itself at less than
6Ad. per aore ; it already has loans of £9OB, besides the
bank overdraft, and it levies on itself the ponderous rate
of Is. 2d. in the £l—equal probably to about 2d. on the
honest value. _ .

Aerial tramways or conveyors undoubtedly
afford the cheapest means of transport, especially when
thev are worked under a system which is entirely
automatic. The largest manufacturers in the world in
this particular line are Adolf Bleichert and Co., of London,
represented in Australia by W. and J. Lempriere, of Mel-
bourne. Delivering ore automatically from the mine to
the mill over almost any distance at a cost of about one
penny per ton, is far ahead of all other means of transport.
Automatic systems are in vogue for the cheap discharge
of tailings. Automatic weighing and counting of loaded
cars is also assured under Bleichert’s system, thereby
nothing is left to assumption or imagination^

provements in the Concentrating art. N. Guthridge, Ltd.

It is interesting to note that the Hornsby
patent safety oil engine has sustained its world-wide
reputation for reliability and economy. During the past
year hundreds of squatters, recognising their adaptability,
have been using them for their shearing and general
motive power, and as they are specially suitable
mining, electric lighting, pumping, etc., mine managers
have shown their appreciation by installing them on some
of the leading mines. The latest type of engine will run
at a cost ot less than a farthing per brake horse power per
hour, and are manufactured by the well-known
“ Upright ” Water Tube Boiler firm R. Hornsby and Sons,
Ltd Hay-street, Sydney and Spencer-street, Melbourne.

Important Notice to Mine Managers and Users
of Concentrators, Wilfley Concentrate Tables. The Wi -
fley patent has not been revoked ; the patent remains m
full force and effect. Judgment , was given against the
Wilfley Syndicate by the High Court, on the ground that
the invention had been anticipated in \ictoria by a pubh-,
cation circulating in the State of Victoria but they upheld
the decision of the Supreme Court that the Card Table
was an infringement; the Wilfley patents in other Aus-
tralian States are not affected in any degree whatever.
The Wilfley Syndicate is appealing to the Privy Council to
have the decision of the High Court upset, and lead-
ing patent'counsel have already been engaged to argue
the case in England. Mine Managers and others are
warned that actions will be taken against all infnn B ers
of the Wilfley patents, whether they be users or manu-
facturers.—The Austral Otis Engineering Co., Ltd.

Aboriginalities.
“Nulla” : There is a plant with a small
greenish white flower, something like clover,
which, without such adventitious aids as varying
degrees of deadness, ranks high in the category of
smells. It “fumes” in the spring, along creeks
in the near West, and is, in my opinion, responsible
for many haunted camps, cattle stampedes, etc.
We had a cattle dog which accidentally bumped the
odor one day. He was not delicate, and used to
collect natural history specimens, etc., but this
was too much. He bit the air fiercely three times,
and then retired to disinfect himself on the car-
case of a wallaby. I have heard different names
for the plant, but I’m not sure as to their scientific
accuracy.

‘ ‘ Dik Thawne ” : In haste I write from
Hobart to nominate a goanna for the light-weight
stink stakes. Also to urge that “San Remo’s”
whale be disqualified. Fancy running a whale
against a carpet snake and a goanna ! Let “ San
Remo ” tackle something his own size or else com-
plete in the heavy-weight class. I also claim for
my goanna (if this is a handicap event) a few
yards start, because “Flabby’s” carpet snake was
dead but my goanna is alive. You can’t expect a
live reptile to start off the same mark as a dead
one. Nevertheless if you won’t give my goanna a
handicap I shall still start him. But if “San
Remo’s” whale is allowed to stand in I will enter
our Upper House and he won’t have a possible.
But my goanna starts anyhow, and I can find a
man to back him up in the contest if he needs it.
What is the first prize for this competition, and
when do nominations close? Mind you keep that
whale out or I will send along the Tasmanian
Upper House, and the deficient postage and
fumigation fees you will have to pay will break
The Bulletin’s record.

“ Haloh ” ; Have known aborigines suffer
from appendicitis long before King Ned received
tiie patent rights. On Thurlagoona, Susan, the
laundress, one day became seriously ill with
cramps. Neither mustard nor castor-oil relieved
her sufferings, and the case seemed hopeless. In
the night the niggers started chanting their
dirges, and we expected to receive obituary
notices in the morning. Next day we got a
surprise. There was Susan, back at the tub,
splashing flakes of white clothes and foam about
.nothing had happened. I inquired for the
physician. “My word,” she replied, “ that
preller Cagie berry klever. Him bit take it jam
tin alonga bingy. Pain all go.”

“Elmore” : Another crime the fox is
guilty of is possum killing. I once noticed a lot
of headless ’possums lying under trees in the
State forest of N.E. Victoria, which is alive with
the brown curse. On -inquiring the whyfor was
told it . was foxes. It seemed improbable, but I
went out m the moonlight and saw for myself.
Reynard watched patiently under some scrub till
the possum came down. One rush and it was all
over. The ’possum seemed stupefied and fell an
easy prey.

At far-west Birdsville (Q.), which is
within sight of The Edge, Justices of the Peace
are regarded as special creations of the Governor-

"; L • says lam drunk. Who is he ? Wire imme-

The Prooshan ” : My cat recently pre-
sented the household with a litter of seven kittens.
Hearing an awful row one midnight, such as only
cats and the Salv’army can make, I rushed
out armed with a walking-stick and a desire
for blood. As I entered the kitten quarter
a large tabby Tom, their reputed father,
fled like a scattered devil, leaving two
infanticided kittens bleeding in the
laager. Next day the mother dragged
the five remaining katlettes into
the dining-room for safety, she
having egress at night through the
window. Well, about the hour
that ghosts take healthy pedestrian
exercise, I was awakened .by the
most awful screams of blue and
green murder in that room, and,-
darting in, caught the murdering
parent at his work. The brave
mother’s tail was out as stiff as a
rolling pin, while the four unmur-
dered kittens stood with bristles
up, spitting out a constant protest
of “fut, fut, fut! ” On seeing me
the Tom-cat, as is usual with T.
cats when cornered, started sky-
rocketing up the wall in about
nine places at once, rattling
among wine glasses and silver in
fine style. As I concluded he
might next try a dash up my
legs, I opened the door and let
him pass out. At 4 a.m. he got
in and killed two more, and now
I’m waiting with revenge in one
eye and the sight of a rifle in
the other for that unnatural father.
But, if this is the custom with Toms, how the
mischief do kittens find a slant to become cats ?

“Roma”: These three events happened
in my own South Gippsland district. They repre-
sent what the people call “ ’ard moz ”
A South Gippslander was offered £3 10s. for a nine-
months-old calf ;he wouldn’t sell under £3 12s. 6d. Two
days afterwards the calf had a blow-out on wet lucerne
with the usual result. (Dead calf.)
Another man in the same locality had a number of
mining shares which he had been hanging on to for some
time. Being pressed for the needful, he sold out :00 at
9d. each. Next day the mail brought news that the shares
had risen to 6s. Sd. each. (Fearful language.)
A young couple were trying to find a suitable name for
their first infant. They couldn’t quite decide between
Hortense Daphne and Estelle Genevieve. While they
were hesitating, an unexpected maiden aunt, who might
do something for the infant later on, settled the question.
Susan Hectorina Jane was the awful result. Very ’aid
moz for the infant. The aunt died soon after, and left
nothing to Susan Hectorina Jane, which was still ’arder
moz.
“D.M.”: Re par by “Priv.”
(B. 29/3/’O6). The first steamer to
navigate the Murray, as far as
Echuca, was the Gemini (Captain
Randell), a twin boat—two hulls
under one deck, with paddles be-
tween the hulls. Capt. Cadell fol-
lowed in Randell’s wake, in the
Lady Augusta, and reached Albury.
Cadell received the bonus offered
by the S.A. Government to the
navigator who should first reach
Albury, but Randell was the real
Captain Cook of Murray navi-
gation. Captains Mace and Jack-
son cannot be regarded as pioneer
navigators of the Murray in any
sense, for there were several
steamers running on the Murray
and Darling long before they
arrived. All they did was to intro-
duce the stern-wheel method of
Eropulsion for steamers. The Lady
•aly and Lady Darling were both
stern-wheelers. I was at Lake
Victoria station (owned by John
andD. McKinley at the time) when
the Lady Augusta stayed there a
night on her second trip up the
Murray, and all hands were drunk
for a week afterwards, except my-
self ; but I was very young then.
Captain Cadell was a poor naviga-
tor on land. Remember the time
he started out from Tapio station
on the Darling to find the Ana-
branch, distant about 40 miles,
taking a nigger with him. This
was in the middle fifties, and at
that time the Anabranch had no
well-defined channel, as now; in
many places it was only a series of
cane-grass and box flats, and it was
at one of those spots that Cadell and
his nigger struck it. The nigger said
it was the Anabranch, but Cadell said
it was not, and kept on his westerly
course. After wandering about in an
// aimless way, for two or three days, during
/ which a horse was killed for subsistence, and
the nigger tied to a tree every night for safe
keeping, Cadell found himself on the Murray at
Overland Corner. Have often questioned the
nigger re his experience of that trip, but all I
could get out of him was —“ That one Cadell
plurry fool.”
“ Edyson ” : My Gippslander is to the
fore again. A few weeks back he noticed that a
dilapidated stranger was camping in one of his
paddocks. The man had a camp-fire going, and
some washing hanging on a cord stretched between
two saplings, and a capacious hollow served him
for sitting and bedroom. The landed proprietor
took no notice, assuming that it was a mere
temporary arrangement, but when a week had
gone, and the camper was still there, and the
Gippslander discovered that his uninvited guest
was making himself more at home, he sauntered
across to give notice to quit. He found the man
rolling another hollow tree up alongside his dor-
mitory. “ How long do you intend staying
here?” asked the Gippslander. “Dunno,” an-
swered the camper. “I can’t have you making
your home here. Why are you shifting the logs?”
“Oh, I was just puttin’ in another room.” “You
needn’t trouble, then; you’ll have to clear.”
“Have ter clear?” “Yes.” “You’d turn a
feller out of his home?” “I would.” “Well,
I’m blowed ; an’ I was just thinkin’ of gettin’
married.”
