“F.Y.7” : “ Itan ” (B. 8/3/’O6) has had
his leg pulled very hard by someone re “blue
Mitchell grass in VV. Queensland. He might as
well write about a horse-cow. Blue and Mitchell
grasses are two distinct varieties, the latter
greatly predominating. It is quite right to say
that it will not flourish if the season is too
moist; an alleged grass that does not exist won’t
grow in wet weather, or any other weather. It is
also news to me (and I have had years of experi-
ence with bore water) to hear that irrigation will
destroy the grass. “ Itan ’’ cannot name a place
where this has happened—in Queensland, at any
rate. 3
BACK TO WORK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the holidays are over
And the coin is nearly done,
And you’ve spent a heap o’ money,
And you’ve had a heap o’ fun ;
When you’re fillin’ up Matilda
With a pile o’ dirty shirts,
There s a pullin’ at your heart-strings
And it somehow sorter hurts.
And you’re feelin’ sick and sorry
And inclined the graft to shirk,
When you’re leavin’ Maud and Florrie
And you’re goin’ back to work.
0, the glory of the Races
As you strolled upon the lawn !
And the women’s lovely faces
That were fairer than the dawn !
O, the green and shaven grasses,
Where the band made strains divine !
O, the clinkin’ of the glasses
As the ice danced in the wine !
But there ain’t no ice at Yarran
On the other side o’ Bourke,
And the plains are dry and barren
Where you’re goin’ back to work.
O, the glory of the ocean,
And her breath so fresh an’ sweet,
As the climbin’ rollers tumbled
In their grandeur at your feet!
And the girl that sat beside you,
Whilst above the heaving verge
The great red moon came sailin’
In her beauty o’er the surge.
But there ain’t no tumbling water
On the other side o’ Bourke,
And you’ll meet King Billy’s daughter
Where you’re goin’ back to work.
X.S.W.

“Bromide” : Be the effect of pyroligneous
gas caused by the burning of timber in a mine
(B. 5/4/’O6). • Some men go down to it very
quietly ; others laugh like maniacs. A few cry,
and some go fighting mad. It’s a sad thing at
any time to be called from work to take out a
“gassed” man. Very often there are crushed
drives to go through, long ladder roads to take,
&c. • even if your man is flat out and quiet it s
hard graft to get along with him, but when he’s
“got it fighting,” and when you find you want a
man for every square inch of his body or you
can’t hold him—then it’s just perdition. In the
old Broken Hill Block 12 fire a pair of men got
snapped on the 200 ft. level. Twenty of us were
sent to get them out, with orders to take them to
the old stables, where they would be attended to.
We got them out all right, but, when the job was
finished, of the 20 rescuers eight were insensible,
and the others were so weak they could hardly
stand. We were sitting about feeling pretty
rocky, when all at once one of the insensibles ran
amok, squealing like a pig. We rushed him, but
were too weak to hold him. What a sight that
underground stable must have presented that
morning ! A couple of men laid out, to all appear-
ances dead ; a couple more laughing like demons ;
one in the corner crying; another singing a
pathetic ballad, and stopping to “shoo” because
no one would chorus with him ; and in the middle
a group of sweating, straining, cursing men, some
with bleeding faces, one with an eye in mourning,
and hardly a man with a flannel on. Now, one
would hop out of the ruck and dance about on one
leg—he’d been kicked by a bigr hob-nailed toe-
plated blucher, propelled with all the viciousness
that a madman could put into it. A little later
another would go down with the blood streaming
from his nose —he’d stopped a “ dirty right.’ Oh,
ves, there’s humor in watching “ gassed ” men on
the surface (vide “Fitful,” 5/4/’06); but get ’em
underground and the humor falls very flat.
* * * *
“ Hamer ” : Occasionally there is an out-
break of statistical fact about the aboriginal
population, and an official breathing of regret that
it is dying out. Yet isn’t it a desirable thing that
the inferior races should die out in a quiet way as
soon as possible ? There isn't so much spare room
on this earth for even the best races, and the
careful preservation of the worst ones is foolish.
Even the Maoris, who are among the least unde-
sirable of colored races, could well be spared. It
is a very false and inverted humanitarianism that
sighs over the diminishment of such peoples—a
sort of humanitarianism that would find joy in
the preservation and multiplication of imbeciles
and lepers and consumptives.

“Q.”: Ben and me are camped at Enn-
gonia. Three weeks ago we killed a moderate-
sized goanner, 13ft. 7in. long. As soon as we
get a proper receptacle to put the remains in we
will send the specimen down to you so you can

Jacky was a witness at the Normanton
(Q.) Supreme Court, the other day. “You
tell ’m true, Jacky.” “Yes, boss.” “No
tell ’m lie.” “No, boss.” “You know
what happen you tell ’m lie, Jacky.”
“Yes, boss; me go alonga gaol.” The
Judge told the jury that as the charge
was murder he thought it wise to im-
press the witness something extra, and
then he started afresh, and again
asked Jacky if he would tell the
truth. Jacky fidgetted and nodded.
“You no tell’m lie, Jacky.” Jacky
shifted from one foot to the other,
drew a deep breath, and shouted in a
voice that blew the Judge backward :
“No blanky fear, boss ! Me no blanky
liar ! Me blanky well tell um truth !
Me blanky ”—— He was suppressed
before he got any further. But it is
wonderful liow eloquent a nigger can be
when he is allowed to say blanky, and
how silent he is when his blanky is cut
off.
“ Kola” : The summer just past was re-
markable, in Western Victoria, for the number of
common or stubble quail. These quail come south
in the early spring, and breed all over the district.
The crops were full of quail, and their nests were
everywhere. The hen quail is a great layer, eight
or ten chicks being hatched at a time; and she
usually manages to raise two clutches in the
season. The quail are now protected until the
end of February, so that the young ones have a
chance to mature. This season, as soon as the
protected period expired, there was a tremendous
onslaught made, and thousands of the toothsome
but harmless little things were slain. To shoot
them successfully a couple of good dogs are an
absolute necessity, as the quail, with a natural
dread of hawks, refuse to rise unless really
compelled to do so. The shooting season lasts
only a short time —from the first of March to the
first heavy rains. If the heavy rains come before
the first of March the shooting is largely spoiled.
But even with a close season there are not, many
points about being a quail.

“Namoi”: Be “Remo’s” inquiry (B.
5/4/’O6) as to the longest period for which sheep
can exist without water. Once, on Euroka
station, owned by Dalgety and Co., in the Wal-
gett (N.S.W.) district, there were 17 sheep un-
claimed at a general muster. They were put into
a yard of about a quarter-acre area, pending
investigation. Next morning business called the
manager (the late F. Y. Wolsely) to Sydney, and
as no one else on the station knew anything of the
matter, the sheep remained in the yard until his
return, nine weeks and four days afterwards.
They were found all alive, but very thim The
yard, which had been well grassed at the time of
their confinement, was quite bare. Probably they
could have existed for months without water in a
sufficiently large paddock. A sheep man told
me of an instance of sheep doing without water
for thirteen weeks on Piedmont station, owned
by Capel Brothers, in the Bingara district
(N.S.W.). A black boy had locked a small mob
in an isolated and little-used yard, for some pur-
pose or other. That night he was killed by a fall
from a horse, and it was not until thirteen weeks
later that the overseer, riding that way, dis-
covered the disconsolate jumbucks—all alive, but
very poor. The idea that sheep sometimes do not
drink for weeks in a good season is correct. I
have known instances myself. Sheep are about
the laziest animals in the world, and when de-
pasturing any distance from water, will not visit
it till their thirst becomes unendurable.
» * * *
“ Quandong ” : Be bucolic names borne by
ancient N.S.W. country inns (B. 29/3/’OG). About
half a century ago the Durham Ox was the only
pub. at Casino, and the Flying Horse was a way-
side shanty on the Casino-Gfrafton road. Both of
these are now memories of the past. This peculiar
nomenclature of inns was a survival of a 17th cen-
tury English custom. Ability to read being the
exception rather than the rule in that century,
shops and inns were distinguished by picture signs
of animals, heads of Turks and other aliens,
ploughs, ships, anchors, &c. Cattle of various
breeds and colors, horses in striking attitudes, and
ploughs were generally the signs of country inns.
The spread of education, the lettered sign, and
the modern system of numbering buildings in
populous localities gave the picture-sign its death-
blow. The names, however, survived for a while.
Many of the N.S.W. innkeepers in the early days
were imported, and brought with them the Spotted
Cow and the Swan with Two Necks. Now that
they have gone the term inn lias largely disap-
peared into the limbo of almost forgotten things.
The veriest one-liquor shanty is now an hotel.
“T.G.F.” : Can any of your fifty-cat
power bush liars answer the following questions :
(1) Why, looking north at a certain time of the
day, on a plain out Moree way, sheep, at a
distance of two miles, are magnified to the size of
cattle ? (2) Also, why sheep, half a mile away in
an opposite direction, are decreased to the size of
rabbits? (3) Also, why black lettering three
inches in height can be read easily at a mile and a
half with the naked eye? [The Bulletin offers
these replies as its theories : (1) They aren’t.
(2) They aren’t. (3) It isn’t. (4) It being un-
necessary to account for the existence of the thing
that doesn’t exist, the matter may be dropped.]

“ O’F.” : The task of providing material
for State school “nature study” is not easily
fulfilled by some city scholars. The country boy
can readily visit the nearest waterhole and snare
a yabbie when the teacher’s next demonstration
is to concern that crayfish (a frequent subject), but
the town j lad is warned off most of the available
patches of water. I recently heard a policeman
at Melbourne University warning quite a crowd
of boys who had invaded the grounds that they
were liable to all sorts of dire penalties for
E caching crayfish out of the lake. Some mem-
ers of the party were also trembling as they
realised the guilt of their attempt to catch for
school nature study the butterflies that the
professors preserve for their own shooting or
something. So the school boys in that quarter
have to choose between being kept in for failing to
produce specimens and receiving some such sen-
tence as transportation to the Cold Country for
annexing the University’s strictly-preserved
yabbie.

“ Janor” : “The Prooshan ” is right re
the ingratitude of the cow (B. 29/3/’O6). Also I
have known for long that the cow’s half-brother,
the bullock, is tarred with the same brush. Some
years ago I found one on Benambra Station (Vic.)
very much attached to a tree. This tree, a big
white gum, was hollow—a mere shell, with a rift
like an Ain one side- Bullock had put his head
through the wide part of the opening to lick up
the fine charcoal lying on the ground inside —
cattle on sour country like charcoal. Throwing
up his head before withdrawing it, he had wedged
his neck in the narrow portion of the slit, and
jammed his horns, and got into a general: tangle.
Tramped over to the camp —four miles through
Bin. of snow—got the axe, returned and chopped a
chunk out of the edge of the opening, large
enough to free his head. Immediately he yelled
at me through his great damp nose and charged
with his horns and half a ton of beef to back them
up. I dropped just in time, and the whole box
of tricks passed harmlessly over me. Hei turned
quickly and came again, but I was then inside the
tree, where I sat for two hours listening to his
threats and insulting remarks. Then he left for
reinforcements; but I wasn’t there when the
ungrateful beast returned with three more
bullocks. Since then, if I find any of the species
in a tree, I’m a strict vegetarian.

THE WOOER’S LUCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Times you’re sinking, sinking, sinking, or, it may be,
simply drinking
(You are bound to find the bottom be it shaft or be it
tank),
And it seems as if some devil working at a lower level
Twists the country so that always you shall strike a
mullock bank.
Now the parsons, after praying—from the Book there’s
no gainsaying—
Read that Providence treats all alike without regard to
rank,
But I haven’t heard it mentioned that an old Judge who
is pensioned,
Goins- to the pay-clerk’s window ever struck a mullock
bank.
Whilst sinking, sinking, sinking, I was dutifully thinking
If my.hole should prove a golden one, the Lord I’d
humbly thank.
Now will some good Christian tell us who the flaming
forge and bellows
I’m to thank that I have bottomed on a barren mullock
bank ?

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The first John Norton "who took root in
Australia was a native of Leatherhead, England.
He sailed for Sydney with a big family in 1819,
and the clan throve amazingly. James Norton,
M.L.C. is a descendant.
Henry Tennent Donaldson, politician,
journalist and civil servant, accidentally killed in
Sydney G.P.O. lift, belonged to the same Donald-
son clan as N.S.W.’s first Premier. The present
Anglican Bishop of Brisbane is also of the flock.
“Dutchy” Neitenstein, who takes De-
lohery s place on N.S.W. Public Service Board, is
German on the father’s side and English on the
mother s. The late Parkes gave him his first
chance and pushed him on on various occasions.
And a little push sent ‘‘Dutchy” a lons' way.
A strange fate appears to pursue the
holder of the billet of steward to Wellington
(M.Li.) hospital. Three have suicided. The
latest cut his throat in the institution, and, though
his attempt was ineffective, he died from pneu-
monia while in the middle of his recovery from
the other trouble.
The late William M'Lellan, ex-M.L.A.
(Vic.), “the Dove of Ararat,” when Minister of
Mines in the M'Culloch Cabinet, offered in the As-
sembly to eat his hat if anyone ever showed him a
ton of marketable Victorian coal. He relied on
the opinions of distinguished geologists. The
mines of Outtrim, Korumburra and Jumbunna
came afterwards, but the hat was never eaten.
9 ar P en ter J. 11. Wood, the Wangaratta
() mail who, some years back, made his own
coffin, and then, as he had no immediate use for it
in the usual way, utilised it as a cupboard, has
grown weary of seeing the grisly thing hanging
round. He auctioned it, the other day, to a local
undertaker for 12s.
Edward Joyce, of Little Plain, Monaro
j i )’ who . was said to be 105 years of age,
died last week in Cooma Hospital of senile decay.
Nominally, Edward Joyce went one year better
just-deceased Edward Dumaresq, of
Tasmania, who was credited with 104 years. But
the age of the old pioneer who pegs out in a
hospital generally rests largely on local tradition.
The late Captain Edward Dumaresq, of
Tus., had been a J.P. since 1828, and reckoned
proudly^that he was the senior J.P. of the earth.
It wasn’t much of a distinction, but it was some-
thing. The fact that Dumaresq belonged to an
old family might be gathered from the fact that
he pronounced his name as it wasn’t spelled—
Dumarek. The only really old name that is pro-
nounced as it is spelled is Smith.
THE CONVERTED
NIGGER.
The revolted niggers in
South Africa are said to he
mostly members of the
Ethiopian Church.
The Orotava took away for a trip to Eng-
land, Charles Sanderson, ex-police Superintendent
at Bathurst (I\.S. W.), and nowretired upon a well-
earned pension. Mr. Sanderson joined the police
force in 1855 and was a sergeant at the time of the
Eugowra escort robbery in 1862. He so closely
followed Gardiner and mate that they abandoned
their pack horse, which was secured by Sanderson,
who found on it four bags of the stolen gold,
weighing 1200 to 1500 ounces.
Talk about the new woman ! Hezekiah
Parker, aged 81, and a Victorian of 55 years’
standing, a pioneer of' Bendigo, who claims to
have sunk the first shaft on that field early in
December, 1851, says that on arriving at Bendigo
Creek the first miners he saw were two women
who were cradling dirt in the bed of the stream.
Who were those two she-pioneers—those early
washerwomen ? Parker also claims to have
bottomed the first holes at Golden Gully (Forest
Creek) and at Spring Gully (Fryer’s Creek) in the
Roaring Fifties. And how those Fifties did roar '•
The late William Bailey, of Ballarat, who
passed out recently, aged 79, owned such well-
known turf-fliers as Annersley, Metford, Coil,
Cocos and Hautvilliers. Also, he owned the
distinction of being called “Weeping Bailey.”
When Learmonth accused Bailey of wilfully mis-
managing the Egerton mine, so as to induce him
to sell the splendid property for a song to Bailey’s
own syndicate, Bailey, so Learmonth said, “pro-
tested his innocence with tears.” Hence the
“Weeping.”
Dr. Murphy, R.C. Archbishop of Tasmania,
celebrated, on Friday, the 40th anniversary of his
ar -n V u on T^ e * s l an( h He is 91 years of age—or
will be on Waterloo day, June 18—and has been
oo years in the ministry. At one time he was the
youngest bishop in Christendom; now he is the
oldest.
Acting-Chief Justice Holroyd retired from
the Victorian Bench on Saturday. His Honor is
the son of a Judge in Bankruptcy, and grandson
? Judge of the King’s Bench. He is in his
/9th year, was called to the bar in 1855, and
arrived in Melbourne in 1859. In 1881 he was
raised to the Bench, after twice refusing a
judgeship. It is anticipated that the senior
Puisne Judge, A’Beckett, who is 70 years of age,
will be Acting-Chief Justice while Madden and
his wonderful moustache are away.
Governor Rawson sends a son to Sydney
University, which may possibly stimulate some
old local parties to follow his example. But one
reason why so many descendants of the old
families, that made money here when cattle-brands
were less distinct than they are now, go to English
universities is that the exams, are easier there
than in Australia. The Australian who goes to
England can get through in quicker time and with
less application than if he attended a local uni-
versity.
The chairman of the Tariff Commission has
given an order to help on a new local industry.
When the Commission was sitting in Adelaide,
a German, one Kumnick, of Lobethal, gave evi-
dence as to his thriving little bat-making enter-
prise, the willow as well as the labor being
Australian. Now John Quick has ordered one of
Kumnick’s best bats. Why should Australia
flog an English-made cricket ball with an English-
made bat ?
Rangi Singh, on whom Judge Rutledge
poured scorn at Croydon (Q.) for pleading an
appointment with the landlady when larceny from
a dwelling was charged against him, was a very
different British subject from the white man who
got into trouble a few years ago in a very high-
toned Sydney suburb. The white man had an
appointment with a lady, and when found on the
premises he saved her good name by passing him-
self off as a petty larcenist, with rather serious
results—in the way of gaol.
In an article on the jubilee of Responsible
Government in N.S.W. Sydney Telegraph states
that, in accordance with instructions under the
Constitution, Governor Denison sent for Stuart
Donaldson to form the first Ministry. As a
matter of fact, Edward Deas Thomson was first
asked to form a Ministry, and when he failed
Donaldson became a stop-gap Premier. Donaldson
was such a makeshift that he only lasted 80 days,
and he never got another chance as Premier,
though Premier Parker made him Treasurer once
for 11 months.
Died in Westralia, “ Bob” Gibson, best
known in these later days as manager, director,
and a while ago largest holder of the Golden Pole
(W.A.). It’s a pity, for the sake of the other
work he did, that his name couldn’t be remem-
bered bv some other business. Trained as a
manager in Victoria, on the Barrier (he managed
the Pioneer, which was worked years before
Broken Hill was found), and at Zeehan, he later
was in charge of the Great Fingall. Leaving there
he went to Kalgoorlie, and stood behind more
prospecting ventures than probably any other
man in the State.
The British magazine-writer finds it hard
to keep King Edward’s head out of his articles.
A twaddlesome London Magazine article on
“Some Great Naval Families” actually claims
the present monarch as an eminent navigator.
Further
The Royal Family of England is a typical naval family.
Vice-Admiral the Prince of Wales is following in the
footsteps of a long line of distinguished naval ancestors.
That eminent seaman, James 11., was appointed Lord
High Admiral of England when a little boy of five.
The London Magazine seems to half believe that
Salt-horse James achieved command of the British
fleet at the age of five by his own unaided efforts.
The late George Coppin is said to have
introduced, in conjunction with Brooke, the first
white swans ever seen in Australia, and that firm
was certainly the first to land “the ship of the
desert ” upon Victorian soil. Some of the camels
imported by Coppin and Brooke, after seeing
much service in carrying juveniles about in the
Cremorne Gardens, were purchased for the pur-
pose of carrying Burke and Wills to the Gulf of
Carpentaria, but, like most of the arrangements
connected with that grossly bungled expedition,
the purchase of these camels proved a failure.
After a close overhaul, they were condemned as
medically unfit to undertake such a journey, and,
consequently, had the luck to be left behind.
The recent announcement of the silver
wedding of Ezekiel Alexander Baker recalls to
mind that almost-forgotten and ordinary N.S.W.
politician of the seventies and early eighties. He
held office in three Governments. In Robertson’s
third Ministry he succeeded Thomas Garrett as
Secretary for Lands. In Robertson’s fourth team
he again succeeded Garrett at the Lands —he
seemed to have had a habit of succeeding Garrett
at the Lands ; also he took a turn at the Mines. He
bossed the Mines Department for eight months in
the third Parkes Government—the only Govern-
ment in which Chief Justice Darley tried his hand
at what Parkes called “hoffis,” if being Vice-
President of the Executive Council could be called
“ hoffis.” Then Baker dropped out —that was 25
years ago—and he has been out ever since. He is
now somewhere about 86.
Passed out recently, aged (so far as is
known) 104, Captain Edward Dumaresq, who
claimed to be Tasmania’s oldest resident. He
came to the island in 1828, and was appointed by
Governor Arthur Surveyor-General, also Police
Magistrate at New Norfolk. He purchased the
Mount Ireh Estate, near Longford, in 1835, and
died there. His father was Colonel John
Dumaresq, whose services in America were re-
warded by the British Government with a Sand-
hurst education and army commissions for his
three sons. Edward’s eldest brother, Henry
Dumaresq, served through the Peninsular war,
and rose to be colonel at Waterloo. Captain
William Dumaresq, another brother, also saw
Peninsular service. Edward himself joined the
Indian army, but resigned to come south. His
son, H. R. Dumaresq, who represented Longford
in Tas. Assembly for some years, is a youth of 80.
“ N.”: Re par (5/4/’O6) about Joseph Capel
Smyth, the silent member of Queensland
Assembly. There’s no need to go so far
back as the ’Bo’s for another example.
Robert Dunsmore, member for Maranoa,
was a very speechless man. He might have
finished his political career without publicly
opening his mouth had not someone proposed
to abolish the refreshment room, whereupon
Robert rose hurriedly to say it was a dashed
shame or words to that effect, and spoiled
his record.
From “Maori Mac,” who writes one of
those fists that look like a fallen tower :
Publican Gow, of Maoriland’s capital city, who recently
organised two unsuccessful attempts to recover the
Elingamite’s lost treasure, is a man of many parts.
For years he was a fireman, and then an engine-driver, on
the Christchurch-Dunedin express. He gave up the foot-
plate for the bar, and settled down for a time to a life of
beer and skittles. He weighs 18 stone, and his vast bulk
and his elephantine motor-car are amongst the sights of
dreary Wellington. Publican Gow grew tired of settling
down, and took to treasure-hunting as a side line. The
first attempt—a very hopeless one—to recover the sub-
merged loot was made in a small schooner with a crew of
five, including the stout publican and a professional
diver. It is alleged that Mr. Gow occupied the exact
middle of the vessel to prevent it tipping up. Next, the
s.s. Pelican was chartered in Auckland and a second
attempt made. All went well this time till the diver
went below, when he discovered what a vast place the
nethermost ocean is, and what a small affair is a three-
year-old wreck. Incidentally he reported that the engine-
room telegraph read “full speed ahead,” which might or
might not settle a lot of nautical discussion. As for
treasure, there was none to be found. Alexander M. Gow
is back at the beer-pump in a cheerful spirit.
Locomotive driver John Brazier, of
Ballarat, leaves the footplate, having failed to
pass the last sight test. Brazier has driven the
Adelaide and Melbourne express train almost
since there was an Adelaide and Melbourne ex-
press—nearly 20 years. He has run, roughly,
600,000 miles, and has never had a serious acci-
dent. Of late years Brazier has been driving
engine 552Aa, and it was “ the pride of the road ”
in his hands. It is said that he spent all his spare
time while standing at Melbourne and Stawell
(three or four hours in each case) in cleaning and
polishing up his pet, and it certainly looked like
it. It is hoped that “commercial ” management
will not prevent a little sentiment here, and will
give a faithful servant an easy billet for the
remainder of his railway life. “ Groom to 552Aa ”
seems an appropriate job.
The reminiscences of “ F.W.” :
Re Fanning Island. I knew the reputed owners, Messrs
Gregg, well. The old man was a hard-headed Scotchman,
who married a Polynesian woman from Monahiki and
settled on Fanning Island about 1865. Prior to his occu-
pation it was uninhabited. Gregg employed a few natives
of Rokohanga and Monahiki to gather the copra, which
was fairly plentiful, and found a market for it in Hono-
lulu. The island is small (about 10 miles in circumfer-
ence), with a lagoon in the centre. Anchorage on the lee
side is fairly safe during the prevailing trade winds. The
Greggs throve and prospered exceedingly. Four boys
and two girls were the issue of their marriage. They
were all educated in Honolulu and San Francisco. Willie
Gregg, the eldest, was fined $25,000 for complicity in an
alleged revolution planned by a few ill-advised sympa-
thisers of poor Queen Lilliokilani, and his pa paid'like a
trump. A daughter died of consumption in Honolulu in
’92. Another daughter married Captain Reid and with
him visited Sydney in 1594 in the brig Douglas. The
vessel was sold to an Auckland firm and re-christened the
Indianopolis, and was lost in Bass Straits during its first
voyage under the new name. Captain and Mrs. Reid
stayed some time in Australia, Mrs. Reid giving birth
to a child in Sydney. She was a very beautiful woman
with quite a regal carriage ; she dressed well and had the
longest hair I ever saw.
“ Melb.” : Maoriland’s Robert Stout is a
stately gent, with the body of a Fysh and the
visage of a Socrates. At the Public Library
shivoo, the other day, he fetched it in neatly that
every institution is the shadow of a man, thus
referring to Redmond Barry’s statue in front of
the edifice, with its gaze to the University. The
busts of illustrious men, which line both sides of
the reading-room, gazed wonderingly down upon
a gathering of fashionable popinjays and pea-
cocks, the sort that flaunt on the Cup lawn, and
care about as much for literature as an old hat.
Premier Bent spoke of Redmond Barry having
helped to make Him, in an educational way, so
that he is not altogether a self-made man. A good
many remember Barry, upright on his brown cob,
with his peculiar straight-brimmed belltopper, a
trifle sugar-loafy, in the French style. His house
was where the Children’s Hospital is, and he
luxuriated in a swimming-bath there.
“ Hexham ” writes
Australia takes little interest in southern hemisphere
celebrities outside of the English-speaking area, but the
death of “ The Honest Man ” of South American politics
should hardly pass unnoticed. Soldier, poet, journalist,
General Bartolomei Mitre was in the sixties and seventies
the best known man south of the line. He was of another
class from the Castros and Blancos. After the battle of
Pavon (in which one of the Mulhalls, either the statisti-
cian, or more probably his elder brother, was his aide-de-
camp) he had Argentina at his feet, and might have made
himself dictator, but patriotically refused. He left the
presidency as poor as he entered it. He wrote much,
and translated many of Longfellow’s poems into Spanish.
His “ Una Limosna por Dios” is a favorite school piece
throughout southern Spanish America, and has lines that
stir even a gringo’s blood. On retiring from office he
started La Nacion in Buenos Aires, and made it about
the freest and most independent high-class daily in South
America. So independent was Mitre that, by opposition
to the Government, he risked and lost the subsidy usually
paid to influential journals in that part of the world.
Would the proprietors of the D.T. or Herald do this ?
Emilio Castelar was one of La Nacion’s European corres-
pondents for many years.
SAMUEL GILLOTT BENT’S ATTORNEY-
GENERAL.
The Rev. Fitchett, orating at a “Royal
Society of St. George ” shivoo in Melbourne, the
other night, said that the man of English stock
who could think of the old land without his blood
growing warm deserved to belong to a Peace and
Humanity Society. The Rev. Fitchett is a pro-
fessed follower of the meek and lowly Nazarene,
the preacher of peace and humanity, but the par-
son in him is overwhelmed by the author of
“Bleeds that Won the Empire,” in which book
all the heroes of peaceful victories are overlooked,
and only the blood and the horrid remains are
chronicled. The Bulletin, on the whole, prefers
the man who sheds the blood to the man who
spends his years sedulously stirring up the pool
with his shepherd’s crook. Anyhow, it is time
Fitchett gave up living a double life—howling for
blood and cannon and battle, yet hiding his own
personality behind the clerical rig-out which saves
him from the risk of even having the gore of his
nose shed by the push in the battlefield of Bourke-
street. Let him at least become a policeman, if
he isn’t equal to being a soldier. Then, if he
cared to be hypercritical, he could run himself in
on suspicion of annexing other people’s writings
to fill his lamentable publications.
Of William Bailey, of Ballarat, who went
to his long home last week, it should be written
on his tombstone that lie managed the Egerton
mine. The Egerton in its young days was the
property of a confiding citizen—one Learmonth—
who took on Bailey as manager, and trusted him
implicitly. Bailey persuaded the boss that the
outlook down below was unpromising, and that
Providence had sene along a syndicate of san-
guine Jugginses to take the show off his hands.
80 Learmonth closed with an offer of £13,500. In
consideration of Bailey’s assistance in the trans-
action, the vendor presented him with £6OO.
Presently the mine began to give splendid
returns, and it transpired that the syndicate of
confiding Jugginses consisted of Bailey mostly.
They got £300,000 in divs. from the non-
paying Egerton which Learmonth sold for
£13,500, but whilst they were piling up their
riches Learmonth went to law for a recovery of
the property on the ground that Bailey had ob-
tained it by false pretences. The suit absorbed
£BO,OOO in costs. The lawyers prolonged it for
two years. Learmonth lost the battle, lost his
health in fighting it, and went back to Scotland
to spend his remaining days in cursing. Bailey
went on and prospered in partnership with Martin
Loughlin. After the death of that kindred spirit
nearly 20 years ago, Bill Bailey put his money
mostly into sheep stations, and became a fine old
representative opponent of land taxation.

The S.M. Herald (9/4/’O6) on “ Immi-
-1 are thousands of splendid emigrants to be col-
lected in England, Ireland, and Scotland if they could
only be got at and told of what Australia has to offer.
We will he told that the land is not immediately avail-
able—that our own farmers’ sons are looking for holdings
without too much success. But that cry must not be
permitted to stultify the immigration movement. It
cannot be accepted as an answer. We know the land is
there we know how much of it has become locked up,
and public opinion has decided that it must be made
available.
And then, just when the Herald might have
mentioned that it was in favor of a graduated
land-tax to compel the owners of the locked-up
areas to make them available, it changed the
subject.
‘‘Snell” : Is the Confucian played out ?
I find that in the Chinese and Japanese laundries
in my Melbourne suburb (St. Kilda) practically
the whole of the work is done by white girls, and
where, a few years ago, you might see half-a-
dozen Chow washermen (I was going to say pers-
piring washermen, but Chinamen never perspire),
toiling at the ironing tables of the boss heathen,
you now discover as many girls pushing the box-
irons for the wages of Ah Sin. The washing, too,
is done by European women. The most lament-
able feature of the business is the fact that these
girls do not seem to think it beneath their dignity
to work for an orange-colored Asiatic. What
about a vaudeville Chow singing a localised ver-
sion of the coon song—“ I’ve got a white woman
workin’ for me ” ?
“ A. Z.” : Pressmen will miss lawyer Kane,
from Melbourne City Court. I have known him
to defend thirteen larcenists, drunks, thieves and
other lawbreakers, inside an hour and a half, ,and
rattle his fees as he wished Panton, P.M., “good
morning.” Remember him once being asked if he
appeared for defendant. “One moment and I’ll
tell you,” replied Kane, for the fee had not been
paid in advance. Then somebody handed him
something, and without a smile he added-“Oh
yes, I appear for the defendant.”

se that yarn (8., 5/4/00) about the old
British ship Silberhorn which 1 iv and mouldered
for four years in San Francisco harbor waiting a
charter at an acceptable rati-, it seems that the
vessel in question was by no means an exceptional
case. The “old Britishers John Bull’s old
sailing vessels which wouldn’t load with wheat till
the freight rates went up, and which saw the
years drifting by while freights flatly refused to
go up - " Were for a long time quite a feature of the
Golden Gate. One of them has roosted and rotted
there for four years, and is still, on hand. The
crews were discharged, the captains brought out
their wives and families and started house-keeping
on their lonesome craft, and advertising agents
came on board with insulting offers of money if
they Were allowed to paint American pill adver-
tisements on the hulls of John Bull’s old ships,
As much as £2O a month is said to have been
offered by a pill fiend and refused with bitter-
ness. The dignity of the laid-up British marine
bad to be maintained.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 1, 1906.
My Dear Arini,—
Miss Grace Palotta is back in Sydney after a
five years interlude, with not a shadow of a
change in face, hair or figure. “ Whom the gods
love live young.” Grace, in a sky-blue silk
kimono embroidered with white silk butterflies,
says, It is just too sweet of you to say that/’ and
when I declare myself butterfly-struck she turns
herself to let me see ‘‘the big as a bird one”
between her shoulders. And she has just
washed her hair and is letting it dry,
watching the India the while swinging at
anchor opposite where she’s staying in Elizabeth
® a y* Apologies are made with the engaging
candor of a child and go well with the innocent
white butterflies. “ Eef as you say I am not
altered it must be from never worrying, never
being sick, a lot of hard work and—perhaps milk.
Directly I got off the India to go to a friend’s
place in Melbourne I made a sortie on the milk.
I am ashamed to look a cow between the norns.”
Someone said “Frocks ! ” and Miss Palotta, as
the frockiest frocker we know, told that most of her
dresses had been made in New York. “The
leading dress designers of America go to Paris
and Vienna, take whatever hints those capitals
have to offer in the way of artistic dressing, and
returning put their own cachet upon the styles,
altering and improving them in their own original
way.
“New York is as good as any place in the world
now to get clothes. Yes, a few of my gowns are
corselets, but I wear them with a deep belt, and I
have more radium satins than chiffon velvets.
Radiums are best for stage wear. A friend who
came to see me this morning told me that she had
her new grey cloth corselet costume in a cab at
the door, and she was on her way to its maker to
get her and her assistants to rivet it on her. Her
own maid and the maid from next door had tried
to put it on so as to get the seams all straight, and
at the tenth attempt the lady began to cry and
the pair of maids sat down on the floor and
wept also. But, talking of America: It is most
curious how there are no old ladies there. I do
not know how they do it, but they have stopped
in age at what looks like well-preserved 45. Their
hair is very often grey, but their faces are never
lined, and their figures rarely bulky. I think
their determination has a lot to do with it—
keeping Time outside the door. And they go in
for face massage much, and get such a gloss on
their hair, and tilt their hats so artfully. At the
fashionable dining places I used to see at night
some lovely Restaurant hats of black or white
costly lace, with plumes or huge flowers. They
wear what suits them, and a debutante will say,
‘ I look best in black, and my coming-out frock
shall be black,’ and she will come out all black,
and pearls and fair hair, with perhaps scarlet
shoes.”
Somebody mentions as attractively-named the
20-minutes playlette “Grace Palotta and Her
Millinery Maids.” With many swift undulations
of arms from which the kimono sleeves fall away,
showing rounded elbows with the dimples in the
right place, the artiste describes the turn as “O,
so clevaire.” It was written as the result of many
sage heads in council, by a well-known composer
and librettist. Midnight in a millinery store and
from the orchestra twelve o’clock strikes
out. The maids by “ arrangement ” and mirrors
show only bust view, and wear beautiful big
hats. They are supposed to be milliners’
dummies, and begin to talk, complaining of the
weariness of being stuck there year after year.
One says moths have eaten her eyebrows and the
white ant is in her lower supports. The stage is
lightened and Madame (Grace), the milliner,
comes down in glittering black and a picture hat
and sings, “All Is Vanity Here Below.” The
dummies ask her permission to be human until
daylight, and the rest of the turn is singing and
dancing in various costumes. Dawn, striking
through the windows, finds them dressed as Kate
Greenaways and singing the praises of the old-
fashioned big bonnet, and then they scurry back
to their unexciting wooden existence in the hat
parlor.
According to other conversational bones thrown
out by the artiste, the Australian abroad must be
a warm-hearted fellow. He would balance up to
her wherever he niet her in foreign parts, take
and shake her hand without waiting for its ex-
tension, saying he had seen her play in dear old
Australia. Also, she reiterates her joy at getting
back, and asserts that in all her travels she has
seen no view like that one which looks out on
Elizabeth Bay. Which is something for the
Elizabethans to go on with. Miss Palotta is stay-
ing with Miss Pearl Faithful, at “ St. Anne’s!”
The mansion owners about there must be a haloed
set. “ St. Monans” (Mr. J. C. Williamson’s) is
next door, and there are other canonized resi-
dences in the neighborhood.
Apropos of Miss Ena Battenberg’s expected
accession to the throne of Spain, it is noted
that for over 500 years the Spanish Queen has
been sent to bed, as a matter of royal etiquette, at
10 in summer and 8.30 in winter, and has been
locked in till morning. Spain started to go down-
hill very early ; any country whose queen was put
to bed at 8.30 like a spanked kid might be
expected to do so. The end of the trouble will be
that the British fleet will be sent to demonstrate
off Bilbao to secure Ena the right to sit up till
10.30. y
The Governor-General and Lady Northcote’s
garden party is now, of course, mediaeval history,
but a few memories of it blossom in the dust of
seven, days. One of them is of a gown (Mrs.
Burdekin’s) of white chiffon painted around the
foot of the skirt with great mauve blossoms and
baby-leaf green foliage. It looked as though it
might have grown a natural product, all in a
garden fair. An American visitor-journalist,
Miss Krout, who has been writing in the dailies
about ’Frisco, was present, sponsored by Miss Mar-
garet Windeyer. She was the lady chosen by a
syndicate of newspapers to represent them at the
Queen s jubilee. Someone did a two-step up to
the lady and said cheerfully, referring to the
xrisco calamity, “Well, so the Almighty’s been
at it again. Mrs. Philip Morton made her first
appearance after her Asiatic airing in pale blue
silk muslin. r lhe Jap Consul’s wife was “the
pet of the party.” She was Frenchily corseted to
a tiny waist and obvious, round little hips, and
her royal blue radium satin trailed a yard behind
her. She doesn’t look more than 18 or so. A
Portuguese countess trod the Governor-General’s
grass. She has come to visit a sister, Mrs.
Gordon, who is living about Point Piper, and
is young and awfully pretty. Some allur-
P 1? . l ace over-skirts helped to decorate the
brilliant autumn landscape. The W. P. Manning
lady had a whole robe of black Spanish lace over
white silk. Very few corselet gowns were abroad.
What has become of all those the swell shops
were making right up to Easter ? Perhaps the
owners, sick of the hard labor of getting into them,
have already handed them over to the ladies who
invite the attention of those “having left-off
clothing of every description.”
All the one-time Australians resident in San
r rancisco _ seem to be safe. The Rennies, of the
Mutual Life of N.Y., have lost their house and
furniture, but rescued their lives out of the debris.
Tittell Brune’s house and effects are gone, but her
husband is safe. By the way, Mr. Brune was
play-acting here a wee bit while ago with a not
first-star company. At that time he was said to
be a lawyer; now he is a bank man. Amaz-
es people the Americans ! Our one-time French
vice-consul, the handsome Count Peretti della
Rocca, had his consular residence in Market-street,
where such awful damage was done. His friends
here await news of his safety. A Wellington
(M.L.) belle. Miss Rena Butt, who lately married
Dr. Napier Maclean, landed in ’Frisco the day be-
fore the ’quake and put up at the Palace Hotel.
She has still to be heard from.
Mrs. Harry Henderson, who has returned to
the dramatic stage under her maiden name,
Maggie Knight, has been engaged by Bland Holt
for the part of Anna in “The Prodigal Son,” to
be played here Saturday week. I don’t know
what Anna does in the play named after the cove
who fled from the pigs to the veal, but she’ll be
fortunate to get versatile Maggie for an exponent
anyhow.. All Maggies are nice girls or women,
and audiences will be glad to see such an old-time
favorite as this Maggie smiling behind the foot-
lights once more.
Mr. and Mrs. Donne, of Wellington (M.L.),
arrive by the Moeraki to-morrow (Wednes-
day), and will anchor at the Australia. He is
head of the Tourist Department of M.L.—that
extraordinary, octopus-like (this is writ compli-
mentary-like) institution that has “feelers”
lying all over Europe to tap the tourist imagina-
tion. When the Department has button-holed its
tourist it just makes a long 1 breathing sound like
a blow-hole and inhales him, and next thing he
knows he is thrown out on the decorative islands
as if he were Jonah and the Department the
whale. Mr. Donne recently returned from a long
trip in the Old and New Worlds, where he was
studying how the old and new fellows run the
scheme of things in those parts. Taking him all
round, tourists who have done the sights and
fished, and shot, and boiled themselves in the
springs declare him to be Necessary. So few per-
sons are really Necessary. Mrs. Donne, who was
Miss Jean Ramsay, was a fellow student with
Ada Crossley in London, but she merged a beauti-
ful voice in early matrimony. Government busi-
ness connected with the Exhibition and the plant-
ing of tourist branches in our capitals brings the
Donnes over. Mr. Owen Cox is also a returner by
same vessel.
Colonel Antill, by his resignation and his
denouncement of his superiors, has soaped that
very important and beautifully-tailored geyser,
the Military Board, and it is spouting indig-
nantly. Some three years or so ago handsome
Jack Antill married a Sydney beauty, one of the
daughters of squatter Wills-Allen. 'The An tills
will go on the land—which, as Cicero says, is the
only life for the wise man. But then Cicero
didn’t know Australia. Still, if you do go on the
land it is well to choose your.father-in-law wisely.
Colonel Antill’s reason for retirement is partly
that there’s no career ahead of the professional sol-
dier in Australia. And he’s about right, as girls who
are inclined to marry young subalterns in the glow
of their first pay might note. The subaltern in
Australia is very well paid ; he starts off with a
very fat salary for a youngster. But the future
has no charms. Promotion is slow; the highest
step possible is really only a low one; and the
“steps” carry very small increases of salary.
Practically, the permanent soldier in Aus-
tralia can at the best hope to finish his
military life as a major. The salary for a major
is of the minor order, and the retiring age is
low, and there is no pension. With luck a man
may come to be Lieut.-Colonel, but even then his
salary will be nothing like the income of a fourth-
rank barrister or doctor.
The trouble is that Australia has imitated
partly and not wholly the British system. In
England, so that young officers may hope for pro-
motion, an arbitrary retiring agehas been arranged.
As a subaltern you must retire at, say, 35. But if
you have risen to be a major you can keep on until
say 50 (I don’t kno.w the exact ages, but you’ll
find them in the Army List). If you have become
a general you may continue on almost whilst you
have a tooth left, and can climb on to your war-
horse with the help of a flight of steps. We’ve
imitated all that, but have stopped short at grant-
ing pensions, which retiring officers in England
draw. Also, in Australia a soldier can’t become a
general—if Napoleon * were in the Australian
army he couldn’t climb higher than a colonel
unless he made a revolution or something—and
has generally to be satisfied with becoming a
major. Add together poor salaries, early retire-
ment, restriction of promotion, and no pensions,
and you have the total that the soldier is not a
desirable parti, except for the girl with a ddt.
“ The hand of the vandal ” is a telling phrase.
State Premier Carruthers liked it so well that he
used it several times in his speech at the Captain
Cook anniversary celebrations at Kurnell on
Saturday. Pity Henry Parkes was not about, for
he would have talked of The ’And of the ’Un,”
which would have been much more picturesque.
The trip by special tram to La Perouse, with an
aide-de-campish trafficofficer aboard, was a delight,
running as it did past the ladies’ gaol, the ceme-
tery whither the Devonshire graveyard bones
were taken, the labor farm, sweet Lilyville, the
aborigines’ camp, green scrub, white sand, and
indigo blue sea. The wives of two Queens-
land Ministers—Mrs. T. O’Sullivan (Works) and
Mrs. Denham (Agriculture) —took the trip, and
so did the local E. W. O’Sullivans and Mrs.
Moore, wife of the Minister for Agriculture, and
two daughters ; one of the latter was the belle of
the gathering—as golden a girl as any Le
Gallienne ever quested.
Mr. B. B. O’Conor, as Minister for Education,
ought to edit the inscription that lies in brass on
the wall of the trustees’ cottage at Kurnell: —
Under the auspices of British scientists, these shores
were discovered by Janies Cook and Joseph Banks (the
Columbus and Maecenas of their time).
Australia was mapped two or three hundred
years before Cook and his wig landed. As we
haven’t much history, Excellency Rawson couldn’t
talk about “our glorious dead on a hundred
battlefields,” like the Yankee orators, but he
is always trenchant, and his facts are given
pleasantly, buttered side up. He spoke, and
unfurled the flag, and a minute later the irrelevant
band was playing “ The Rajah of Bhong.” Those
two royal vegetables planted by the Dukes of
Clarence and York some 20 odd years ago on the
“Discovery” ground are now high enough to
tickle the clouds.
No one “ was in their gaudy,” as Mrs. Brook-
field says, writing of her Circle. Patriotism and
plain clothing go together—mostly. Only Mr.
Harkness, the State Premier’s private sec., may
he said to have worn confections in the way of a
hat and boots, and Mrs. Harrison-Smith, wife of
Sir Harry Rawson’s private secretary, had a
sweet early-Autumn get-up of yello wing-leaf
embroidered silk.
Signor Steffani will be back here soon. His
return does not surprise those who know how
badly his wife stood the English winters. He had
fot up an extensive voice-training connection in
iondon, and his break-away must mean a financial
loss.
What a hail of au revoirs and good-byes are due
for the Royal Comic Opera strutters ! Margaret
Thomas (whom rumor has been engaging to a local
K.C.) is flicking the silverfish out of her Saratogas,
Rose Musgrove is nearly ready to be sea-sick, and
Doran and Haigh Jackson are manifesting a
readiness for the cradle that the deep sea rocks.
The fashionable padlocking of the Easter season
in Sydney was the marriage of Miss Lily Watson,
a daughter of Mr. James Watson, to Mr. Dyke
Acland, a barrister of Christchurch (M.L.). The
Dyke Aclands and Acland Hoods are all related,
and fancy themselves enormously, as do most
persons whose names are in the beerage and
baronetage. Bride is taking all the furniture for
her new home from this city. The paternal
billiard-room was filled with her presents, for the
dark Lily is a great favorite. Presents from the
Ewen Frazer family were extra fine, but a family
with something like £lB,OOO a year can afford to
give extra-finely, especially to an extra rich bride.
Miss Emma Muriel Glasson, of Manly, daughter
of late Rev. Gustavus Glasson, member of a well-
known squatting family, was married last Wednes-
day night at St. Matthew’s Church, Manly, to
Charles Palmer Copland, of Dalby, Queensland,
grazier. I never was at Dalby (Queensland), but
a friend of mine struck the town one dry, dry
December, and if it is always like her description
I wouldn’t be “of” it for a reconstructed over-
draft.
George Willoughby, the actor, who is to appear
with Grace Palotta and Hugh Ward in “The
Man from Mexico” on Saturday week, arrived
by the Sophocles on Saturday, and yesterday
(Monday) he married at St. Patrick’s R.C. church
Miss Rosina Kenna, of Orange. Artist Alf.
Vincent and Hugh Ward were at the wedding.
Miss Kenna is a tall, slight, fair woman, and a
noted dresser. She is related to Dr. Kenna and
to the singer, Pat “Bathurst” Kenna, now in
London, and is said to have £lOOO a year of her >
very own.
A local mercantile firm is advertising for a lady
clerk used to billing. Perhaps the firm undertakes
to teach her cooing itself.
The widow of the late statesman Henry Parkes,
with one of the latter’s daughters, was at the
Governor-General’s garden party, sitting apart
and watching the brilliant crowd spread its train.
AVord as to the identity of the fair fat lady passed
round, and she was observed with interest.
Miss Rosina Buckmann, the soprano of “The
Moorish Maid,” is (like nearly all the good singers as
well as the great singers) fairly fat and wide and
short, with a strong neck and face She reminds, in
appearance, of Amy Castles, and is fair, with that
charming speaking voice peculiar to the Maori-
land maiden. Her acting has jagged edges, and
is amateurish as yet. But her voice is a Voice —
in fact, it might be almost called a VOlCE—and
is well-trained. She was born at Blenheim and
studied singing in Birmingham under well-known
teachers. Also she has reached the fetching age
of 22.
Mr. A. G. Ralston, whose tail is now lengthened
by the letters K.C., is married to a daughter of
the late Justice Windeyer. She has the
unusual qualifications of good looks and brains
(they are unusual, that is, when embodied in one
person), and her presence on charitable com-
mittees always made for the success of the charity.
The tragic death of her student son some i 8
months ago was a shock from which she yet
suffers —to the great loss of many benevolent com-
mittees.
Two old N.S.AV. colonists have gone to the Cold
Country per the N.D.L. liner Scharnhorst, Mr.
Julian Salomons, K.C., and one-time Chief Jus-
tice of N.S.W., and Mr. Fred Lassetter, of
Lassetter and Co.
Darling Point, Potts Point, Point Piper (why
isn’t there some place called Pins Point ?), and
other more inland spots whose residents are also
on the Government House list, sent each a small
galaxy of persons to Herr Franz Degen’s two
violin recitals at the Centenary Hall. These
people would tell you, on oath, that they adore
music of the kind that Beethoven and Chopin
wrote “at the zenith of their musical creative-
ness ” (what they really appreciate is a cakewalk).
Amongst the exceptions were the T. H. Kellys—
he is a real musicker —and the W. P. Mannings.
Mr. W. P. is president of the Philharmonic,
and wallows in the kind of music that gives the
average person vertigo. And the Lachlan Beatons,
of course —he also can take a fall out of the
most occult sonata or symphony ever composed.
The remainder of the audience consisted of violin
enthusiasts, student or graduated. Franz Degen,
with his cataract of sdky dark-brown hair and
captivating smile, has great talent. His tones are
full and pure, with an emotional result that pro-
phesies for him a place among the leading
violinists of the future. He has strongly im-
pressed the unprejudiced musical critics of
Sydney ; and on his second recital, when he was
in splendid form, he was encored again and again.
Herr Degen will tour Maoriland in about a
month’s time.
Mrs. Brady Sampson, only child of the late Dr.
Clune, of Sydney, who was presented at the
Dublin Castle Drawing-room recently, would
seem, by Irish notices of the function, to have had
a white orchid kind of gown—cloth of silver,
embroidered, with cascades of chiffon and lace.
Mrs. T. A. O’Farrell, who presented the young
Sydneysider, is a sister of Lady Darnley who, as
everybody knows, was a Miss Morphy, of Victoria.
From a London correspondent :
The Agent-General and Mrs. Coghlan have very com-
fortable quarters in London now—at Albert Gate Court
Mansions. Their flat is furnished throughout in old oak,
the color scheme rose and white—a bright, warm-
looking combination much in favor just now. The
Agent-General’s pretty wife is a great favorite socially.
She is to present a Sydney girl, Miss Coles, at the next
drawing room.
Mrs. Coghlan is always at home to Australian friends
on Monday afternoons, and one comes across many well-
known faces. “A sweet thing in babies” was the attrac-
tion last Monday—a mite in blue and white, whose
mother ;(n£e Miss Hordern, of Sydney) had motored over
from the Langham, where she is living. England agrees
with Mrs. Walford, and while grumbling for the want of a
little Australian sunshine, she acknowledges the advan-
tages, in a complexion sense, of English drizzle.
I saw Miss Muriel Rich, the Queensland beauty, dining
at the Cavalry Club last week with General and Lady
Hutton. She had been rusticating at two successive rec-
tories in the country. One notices the great proportion
of pretty Australian women in London. Later on Miss
Rich joins Mrs. Conran (nee Miss Molle, of Queensland)
at her chalet in the Grindeiwald.
Lady Jersey, in black crepe-de-chine and rather weary
black toque, opened the new rooms of the Austral Club
in Dover-street last week. What otherwise might have
been a pleasant affair was spoiled by the crush. Vainly
did the committee try to clear a way for the countess and
the John Forrests, the Coclcburns, Mr. Reeves, and other
Anglo-Australian lights. Finally, these folk had to get
in through the area, or the kitchen, or something.
Sydney singer, Ada Colley, has just essayed matrimony
for the second time —if you will remember she was left a
widow at 28 with one little child. She is now Mrs. Leo
Dryden, Leo being a vocalist also, and they have a
pleasant house at Streatham. Ada was in grand opera in
Germany, but finds music halls more paying. She is at
the Tivoli just now.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Imitation may be the sincerest form of flattery,
but at times it must be very annoying-, as in the case of
“ Valaze ” the wonderful Russian skin-food. Three years
ago it was introduced to the public of Australia by Mdlle.
Helena Rubinstein, and so speedily were its good qualities
recognised that quite a big demand sprang up almost
immediately. Instantly dozens of imitations of “ Valaze ”
appeared, got up to closely resemble the Russian skin-
food. Often the food bore a name so nearly resembling
“ Valaze ” that it deceived buyers. All this was very
flattering, but at the same time decidely aggravating, for
often not only worthless, but positively harmful so called
skin-foods were trading on the reputation of “Valaze."
The nourishing, restoring, soothing and healing qualities
of the genuine “ Valaze ” are widely recognised. There is
scarcely a dressing table where the simple, but dainty
little white porcelain “ Valaze ” pot is not found. Price,
3s. 6d. and 6s., posted 6d. extra. Comtesse Potocka’s
“ Voskpasta,” (3s. jar) “Valaze” Herbal Soap, (2s. cake).

entertainments, 3s. 6d. jar. “ Valaze ” special “Black-
head and Open Pores Cure” guaranteed, 3s. 6d. jar.

8s. 6d. Washington H. Soul, and Civil Service, Sydney,
