MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 1, 1906.
Mr Dear Yarrie. —
The little Governor-General does a hustle now
and then. He was landed into this city by
the Sydney express and a body-guard, one
day, and landed back in Sydney the next
—having snatched a bath, a sleep, and
an hour or two at a ’Varsity function
(some degree-conferring business) in the mean-
time. Nothing slicker than that is on the Aus-
tralian Vice-regal record. In this State we have
an Ex. of great longitude, who takes a week to
make up his mind for a mere glance at the weather
or any other matter of equal consequence. But
Lord Hopetoun was a prompt sort. Even a fall
in the,track of the hounds was a matter of celerity;
his bones were adjusted with despatch, and he
speedily resumed the sport. He didn’t know how
to idle, or saunter, or dally, or leisure. Governor
Clarke was the only one of his successors with any
idea of time-value. The Brassey, Tennyson, and
Talbottle type has the sprightliness of a camel and
the hustling capacity of a silk-worm. It rather
takes pride in its noble and elevated apathy.
The Grey Dame of Stonington sampled “The
Wasps” performance one night last week, as if
she hadn’t enough show of sting and tail from her
Tooraketty neighbors all the year through. Lec-
turer Darnley Naylor, who mostly worked up the
production, is the one distant connection of. the
Apollo Belvedere in the University. When he
first bachelored here and serenaded the Toorak
Tortoise-shells, femininity exclaimed, “A second
Charles Byley!” But he married, and the
serenades were rung off. the llyley
compliments—the highest Toorak knows—came
to an abrupt finish. For a beautiful He to marry
wealth and live happy ever after is a crime past
all forgiveness.
A sensation and a scandal in “’igh life” at last
—after a long depressing immunity from all wicked
excitements ! The principals are a leading Mel-
bourne man and an extra-sweet footlighter. No. 1
principal has a wife—a fact that lends all the
romance to the runaway escapade—the pair having
tripped off to a cosy corner somewhere out in the
wide, wide world. Possibly a divorce is wanted
and the elopement is staged to bring it about.
Another footlighter has landed a golden prize—
Miss Bertham, of the Boyal Comic ranks, a pretty
young thing who had been footlighting for only a
brief season or two. A gilded man offered his
hand and heart and motor and fortune to the
beauty, and on Saturday they faced the Eden
chant m Sydney. The happy pair landed in this
city to board the outgoing liner. It’s up to J. C.
Williamson to charge a premium to the chicks
who flash for a moment on his stage and move the
rich stall-johnnies to prompt proposals, and then
vanish; 2J per cent, on the johnny’s fortune
would be a fair thing. The fashionable Toorakite
grinds her borrowed molars with envy as one
eligible after another is swept into the vortex of
matrimony by charmers from the limelight. The
stage, alike here and in John Bull’s land and in
Sambo’s country, is the surest road to matrimony.
Ihe universal cry of the Birth-Rate promoter is
now, “Go on the stage, young woman ! ”
From latest accounts, artist Longstaff waxes
portly. He is drifting into the middle-age spread,
and loses his early lines of grace. Time was when
the Victorian dauber had only to blink a lid to
have some eye infatuated. Not that he was out
to infatuate. Not in the least. But he had the
eye to coax and command had he been inclined.
He keeps on longing for the Australian sun, and
comes along presently to warm his art at it.
The last appearance of Miss Eileen Castles,
the third of the boomed sisterhood, was faithfully
promised for last Saturday. The young songstress,
who is a slenderer Miss than the other two, with
all the comic opera sister’s dimplesomeness and a
degree more hustle than either Amy or Dolly,
goes to join the fortunate Amy in the Cold
Clime.
From Mr. H. Ward’s account, the performing
daughter of ex-Governor Clarke is going to catch
on as a drawing-room singer of great price. The
voice is not a big one, but it’s packed full of
music, and the little lady called Violet has eyes
to match. They look as melodious as any Men-
delssohn “ Song Without Words.” She always had
those melodious eyes. Tuition was only needed
for the voice. With her maid, Miss Clarke has
been flatting it along with Miss Amy Castles, and
the tuneful young things love each other dearly.
“ A charming pair,” says H. Ward, and he under-
stands the term.
Canon Tucker, of the flowing whiskers, con-
tinues to advance the cult of Muscular Christianity
at South Yarra. In Christ Church and the
adjacent building a young man is supplied with
those things that are requisite and necessary as
well for the body as the soul. If he forgets to
take his weekly allowance of religious exercise he
can do some extra devotions in the gymnasium and
billiard-room. Christ Church celebrated its
jubilee last week in holy rivalry to Melbourne
University and the Eight Hours movement, so
Lord Mayor Weedon, Donald Mackinnon,
M.L.A., and other people assembled and
met together around the genial Canon, and a
new athletic hall was formally added to the
premises of the Church Club. The Tucker in-
stitution is highly estimable in a one-sided way.
It is a^ comfort for anxious mothers to know that
when Tom and Beggie stay out after ten they are
improving their biceps under the immediate
sanction of the church. But if Christian athletics
are intended to benefit humanity in general they
ought to be “mixed,” like the bathing at Frank-
ston. Under present conditions the horizontal
bar distracts young men from the matrimonial
snare, and from the point of view of spinsters who
stay at home with mommer it is almost as danger-
ously alluring as the bar where yellow-haired
sirens pass the bottle. The true social duty of
the Church is to bring about a commingling of the
sexes in gymnastic sports, and make the achieve-
ment of matrimony a graceful muscular effort on
the young woman’s part.
Talking of South Yarra, rumor states that c’d
Mr. Buckhurst, who suddenly departed this life
with a cigar in his mouth the other Sunday after-
noon, has dedicated his stucco mansion on the
Toorak Road to the purpose of a Convalescent
Home (for Melbourne Hospital patients) in the
dim future. Meanwhile, it is to be the home of
the second Mrs. Buckhurst, and as the widow is
still on the youthful side of life, her influential
neighbors are not likely to get excited over the
convalescent question for many years to come.
Whenever chis question may arise, the hospital
trustees will probably be persuaded to take it
into court, and get permission to sell the gaudy
mansion and build with the money a more
suitable and rural place for convalescents. The
late Buckhurst was always so unphilanthropic
in word and deed, that he is naturally sus-
pected of an unpleasant motive in bequeathing
Goodrest ”to the hospital. Not being a man
who loved his neighbors as himself, he may
have rejoiced in the thought that his home
for convalescents would depreciate the pro-
perty of the folk next door. Seemingly the
aged citizen took a delight in disappointing the
hopes of everybody except his young wife, who,
according to rumor, will get the great bulk of his
real and personal estate. He was 70 when he
married the lady (within a year of her predecessor’s
decease) and he rightly reckoned himself a lucky
old gentleman in the transaction ; nevertheless he
couldn’t refrain from “ taking down ” the parson
whom he had engaged to perform the ceremony
at his domicile. As soon as he was made a happy
man the beaming Buckhurst turned to the reverend
officiator and asked “What is your fee?” In a
congratulatory tone of voice the gentle shepherd
replied, “ Whatever you please, Mr. Buckhurst.”
Ihe blissful bridegroom chuckled, and said, “ Oh,
then I suppose I can give you a cigar, for your
trouble.” Again the parson murmured, “ What-
ever j 7 ou please,” whereupon W. P. Buckhurst,
Rsq., took a cigar from his vest pocket and pre-
sented it with a mocking bow—“ There is your
fee.”
Madame Summerhayes and daughter Gertrude
are giving three concerts at the Pianola Hall,
under the patronage of the Talbot dame, who
acknowledged the receipt of the prospectus by
a PP ] ying for tickets. The second concert is fixed
for Thursday (15th) evening, and the third (June
2nd) will be a Saturday matinee.
people are frightfully particular. A
Melbourne woman of 3G lately applied to his
Honor Holroyd for an order to have her birth
registered, her father haying forgotten the arrival
of the new baby at the time. She didn’t explain
why she wanted the registration made ; possibly
she wanted her name put on the electoral rolls,
and the registrar said she didn’t look old enough!
But really she needn’t have troubled Justice
Holroyd, who is an old gentleman with a lot of
work to do. I know 20 women of 36 who would
gladly give away their birth certificates any day.
A wildly - theatrical international incident
cropped up at a Melbourne University reception.
Bittle Russian Dr. Vasilyev pitched himself into
a stream of eloquence, to finish with a passionate
declaration of the cosmopolitan character of
science, and to “ extend the hand of friendship to
my Japanese colleague.” Japanese Ishikawa—■
still smaller than Vasilyev—wriggled like a little
brown streak between the stomachs of fat doctors
of divinity to seize the Russian by the hand and
jerk his arm enthusiastically up and down. It
was the fitting moment for two supers, to unfurl
the Jap and Russian flags, and Professor Peter-
son s orchestra to start two national anthems at
once. But no stage-manager was present, and a
fine tableau opportunity was lost.
How the ’Varsity kept the flag flying is a tale
of some routine afternoon sprees and a few
Arabian-like nights. The ’Varsity Jubilee pro-
vided a week’s wassail, through which one small
Monkey-land curio kept up a nodding that looked
suspiciously like an active outbreak of St. Vitus.
-Every white attention, introduction or comment
set the brown curio on the nod. Some of the
Cream played round, and called him Prof.
Itchycaw, which was as near Ishikawa as they
could get. One ’Varsity wag offered to take on a
few coats of varnish and oak polish if the flutterers
would nutter about him in the same coy manner
The suggestion annoyed the fair things, and
wasn t carried out. The one local nodder on the
precedence roll-call was missing. Vice-Chancellor
Wrixon was laid low with lumbago or some other
souvenir.
The Stonington Dame is wearing glad garments
again. The second period of her mourning has
come to a purple end. At the recent shivoos her
Itx. shone forth in gay habiliments. Her dress
for the University Vice-Chancellor’s tea-fight was
‘h plum-colored velours cut in the new
giddy corselet style a style that commends itself
to an embrace. No fashion has ever suggested a
loving hug in the same eloquent form. There’s
no waist-belt and no pins or buckles. It’s a skirt
built up to the line immediately below the bust
and the waist stands clearly out—well-defined
smooth, inviting. Many a willing maid has lost
an impromptu hug through her perverse fashion
of wearing belts with daggers pointing at a man,
or buckles with stabbing points flaunting deadly
threats. But the corselet rig, leaving the waist-
line free of all obstacle, showing it up in all its
inviting spanfulness, ought to help the marriage-
rate a lot. And it’s quite good form (in more
than the dress sense) since the aristocratic Dame
of Stonington favors it ; though probably she
doesn t favor it for the reasons that occur to the
younger generation.
At that Wrixon party from which t he academic
host was missing a flock of the frou-frou species
aired fresh chiffons. They were mostly cold-
season samples, borrowing tiie hue of the beaver,
the bear, the zebra, and other thick-coated
beasties. Winter apparel is at best a dull matter.
Any vivid material aiming at temperamental
optimism is reckoned “ flash.” It is the thing to
look dull and warm, with just a fleecv tucker at
the neck as a concession to the cleanly effect in
the complexion neighborhood. The hostess tried
hard at a bright result, and got it out of a corn-
flower glace rig, with much whipped cream giving
a trifle effect to the dress-top. But the one miss
of the Wrixon household went right into a dingy
blue, and a black hat only increased the dreari-
ness. Mrs. Clement Vallange and Miss Lesley
Madden were of the house-party, as it were, and
helped to revive bored guests with cherubic smiles.
The dame wore the Lenten hue, with sable as a
substitute for aslicloth. Miss Madden was turned
out in material of rich soil color ; half a rosella
parrot perched on her brown hat. It relieved
things rather.
Mrs. Bevan, the one social twinkfer in this
firmament who never wears an extinguisher of
any millinery shape or form, struck the ’Varsity
spreers as a “ Picture of a Lady” roaming among
the common or garden mortals without her gal-
lery frame. Her laces had been freshened up for
the junkets and were furled and twined round
her head and shoulders with more than usual
care. The vanity of a grey dame who drapes her
snowy top with the vaporish Maltese mesh, and
exercises the utmost discretion in bringing it over
her lovely old shoulders, is excusable. It’s a fair
thing' for beautiful old age to pretend it’s the
Portrait of a Lady ” escaped without its frame
from the gallery collection.
A Ballarat divine—Dr. Cairns—cut a high dash
at the ’Varsity proceedings. He’s one of the
longest of the Cloth in or out of the Anglican
diocese. He has supplies of good humor and
vigor in proportion to his stature, and Ballarat is
proud of all his dash. A little dash is a dangerous
thing. But Dr. Cairns goes in for much dash
and stands a winner.
A rather dowdy section stood sponsor for the
State Government when its turn came along to
keep the ’ Varsity flag flying. There were Tommy,
the round Premier, Mrs. Bleazby (Falstaff’s bona-
fide niece), Dr. Tay Pay M‘lnerney in a Picador
red mantle (a color that fought with his hot north-
wind thatch), Mr. Davies, Mr. and Mrs. Sachse
and Prof. Spencer. These all extended paws to
the company and made the Government of the
country responsible for the shake and the lumbago
caught on the damp lawn.
One family was in mourning for the fetes owing
to the death of Charles Francis, brother of Mrs.
I rank Madden. The deceased was the eldest
son of the late politician who Premiered in the
early ’seventies.
The ’Varsity Sports hop gave students and their
girls an opportunity for a fling. It was the one
giddy spree of the Jubilee outbreak. The hall
had a thousand lights and the hop 32 debutantes.
-No fresh knickerbocker parties arrived to watch
the girl bevy, so the bald-headed Councillors did
dancing duty,. Thirty-two fresh young things
gaily tripping into that small tragedy of the social
bfo the wall-flowered ball-room ! Perhaps when
the programmes slowly filled up with the wobbly
initials of old age some of the 32 realised the
sorrow in store for the hopeful social Miss.
Prof. Peterson’s dame dressed her conductor’s
part for the recent Conservatorium outbreak in
glistening black coat of mail, while the canaries
wore vestal white. The new mistress of the baton
wielded it in white kid gloves, and the pale music
produced suggested kid-glove themes.
A notable dress is in the possession of Miss
x lorence Young. It is a marvellous Limerick lace
triumph valued at three figures—and running well
mto the hundreds at that. It arrived too late for
exhibition at Hand wick, so is to be musked and
lavendered for the Oaks. Fancy wearing a chiffon
treasure of such value and having no fear! It
suggests a fresh field for the pickpocket, who
might pop on the wearer suddenly as her partner
backed a nag, and wrench the £3OO dress clean off.
The Frisco calamity served in a dismal, weird
way as a kind of resurrection event, bringing
back to knowledge the existence of long-forgotten
plat formers. JYliss -Ama/ndst Corcoran is an in-
stance. Nine years ago she took a benefit and
left Australia. Nothing more was heard of the
sovereign-headed warbler (she ran to gold towards
the roof) till the ’quake, when a cable announced
Goldtop’s safety. Only a very few' can recall the
nervey, excitable, fine strummer. This is a clime
of the perpetual benefit, and the send-off is so
habitual that the names of the departers are a
mere blur in the memory.
The wages of wealth, when the end comes, is
trouble. One of the Dives race has been dying
for some weeks, and members of a large mercen-
ary leading family are fighting each other at the
bedside.
The old nobility is so impressive on the stage
that it pains one to learn how differently it some-
times shows in real life. English writer J. B.
.torth, who has been studying portraitures of
winsome” Dorothy Vernon and her relatives,
records that “Dame Dorothy’s face betrays no
sign of beauty, and it is difficult to associate
romance either with her or her melancholy-look-
mg spouse. Her face is peaked, shrewish
even ; one would say, to look at her, that
she ruled her household with a sharp tongue,
fohe is a thin-clieeked, hard-visaged, Calvinistic-
looking woman with tight lips.” Also, the
romantic story of Dorothy’s love affair and elope-
ment seems to have no foundation but the fancy
of a gushing early-Victorian lady novelist,
x urther, the tantrums of the stormy damsel
< a ff ran< 3son who was plainly mad.
‘ That fact is whispered with bated breath,” says
Firth. “ ‘The poor young gentleman, if I may
say so, was not quite right in his head.’ So
spake one of the caretakers to some visitors not
long ago, with a furtive glance around, as though
she were uttering treason to the House of Rutland.
I his respect for even the remote ancestors of the
great family of the neighborhood lingers still in
English country districts.” Even a dead lordling
who was addled in the head is immeasurably
superior to a sane living worker.
A strike of choir singers is an Australian possi-
bility. “We are worse treated than softgoods
apprentices,” said a tolerable shrieker the other
day; “ we’re promised something after a while,
but we never get it. Just a couple are paid (not
a great deal, either), but the rest go on the long
finger and pay-day never comes round. We were
to share in a collection for the choir last Easter ;
but it was the same story as at Christmas—after
paying - two or three at the top, the balance was so
small that we were told it wasn’t worth dividing.
Yes, we have the satisfaction of showing off, no
doubt—the Sunday Miss F. took the high C she
had an offer of marriage on the way home. But
you tire waiting for such chances.”
The fact that Melbourne University Jubilee
people exhibited, among other congratulatory
addresses, one from Tokio University, which was
put on view upside down, is said to have been
paralleled by a Sydney leader of society who, for
months after the departure of the Jap Admiral’s
fleet, displayed a framed capsized letter in
Japanese thanking her for various kind attentions
A couple of Japs who saw the letter thought its
inverted attitude was intentional and carried
some social significance, and forbore to remon-
strate. It was only their struggles to get upside
down and peruse the document that at length
caused suspicion to .-Lf in 1 1- 6
The local girleen is just now keen on working
out chords and threads of melody from the ivories,
and when she does this she calls herself a com-
poser. One of her has broken out with alleged
waltzes, but forgets to put the time in. Now if
ouly called the composition “Wallflowers”
or ‘ Sunset, ” or “Ma Honeycomb ” all would be
well. As long as she doesn’t dub it waltz or
polka, the uninitiated are none the wiser. But
give an effort the waltz label, and the smallest of
the bib-and-tucker or knickerbocker tribe says :
Ices, but where’s the time?” The next girleen
who works out a tune on the ivories, marking
this, should christen her effort vaguely a “Chan-
son ” or a “ Rondo,” or merely “Melody in L.”
Yours Affectionately,
TRYPHENA.
Calculated that Britain spends every year
£18,000,000 on foreign pork, Freetrade and land
monopoly having brought the country down to the
point at which it can’t even keep its own hogs.
“Lone Female”: I am disappointed in
J. C. Watson. He is not at all the sort of person
1 imagmed a Socialist Labor member should be.
Why he looks far more like a gentleman than Mr.
Keid ! He speaks more correctly than Mr. Reid.
He doesn’t say, “ Will yer ” and “ pianner,” like
■^ r * Perhaps when Mr. Reid lectured us
poor bush women on the horrors of Socialism, he
just spoke like that to put us at our ease—like the
beautiful anecdote of Our Gracious King eating
peas with his knife to make his poor ignorant
guest feel at home. Nevertheless, the fact re-
mains that Watson looks, speaks, and acts like a
perfectly respectable and well-conducted member
of society. And if he is the rabid monster who is
to tear from private enterprise its hard-earned
gains, shatter the marriage tie, and profane the
sanctity of the home, he ought to be compelled by
Act of Parliament to look more like the character.
Papua has already cost the Australian
Government £300,000, and the only “satisfaction”
we have got for the money is to see an English
Johnny administration spoiling the chances of the
Possession.
The A.N.A. is frenziedly crying out for
the establishment of an Australian school of
sculpture. Its patriotic young members might
stop and ask themselves what the sculptors are to
do when the school has turned them out. There
are already plenty of capable sculptors in Aus-
tralia who cannot make the wages of day laborers
from the sale of statues. The majority of them
live by teaching drawing to hopeless infants, or
instructing fashionable damsels how to burn
pictures on bits of wood, or to punch sheets of
brass into something resembling flowers. One
of the best earns a living cooking chops
m a Melbourne restaurant. Not only has
the Australian sculptor a hard row to hoe in
regard to commissions, but the State galleries do
not give him even the slight encouragement they
give to painters. There is not one production by
a local sculptor in the Melbourne National
Gallery, though £5OO of the Felton money would
go a long way towards getting some excellent
works representative of local men. Douglas
Richardson’s “Octopus” and Gilbert’s “Nautilus”
are worthy to grace any gallery in the world, but
the Vic. trustees didn’t even consider the question
of their purchase. Mackennal is, of course, repre-
sented, but, then, he is not an Australian any
longer. He has received the stamp of Hinglish
approval, and become an Englishman in resi-
dence, sentiment, and everything. To reach the
heart of the Gallery trustees it is necessary to
speak through the ultra-Hinglish ear of director
Bernard Hall, and nobody who doesn’t wear the
trousers of his art turned up at the feet of his
intelligence can do that.


POLICY-HOLDERS in the AUSTRA-
LIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT Society
should vote for ARTHUR GRAHAM WALD,
the South Australian Candidate, who has
had 18 years of practical experience of Life
Assurance in Australasia, and favors: —The
Election of Local Boards by each State; an In-
dependent State Audit of the Accounts; a Reduc-
tion in Interest on Overdue Premiums from 8 per
cent, to 5 per cent. The Interest on Loans on
Policies to be Reduced to a Uniform Rate of 5 per
cent, per annum ; the Status of the Canvassing
Staff to be Greatly Improved, and whilst favoring
the Extension of the Society to the United King-
dom and British South African Colonies, would
strongly oppose any further extension.

POLITICAL
POINTS
It was Lever, the Sunlight Soap man, who
moved in the British Commons the resolution
that members should be paid £3OO a year. The
motion was carried by 348 to 110.
What is N.S.W. Government doing about
its 500 or so kanakas said to be scattered round
the northern rivers ? The bulk of those who have
crossed the border to N.S.W. would, if in Queens-
land, be deported this year.
An Australian navy finds favor anywhere
except among a certain foaming section of “loyal”
Australians. Admiral C. P. Fitzgerald (it is
cabled) urges in the National Review the en-
couragement of locally-controlled navies in all the
British countries.
The latest Chow prosecuted in Melbourne
under the Factories Act, was ironing shirts at 2
a.in. Said he didn’t know the time, but had got
up to make an early start, and that he was ignor-
ant of the law. All the same, the innocent
heathen gave a wrong name.
An old farm-burner wrote to the S.M.
Herald last week to suggest the despatch of an
Australian contingent to help Natal to shoot its
nigger. But the contingenting fire burns very
low just now. Even vigorous blowing hardly
raises a spark among the ashes.
March 31st saw the end of the first three
months’ experience of the new N.S.W. Liquor
Act. The Act was supposed to reduce drinking,
but the quarter’s liquor revenue was considerably
more than that of the first three months of 1905.
As a result, there is a certain amount of gloom in
Cold Tea circles.
The joke of the month has been a written
offer made to the Victorian Government by a per-
son in England, who is willing to “ lay out a new
sea-side town.” All he asks is_ rations, a few
tools, a hut to live in, and a few acres of land
“from Port Phillip heads to the boundary of
N.S.W.” Premier Bent has burst two waistcoats
with laughter.
For 30 days Nicholas Hart’s case against
the Australian Government for alleged wrongful
dismissal from the post office was before Justice
Real and a jury at Brisbane. The wealth of
detail was bewildering. The jury came back to
court with a verdict for the Commonwealth. The
bill of costs looks heavy enough to swamp a bank.
J ury fees alone run to £2OO.
THE BEGINNING OF THE END.
The Labor party has announced in its own brutally
picturesque language its final determination to “put its
mercenaries to the sword.” —Daily paper.
GEORGE : “ Well , that’s a breather, anyhow.”
L.B.M.: “ Only for a little while. You’re next.”
The Commonwealth has dropped the silly
idea of appointing a Crown Solicitor in Sydney at
£4OO a year, with no right of private practice.
Now it is running after the almost-as-silly idea of
getting the N.S.W. Crown Law Office to do the
work. Surely the Commonwealth knows its
Joseph, and knows what sort of service it
would get from a man controlled by Carruthers ?
George Reid has evidently only seen the
Ten Commandments in the distance. When he
was taunted recently with having a purely nega-
tive policy, he replied that the Decalogue also con-
sisted of negatives. Which it doesn’t —not quite.
Most of the commandments begin with “ Thou
shalt not,” but there are exceptions—including
the commandment against making bad jokes on
Sunday.
William Redmond lately asked Winston
Churchill in the House of Commons if the coinage
of silver would be allowed in Australia. The
answer given was, “ The question of the intro-
duction of a special silver coinage in Australia,
which has been under consideration for some time,
will be further discussed verbally with Mr. John
Forrest who recently arrived m England.” We
don’t seem to be getting much further forward.
“Itan”: The Bulletin
rose in a disorderly way and
remarked the other day that
the Brisbane Daily Wail was
only happy when bathed in
woe, and that its pall was
always rugging it closely; also
that it seemed to have no large
quantity of hope stored about
the office. Wrong. A ray of
brightness entered its soul
quite recently. It discovered
that it was quite common, as
proved by history, for people
to go back with enthusiasm to
politicians they had rejected,
and to yank them again into
power. So it saw a chance for
I’hilp. After that, stirring
leaders appeared about the
“handwriting on the wall,”
and stating that Kidston’s
Government was “discredited,”
that its members were
“doomed,” and a large number
of other slops to the same
effect. Nothing happened, but
there is still a labored cheer-
fulness in the Wail office about
Bob Philp’s prospective return
to his lost job.
Premier Bent has placed,
besides others, these Bills on
his list for the comingsession:—
Indeterminate Sentences,
Boiler Inspection, Scaffolding
ditto, Criminal Law Codifica-
tion, Butter Export Grading,
Met. Board of Works Reform,
Agricultural College. After
which, when the Assembly has
shaped the measures m a fairly
democratic fashion, the House of Fat can be
trusted to knock them end-ways, as usual.
The Russian Government is a humorous
animal. It has just finished a disastrous war, and
is still in the throes of what may yet be a revolu-
tion ; and yet it finds time to send Australia a
quiet note to the effect that it is willing to ex-
change official publications relating to land
matters, agriculture, and the treatment of the
cow. A Government that can keep as cool as that
while the empire rocks under it will take a lot of
shifting.
Dr. Graham, once a prop of the N.S.W.
Foreign-trade party, has returned to Sydney after
a trip to the Cold Country. His opinion of the
late British election is that the electors went to
the polls with a settled determination to down
the Balfour Government, first, because of the
Education Act, and, second, because of the im-
portation of Chinese into Africa. Freetrade did
not, apparently, occur to the doctor as worth
mentioning.
In his recent speech at Parkes (N.S.W.)
Yes-No Reid made two striking statements. One
was that he “ favored closer settlement, but on
condition that a man’s land should be honestly
and straightforwardly taken, and paid for fairly.”
The other, a little later, was that he opposed
Labor-leader Watson’s proposal to buy and pay
for big estates, because “it would cost an enor-
mous sum, and he couldn’t see where the money
was to come from.”
Natal Government, yelling that the British
Empire is in danger if Bambaata be not captured,
has offered £5OO for the body (alive or dead) of that
warrior, who is an open enemy in the field ; and
£2O apiece for the heads of his followers. It was
because “ the Empire was in danger” that the
Boer children died like flies in the concentration
camps. For the same reason now an open enemy
is to be fought with blood-money. Oh Empire,
what crimes are committed in thy name !
One of Joey Carruthers’ reasons for seces-
sion seems to have died a natural death. The
reason was something about the Governor-Gene-
ral’s tenancy of Sydney Government House —-
either he was there and shouldn’t be, or he wasn’t
there and should be ; anyhow, Joey threatened
violent ejectment, and breathed long blue flames
through his nose. The tenancy, however, has
been renewed by peaceful agreement, and on this
matter, at all events, Joseph snorts no more.
N.S.W. Deform Leaguer Stinson has
been talking again. His latest yelp is to
tbe effect that the destruction of the mar-
riage tie, the battering down of the sanc-
tity of the home, and the blowing up of all
respect and love for parents, is the natural and
logical outcome of “Continental” Socialism.
Apparently poor old Stinson is mixed up between
Continental Socialism and Continental bathing,
and knows little of either.
After a brief career, mostly devoted to
hard borrowing, Cornthwaite Hector Rason has
decided to fly from the Westralian Premiership
and appoint himself to a reasonably “stiddy job”
as Agent-General. Probably no one save himself
would have dreamt of Rason as an Agent-General,
and he had great luck in getting the Premiership
by accident and thus securing the chance to point
out to himself his own eminent fitness for the
A.G.’s position. With the exception of Coghlan,
of N.S.W., who went to England on his merits,
the A.G.’s nowadays are mostly rather lamentable
politicians who were badly in want of a “stiddy
job ” and got it by the pulling of wires. Newton
Moore (rather a barren Moore by all accounts) is
expected to get the Westralian Premiership if he
can reach up to it.
It is beautifully easy for irresponsible
State Premiers to give advice to the Prime
Minister, and then wonder why he doesn’t follow
it. At the recent conference, it was decided by a
majority that the Federal surplus should in future
be divided among the States according to popula-
tion. The majority consisted of Queensland,
S.A., and Tasmania—the three States that would
profit by the new arrangement; the minority was
N.S.W. and Victoria, which would lose by it.
Westralia wasn’t represented, but it would also
lose by the proposed system, and it has tele-
graphed a red-hot curse on the subject. The
trouble now is that the three States which formed
a majority at the Premiers’ Conference, and ex-
plained so cheerfully how things should be done,
are a poor little minority in the Australian Par-
liament, where things are done. So the advice
they gave to Deakin is of very little use to that
gentleman. Nevertheless, the State Premiers
went home proudly saying, “See how quickly
We can fix things up when We get on the job.”
THE DUBIOUS RELATION.
If I explain what Socialism is, and then tell you I’m
Anti, the thing’s as clear as daylight.—Reid at Dubbo.
“ Don't you know me ? I’m your anti-Sosh! ”
N.S.W. anti-Sosh M.L.A. Dan Levy is
just now making a determined effort to have the
Darlinghurst-Potts Point line of Government
motor ’buses, recently withdrawn as unpayable,
reinstated. But where is good old private enter-
prise? Why doesn’t it step in and give Levy’s
constituents the benefit of its superior manage-
ment. By the way, where are all those motor
’buses that were going to settle the trams in one
act ? Are they all waiting to see how the Govern-
ment ventures pan out ?
Thus Senator (until next election) Drake
in his electioneering rag, which he has the
effrontery to call the Commonwealth :
At the solicitation of certain politicians, who wanted a
couple of years in office, and were willing to barter their
political souls to get it, Mr. Deakin “ come-hither’d ” the
Labor party.
“ Come-hithering ” is a subject that Drake should
understand. He was “come-hither’d” from
Queensland Liberalism into a Tory pro-Kanaka
Ministry ; from that into a White Labor Protec-
tionist Federal Ministry ; from that into a black-
and-tan Freetrade Federal Ministry ; and, next,
he is going to be “ come-hither’d” into the politi-
cal dust-bin.
Magistrate Panton, of Melbourne, has
long been regarded as a judicial humorist, and
his professed ignorance of the Aliens’ Immi-
gration Act can only be put down to the
aged gentleman’s curious brand of wit.
When the Crown Law officer explained the
statute, Panton indignantly demanded : “ Is
this corner of the British Empire allowed to
exclude British subjects who have been living
here, on their return, if they should go
away?” Being informed that such was the
law—a fact which Mr. Panton should have
been perfectly well aware of, that august
Rhadamanthus burst out: “It is a position
which no British subject should permit to
exist. Such an Act should nevek have
RECEIVED THE ROYAL ASSENT.” And yet, till
this hideous indiscretion showed him in his
true revolutionary, red-Republican colors,
everybody regarded Panton as a right-
thinking pillar of the Throne and Person. It
is understood that the news that Panton has
disapproved of his action in assenting to the
Bill will be carefully concealed from King
Ned.
The recent Australian Manufacturers’
Exhibition in Melbourne having wound up
with a profit, the promoters promptly
returned to Treasurer Bent the £l5O uncon-
ditionally granted towards financing the
show. So much for the ways of the manu-
facturing interests. Now, did anyone ever
hear of the primary producer, in the shape
of the agricultural show, handing back the
amount of the State subsidy which it had received,
when the show turned out a profit ?
Something extra in the way of chilled
cheek transpired t’other day during an interview
between Chief Secretary Hogue and representa-
tives of the N.S.W. Nurserymen’s Association,
concerning the trade of Lord Howe Island. The
graceful Kentia palms are indigenous to the
island, and Sydney seedsmen had been giving
12s. fid per bushel for seeds of the plant. Lately
an English firm stepped in with an offer of from
£2 to £2 10s. per bushel and secured a monopoly
of the trade for seven years. Hence the deputa-
tion, introduced by that staunch barracker for
Private Enterprise—Broughton, M.L.A. The
monopoly it was stated would kill the N.S. Wales
export trade—not the Lord Howe Island export
trade, be it noted. Mr. Hogue in reply remarked
that the people principally concerned were the
islanders, numbering about 100. The sale of the
seed was their source of livelihood, and they
were quite justified in selling their produce to the
best advantage. He thought the seven Cyears
arrangement would have to stand, as he knew of
no power to break it.
“Hal”: I was sitting beside a parson,
waiting for the beginning of the Reid-Holman
debate, and he started talking fiscalism. “Are
you a Freetrader?” I asked. “ Why, of course I
am,” he replied, in a surprised way. “lam a
Christian , and a Christian must be a Freetrader .”
The statistics of the two “ Made in Aus-
tralia ” exhibitions, in the two largest capitals,
give some sort of idea of Sydney’s stupid bias
against Australian goods. Melbourne receipts
totalled £6369 ; balance after paying all expenses,
£3003. Sydney received but a paltry £328, leaving
a deficit of £1676.
Cablegram from London :
The Daily Mail has published a contributed article
alleging that secular education has caused a great
increase of crime in Victoria, and that the results
throughout Australia are deplorable and absolutely
shocking.
After all, great daily journalism in London seems
to be more ignorant and incompetent than a good
deal of the journalism one meets in the second-
rate towns of Australia. Anyone would expect an
editor, when he had a large statement like that
contributed to him, to look up, either personally
or by deputy, one or two back numbers of, say,
the Statesman’s Year Book, and find out whether
his contributor spoke the truth, or whether, as in
this instance, he was merely trying to use the
paper as a vehicle for scattering huge, virulent
lies in order to discredit the secular education
proposals of the Bannerman Government. Ap-
parently the Daily Mail is run by cheap incom-
petents, who never think of a simple precaution
like that.
“Priam,” from Melbourne :
Victorian Railway Commissioners have perpetrated a
piece of the coolest possible insolence with regard to the
Gembrook railway smash. After that instructive event
the Government appointed a court, consisting of a Judge
with expert advisers, and that court decided that the
permanent way was in a dangerous condition,
expressing an opinion that the maximum speed allowed
was excessive. Bent “ formally ” submitted these find-
ings to the Commissioners, and they replied with a
long report, in which they set at defiance the
decision of the court, and loaded all the blame of
the accident upon the engine-driver by declaring
themselves “ firmly convinced ” that he drove the
train at express speed. The Court observed that the-
maximum speed allowed by the Commissioners (15 miles
an hour) was risky considering the state of the line. The
Commissioners reduced the speed temporarily, but now
announce that thev will revert to the old velocity. Such
insolent flouting of the instructions of a properly-consti-
tuted Court on the part of persons who are practically on
trial in the matter, should not be tolerated.
The statement by the Western Morning
News (England) about how, owing to secular
education, crime and vice are leaping forward
with grand strides in Australia, represents a lie
that is especially frequent with the clergy (“I
find clergymen more disingenuous and unreliable
even than women in the witness box” is the
dictum of a well-known barrister ), but it has not
an atom of real foundation. On the contrary, as
Mr. Coghlan has pointed out in London, with the
spread of secular education there has been a
marked decline in Australian crime. Most serious
crime in Australia nowadays is committed by im-
ported criminals, or by natives who have not come
under the influence of the secular schools; and
the religious schools compare very unfavorably
with the State schools in the way of turning out
criminals.

taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. ivill be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

LADIES,
The Secret op a Good Complexion is Good
Digestion.
The story of a great deal of the unhappiness of
women is a story of lost health. Women wonder
how it is that little by little their forms lose
plumpness, the cheeks grow hollow and sallow,
and they feel tired and worn out all the time.
In a large proportion of cases when women are
weak, run down, and falling-off in flesh and looks,
the root of the trouble can be traced to indigestion
and other stomachic disorders which undermine
the general health. Health alone can produce a
fair skin, which is the secret of beauty. If your
digestion is bad your complexion must suffer. If
your digestion is perfect your complexion will
need no artifice to hide its imperfections. There-
fore it behoves every woman who regards her good
looks with that jealousy characteristic of her sex,
to study her digestion. Properly assimilated
food yields the maximum of nutriment which goes
to make new blood and replenish the, blood cells
of the skin. Indigestion causes all manner of ills
that taint the blood with decayed matter,
interferes with the circulation, and consequently
does not impart to the complexion the influence
of pure and healthy blood. Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills purify the blood and aid in the digestion
and assimilation of {food, whereby the system is
nourished and replenished with new blood, rich
and red, which circulating through the veins gives
the complexion a fresh and pure appearance,
causing the cheeks to glow with the ruddy tint
of perfect health.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The struggle between Glebe and Burwood for
the first-grade championship of Sydney metropolitan
cricket was resumed on Saturday amidst keen excite-
ment. Glebe was exercising its skill on University, and
Burwood was using Waverley as a block on which to chop
out its destiny. At the close of play the previous week
University had been dismissed for 127, and Glebe had
rushed up to 76 for the loss of one wicket only. Last
Saturday saw Glebe resume its place at the sticks punc-
tually, and play with feverish energy. The balls could
not come quickly enough, and Bardsley batted in baresark
style, rushing out to meet everything that came along,
and endeavoring to hit it to Hades. His partners were
just as enthusiastic, but a lot less effective, and it was
Bardsley’s 104 that enabled Glebe to close its innings
with seven wickets down for 220.' With the exception of
Buckle, who made 22, none of the other batsmen reached
a score. Glebe bowling was as savage as Bardsley’s
batting, and University’s second innings began with
disaster and ended in collapse. The first four wickets
went for nine runs, and after that they fell at 42, 49, 63,
67, and 72, thus leaving Glebe winner by an innings and
21 runs. This three-point win was due to the bowling of
Bowden, who took eight Uni. wickets for 32 runs.
* * * *
Burwood was not so successful in its struggle
with Waverley : the chopping-block was ever so much
harder. The previous week it had got rid of Waverley for
154 and had lost two wickets for 72. Sunny Jim Mackay
played the Goliath in the Burwood batting, and his
smacking up of 158 (not out) was a mighty performance,
without which Bin-wood’s address would have been No.
13 Queer-street. The only other batsman to shine was L.
P. Deane, who made 45. With 261 for nine wickets
down, the Burwood captain determined to risk it, and
declared his innings closed. Now came the crisis. If
Waverley could be summarily disposed of before scoring
107, the premiership was won—otherwise it might be a
ease of losing it to Glebe next Saturday, or sharing the
honor. Sid. Gregory and H. Carter opened for Waverley,
and lashed out in fine style ; 69 were scored before Carter
was caught by Prentice off O’Connor, and Alphabetical
Mackenzie joined the great little Sid. Mac., too, was in
a slogging- mood, and banged 10 fives and a six.
Gregory’s 56 included nine fours. At the drawing of
stumps Waverley had scored 142 for the loss of one
wicket, thus leaving Burwood winners by 101 runs on the
first innings. But Waverley went home satisfied—it had
prevented Burwood scoring a three-point win and getting
the premiership.
The direct result of Burwood’s failure to score a
straight-out win was to place it and Glebe level at the
head of the competition list with 19 points each. The
second place on the list is held by Waverley with seven
points, and behind it Redfern and North Sydney toil with
five and four respectively. The other seven clubs are all
something less than nothing, Middle Harbor being at the
bottom of the list with 12 below zero. What will happen
is not known at time of going to press. Owing to the
difficulty of securing a ground for the play-off it is
possible that Glebe and Burwood may share the premier-
ship ; but a decisive struggle between the two leading,
clubs would be much more satisfactory.
* * « •
Wicket-keeper Carter made a fine effort
against Burwood, stumping four batsmen, and allow-
ing but eight sundries to be recorded against
Waverley. Carter’s form throughout the season has been
decidedly good, and generally gives the impression that
the Australian XI. billet will fall to him. The only
thing against Carter is that he is small and the bumping
of wild trundler Cotter is likely to lay him out; still in
inter-State matches he outshone all other “ stumpers.”
* * . » *
A feature of the just ended cricket season was
the slumping of Noble and Victor Trumper. The former
did not score a single century, his highest being 69 ; and
Trumper only managed one meek little century, which,
for a man of his rank, was trumpery indeed, although
there are others who declare that it was anything but so.
* * * *
An inspection of the first-grade cricket averages
for the past season shows how unreliable these stereo-
typed statistics are. Of the leading dozen in the batting
list, only three—Mackay, Diamond and Trumper—were
thought good enough to be picked for the match against
Victoria. Two men with averages of over 60 were passed
by, and also half-a-dozen in the 50’s. The position of
Mackay is extraordinary—l 2 innings, 972 runs, 3 not outs,
and an average of 108. Should he score a few in the
return match against Glebe, he will have made 1000 in
grade matches alone. He only wants five to notch 2000
in all matches for the season ; and if he scores 50 he will
defeat Trumper’s present record for N.S.W. grade cricket.
In bowling, the season’s averages also belie the selection
committees. Of the leading 10, Garnsey is the only inter-
state trundler to get a place.
* .* * *
Sydney football of all sorts is just now in the
uninteresting stage—like the egg that is being sat upon
by the prosaic barn-door hen. Practice matches are
being played every Saturday afternoon, and the cubs are
getting somewhat licked into shape. This metaphor is
mixed but so is the football ! As far as Rugby
is concerned there seems to be a woeful lack of proper
instruction, and although Soccer men and Australian
gamers are to be found practising in little groups in the
open spaces in the early mornings, there’s never a sign of
Rugbyites practising passing and dribbling. And yet
Rugby is the one game in which hard individual practice
and good tuition are indispensable. In the days when
Otago dominated Maoriland football a horde of savage
instructors would have the lame ducks of their clubs out
at day-break on a muddy field, racing them up
and down through the slush with the wind
and sleet in their faces, • bellowing admonitions and
curses at them as they ran. Writer worked on a mountain
survey once, and climbed hills like the slope of a Gothic
gable with the camp-oven wrapped in his blankets,
strapped to his shoulders. But even that was child’s
play to the way a savage old Highlander, in kilts and a
“ bonnet,” used to make him toil on those awful morn-
ings on the Asylum Ground. Granted that the mornings
in Australia are hot for that sort of practice, with a day’s
graft sticking- up beyond ; but it is not necessary to do it
more than three mornings a week for a month or so.
And it is the royal road to success at Rugby. At present
all the club practice seems to be carried on at nights, and
to consist chiefly of running and physical culture. It is
good, no doubt, bub steady practice in the light at pass-
ing charges and dribbling rushes is infinitely better, and,
as far as this writer.can ascertain, University Club is the
only one that is having daylight training.
Sydney Metropolitan Rugby competition starts
this ensuing Saturday, and the clubs that seem likely to
get off the mark with some suddenness are South Sydney,
University and Glebe. The University men have been
going in-for a lot of out-door practice in the afternoons
and evenings while the sun was hovering over the tops of
the Moreton Bay figs, and the result is that they have
combination. On the other hand the South Sydney team,
with its legs strengthened by ploughing its weary home-
ward waj r through the sandhills, is a formidable rival;
and on top of the lot comes the tomato-tinted jersey of
Glebe, which represents solid weight, a pretty turn for
dribbling rushes, and speed that is not by any means to
be scoffed at. The new system under which the competi-
tion will be conducted causes much more interest to
centre in the first games of the new season than in
previous years, for these early games will decide in which
class a club will play during the second part of the
season.
Vic. Football League, besides trying to popu-
larise the Australian game in States where Rugby has
hitherto been the rule, is starting on the long road that
leads to international contests and tours abroad. Besides
drawing Roosevelt’s attention to the beauties of the
game, as played by Collingwood and “ South,” it ha*
circularised 69 universities and colleges in America to that
effect. In the circular the League modestly remarks that
in nearly every department of sport its countrymen have
achieved fame—cricket, rowing, running, tennis, boxing,
&c. If that doesn’t cause the Eagle to scream, then the
bird is moulting.
•* * * •
Melbourne University Sports Union, after much
travail, has gathered together a football team and become
a unit in the Metropolitan Juniors, where some of the
best football in Melbourne is played. The ’Varsity team,
however, aspires to something higher, and in a lett.er
to the Victorian Football League requested that body to
graciously admit its team into its fold. The League, some
years ago, pitched out all the weaker clubs and reduced
itself to eight. Since then its gates have been phenomenal,
and the League has never been stronger than it is at
present. In the circumstances, the University’s request
was regarded as humorous, and was unanimously refused.
The only point the University could urge in its own favor
was that the team would be strictly amateur—a rather
peculiar inducement to offer the Victorian League, which
is notoriously professional. When the University has
built up a big football fund, and can buy the best players
in the League ranks, and beat the best teams in the League,
it can come and knock at the door again, and perhaps it
will be opened—but only if the Uni. team is too strong
to be kept out. What close corporation ever willingly
admitted an outsider to share in its spoils ?
* * * *
The country football teams, hitherto well treated
by the N.S.W. Rugby Union, won’t have such a good time
during the coming winter. The country will be divided
into five districts, which means that only five country
teams will be brought down to Sydney. Considering
that the combined country team more than held its own
against the city last year, this whittling away of the
“country football week” isn’t deserved.
■*•*•■*■*
The unexpected victory of Ireland in the last
Rugby international of the British season robbed Wales
of a record. If Taffy had won, he would have scored all
the internationals for two seasons running ; and that has
never yet been done. However, he has the win against
the Maorilanders as consolation.
* * * it
‘•Jimmy” Duncan, coach of the Maoriland
football team, has been talking about referees to inter-
viewers in M.L. “ Referees in England will never im-
prove. .. The referee comes on the field of play in white
shirt and collar, and cannot, and does not, follow the
players about. That is the reason the Maorilanders
were disallowed the try scored against Wales.” Accord-
ing to Jimmy, the haw is an awful thing in English sport;
and the football referee evidently regards a spotless
white shirt and collar as the most important part of the
match.
«-*•»*
“ J. Shay ” : Printed that the football players of
the local British fleet have had enough of the tars of the
U.S.S. Baltimore. In a match, the Yankee Doodles
played American Rugby, which consists mostly of rushes,
“like mad bulls at a gate,” and wild plunging, trampling
performances of a deadly description, all boots, breast-
bone, wooden head, and elbows. When a big American
footballer “ bucks centre ” he is expected to trample on
somebody’s ear, and the side that tramples on the most
ears wins. There is a story of an American footballer in
South America who broke up a dangerous and very
influential conspiracy against the Government—he was a
friend of the President and wanted to do him a good turn
—by uttering his college yell and rushing the conspiracy
on football principles. When he had finished yelling,
bucking, elbowing, butting, kicking, dancing, and
trampling, the astonished sedition hadn’t a leg left to
stand on, except the two with which its chief was light-
ing out for the horizon,
Maoriland is making another spasmodic effort
to grapple with the elusive crook who infests the pro-
fessional running paths. At present sports grounds are a
verdant field for the fleet-footed stranger from afar. One
of him turned up at Gore recently, carrying on his
person indications of a preparation unknown among
maiden runners. The local peds. did not feel equal to
winning, so they decided to instruct their friends
accordingly, and without acquainting the visitor of their
intentions. After gathering in all the available cash, the
bookmakers made it clear to the visitor that it would
be infra dig. or something to beat these honest Maori-
landers on their own fern-flat. The stranger jumped
away like a kangaroo, and in a few strides had ten yards
to spare. He then dropped back for company, and
allowed one of the others to pass him. Nearing the tape
the leader fell. The visitor courteously stopped to pick
him up, while the other competitors gathered round to
inquire if the fallen man had broken his back or his front
or something. The judges are still waiting for someone
to breast the tape.
* * * *
Inter-’Varsity sports at the University C.G.
(Melb.), on April 25th, provided some interesting contests
among Sydney, Adelaide, Tasmanian and Melbourne
students, especially in'the quarter-mile. French (Sydney)
won that eventall the way, beating Norman Barratt (Vic.)
by 4yds. in 511-5 sec.—excellent time on heavy going.
Sydneyite R. G. Waddy captured the mile from E. P.
Oldham (Melb.), with Byds. to spare, in 4min. 41see. The
100yds. fell to O. R. Dowdy (Adelaide), and the half-mile
to Barratt (Melb.). For the Inter-’Varsity championship,
Sydney won with three points, Adelaide second with 2£,
Melbourne, with lj came third, and Tasmania was nil.
Moyes (Adelaide) and Lawton (Melb.) provided some.first-
class jumping, making a dead heat at sft. BJin. Both
failed at. sft. 9Jin., after a plucky exhibition. Welch
(Sydney), Australasian amateur champion, failed early.
• * * *
Either A. B. Postle is Duffey in disguise, or
there is something grievously wrong with Westralian
clocking. The “ Kalgoorlie Pet” has just smashed
another world’s record by covering 130vds. in 12sec., in
a handicap at Menzies (W.A.). Postle’s challenge
to the world is bringing out Englishman B. R.
Day, and a match, for a £lOO purse and £lOOO side wager
will be decided at Kalgoorlie in August. Somehow, The
Bulletin cannot help reaching for the salt when that
£lOOO side wager is mentioned.
* * * *
Professional athletics are booming in Sydney.
The N.S.W. Athletic League has 105 clubs and 1393 peds.
nestling under its wing. The League recently held a con-
ference on its rules, and started the end of the boom by a
. senseless rule inflicting a fine “ not exceeding £2, with the
alternative of disqualification for 12 months” for offences.
It would be a mild piece of “stiff” running that
wouldn’t repay £2 with interest. Ikey Mo. is also to
■ be permitted to aid the cronkness for registra-
tion fee of ss. A better way to keep the sport clean
would be to provide for compulsory wiping out not under
12 months for offences, and to restrict betting as far as
possible by a higher annual fee upon Ikey Mo. At pre-
sent, there are too many “ books ” who pay the ss. fee,
and too many books enlarges the competition to have the
favorites “ in the bag.” The more bags there are the
more competitors are likely to be in them.
* * ♦ *
Although the Australians at the Olympic
Games are not setting the “ City of the Violet Crown ” on
fire, they are at least figuring among the front-rankers.
Cecil Healy finished second in his heat of the 100 metres
(about 110yds.) swimming championship of the world, but
in the final was unable to stay with Yankee streaker O.
M. Daniels, who won, or de Halmay, the Hungarian, who
shot home second. Still, even third in such distinguished
company is no disgrace to the Australian representati e.
It was nearer than the Englishman, Derbyshire, could
In the running, Nigel Barker was not up to his local form.
He was third in the final of the 100 metres foot race, but
woke up in his heat of the 400 metres, which he won in
53sec. He left the track favorite for the final, which had
not been decided when this issue went to press. The 400
metres is just a trifle less than a quarter mile, and
Barker’s record for that distance is 48£sec., so either
Nigel was not seriously pushed and took things easily,
or he has gone off. Meanwhile the brethren are re-
quested to pray that it was the former.
Hackenschmidt has evidently got rid of the
housemaid’s knee on his elbow, for the cable tells that
Madrali, the “Terrible Turk’’ and the “Russian Lion
met at the Olympia Hall, London, last week, in the
presence of 4000 people. The Russian commenced with
his usual hurricane act (which he always lets loose
when he means serious business), and after ljmin.
wriggling the “ Terrible ” was overthrown. Either Hacky
tired himself somewhat in that first rush or Madrali
learned something, for in the second act it took
the Russian 4min. to place the Turk’s shoulders
against the mat. Still Mahomet got there. Hack, is
now undisputed champion of the world ; even
the Rev. Oarkeek won’t deny it any longer. The
present contest is a much more satisfactory one
than that of 1904, when Hack, beat Madrali for the
first time. Then the Turk agreed to wrestle Grseco-
Roman style rather than forego the contest, and was
whirled off his feet, and bashed down on the floor
with an injured arm before he had time to think.
This time Hackenschmidt, who had become pro-
ficient at it in the intervening years, met Madrali at
his own style, and the Turk proved to be but little better
at his old catch-as-catch-can game than he was at his new
Graico-Roman.
* * * *|
Stanbury has at last agreed to row Towns for
the championship of the world, and incidentally for £5OO
a-side. The distance has been fixed at 3 miles 330yds.,
the date July 30th, and Michael Rush and A. H, Fitz-
hardinge have been agreed upon as umpire and judge
respectively. The only detail that has not been settled
is the course—that will be fixed when the gentry who own
the foreshores have been brought to terms. Both Towns
and Stanbury are determined that the ’longshore pro-
prietors who make money by charging for a sight of the
race shall shell out pro rata. And both Stanbury and
Towns are determined fellows where the boodle is con-
cerned.
* * * *
So far there is no word of the authorities having
declared the Ancient Scullers’ Match, which the Sydney
Sunday Sun is promoting, a dangerous performance ; and
as anybody who hints that it is unsafe is immediately
challenged by some one of the old buffers to run ten
miles, or fight the buffer 20 rounds with bare knuckles,
the chances are that it will eventuate. The ancients are
understood to be feeding on raw meat and beef tea, and
taking preliminary training ; and if they keep at it long
enough, no doubt the thing will be run off without any-
body bursting his aged and honorable boiler. One thing
is certain—no other part of the world can put up such a
crowd of so famous has-beens in the sculling line. The
contest, if it eventuates, will be unique in rowing history,
and is likely to remain so for the best part of the current
century. Up to the present £65 2s. 6d. has been received
in contributions for the prize fund.
« * * *
The United States warship Baltimore’s crack
rowing crew is to meet the Fire Brigade crew on May 9,
for a match over the Shark Island course in Port Jackson.
The Yankee crew numbers a dozen, and as we don’t keep
that sort of boat in Sydney, there were difficulties in the
way of a match. However, the Yankees agreed to row
under Sydney conditions, and just now are busy taking
odds against themselves. Whichever team wins is to be
challenged by a crew from the Powerful.
-r * * *
His Britannic Majesty’s fighting ship Powerful
is a regular hive of sports. On Saturday last it sent a
team of soccer footballers out to Wentworth Park, where
it downed Ultimo soccer club by three points to two.
There are some dandy boxers aboard, and at rowing and
big gun marksmanship the Powerfuls reckon they are as
good as anything afloat.
* * » *
“Ramoth Gilead”: Apropos of the Engine-
street, Sydney, two-up school raid. Doesn’t it seem
rather mean of the authorities to wait till a holiday
season, and then, when William James, from the Barcoo,
has left ring-barking and tank-sinking for a few weeks
hard-earned holiday, and has been lured into the two-
up den, to make a raid and grab the simple waybackite,
and pocket his coin ? I met one victim in a coffee-stall,
shortly after the raid on the Engine-street school. He
had just been bailed out, and, in giving me an account of
the affair, he said there were many “ bushies ” in the cap-
tured gang, and it had “ cleaned him out ” and left him
without means to return to his plains and gumtrees.
* * * *
Some 40,000 people in all swarmed to the Eight-
Hour fete at Melbourne Exhibition on the shilling “ art-
union ” ticket, which is seemingly the only means of
enticing a crowd to witness cycle racing nowadays, even
when MacFarland, Rutt and co., are on the job. The
Druids, deprived of the old gamble, are said to have lost
£2OO by their recent Gala meeting, run on the same lines as
the A.N.A. affair. MacFarland won the £lOO wheel race,
but the performance was brilliant on paper only—for
various reasons. The most attractive item turned up in
the mile foot race, won by J. T. Leonard, ex-Footscray
harrier, in the fast time of 4min. 22 4-ssec. The usual
crying of odds was silenced under the Betting Suppression
Act.
* * * *
The atmospheric depression that swept over
Australia last week was, no doubt, due to the homeward
rush of Henri Mayer and Floyd MacFarland, who started
off for their native soil by different routes. Poor old
Mayer felt that he must get back to his bier and sauer-
kraut and big appearance money, and MacFarland has
business at the six days contest at Madison Garden, New
York. Mayer swears he will never return. MacFarland
promises to come back for next season. But nobody cares
a tinker’s benediction whether they do or not.
* * * *
Mighty cyclist George J. Farley has gone into
the summer drink and confectionery business in Claren-
don-street, South Melbourne, and the wheel is likely to
know him no more. Presumably there were times when
he was whirling round the track that George felt he
would have given his soul for a long lemon squash, and
when he decided that racing was vanity and vexation of
flesh he went into the business where he could realise his
dream all day long. Well, he is an acquisition to the
business, and the track could have better spared many
other men. Peace to his thirst.
* * « *
Some 30 members of the Sydney Motor
Boat Club have signified their intention of taking part in
that Ocean Reliability Contest between Sydney and
Newcastle. Providence has been playing up lately, and
in addition to wrecking- San Francisco, has been stirring
up rough seas along this side of the Pacific. So the
chances are that the reliability contest will not take place
till June brings its soothing atmosphere. Which is a
pity, for June is a mighty cold month at sea. _ However,
when this contest does come off it will be an interesting
event, and, as far as this writer knows, it will be the
first of its kind to take place in a, full-blown ocean. Most of
the European ones have been in the Mediterranean,
which isn’t quite so open as the Pacific, and even there
the usual result has been disaster to the boats. So if the
Sydney Club can bring its 62 miles rush through the
Pacific to a successful ciose it will be an event
worth shedding whisky over. There is a general
impression that the motor boat is only fit for
darting about landlocked waters, like those of Port
Jackson, but that is a mistake. During one of the
attempted French runs from Toulon to Algiers the flotilla
ran into the father of all Mediterranean gales, and the
people on the following warships were astounded at the
manner in which some of the larger cruisers with whale-
back fronts ran through the trouble. One motor boat,
steered by a lady, showed no signs of being m difficulties
till a steering line broke, and then it drifted round broad-
side on, was caught by a curler and buried. The plucky
raadarae, who was wearing a cork jacket, was picked up
by a gunboat, the crew of which saluted her with frantic
enthusiasm. The Sydney Club hedges its contest round
with conditions that will keep out any boat that the club
thinks unfit to participate in the struggle, and this will
render a fatality improbable.
* * * *
That freak motor boat, the Baby De Dion,
in which secretary Whatmore, of the Sydney Club,
put up 15 miles an hour at the recent regatta,
was throwing up the water of Port Jackson in
great style last Sunday. The Baby is a mere
shell, fitted with an 8 h.p. engine, and pants like
an animal when travelling at top speed. The water curls
up above the slope of its whale-back, and on Sunday it
was observed to be travelling at what looked about li
miles an hour. Writer has his doubts about the light
shell being best for racing purposes, and is firmly of
opinion that stronger frames, giving more rigidity, would
produce better results with the same horse-power. What-
more is importing a 30 h.p. Mercedes, which he proposes
to fit into another freak craft of his own design in time
for the opening of next racing season.
A recent inventor has discovered a simple and
effective method of doing away with the objectionable
stink from the petrol exhaust of the motor cars. And as
soon as the method was proved a success the French
Government passed a law inflicting fines upon motorists
who carry the old stink-pot. In Holland a law has just
been passed confining the use of the tooter to the motor
car and motor bike. Now, when the trembling pedestrian
hears the soul-stirring toot, he’ll know for certain there’s
a space-annihilator behind it, not a kiddy with a pram, or
a doddering cyclist doing six miles per hour. And that
pedestrian won’t stop to look round ; he’ll jump like an
express kangaroo pursued by a devil.
There is no further need for the Sydney man
who has had a row to tell his wife lies about a tram
accident —the second grade hurling competition started
on Saturday, when Redfern downed St. James’club by 34
points to two. Other games were played all over the
suburbs, and the evening found quite a number of happy,
exhausted Irishmen going home covered with bruises and
contusions from the hurleys of the unlicked cubs. The
hurling game wears a new aspect in Sydney, for
whereas, even as late as last season, nearly every
hurler was either Irish or Gaelic, this season
finds long-legged Australians, beefy Maorilanders, and
bull-faced Englishmen swinging the crooked stick.
It will be next season before the first-grade clubs get the
full benefit of the inrush of new blood, although straight
away they will be able to draw more liberally on the
better second-grade players. A pleasant feature of out-
door sport tins season is the increase in numbers all
round. Hurlers are quite numerous, lacrossers are twice
as plentiful as formerly, and footballers seem no less —in
fact the soccer crowd has puffed out to the dimensions of
a horde. Looks as though the bulk of Sydney young men
had suddenly grown tired of paying to watch sport, and
were going to take to it instead. Which may be bad for
the gate, but it is splendid for the community.
The unpleasant mob which amuses itself by
shooting pigeons out of traps and gambling on the
results, and by coursing hares which have no chance of
escape, is feeling uneasy. Hitherto it has posed as being-
very haughty and demn’d exclusive, doncherknow, about
whom it admitted to its grounds. The exclusiveness
was prompted, perhaps, by the fear that some person
with ordinary humanity and decent sporting instincts
would gain admission and lay an information against the
gang for cruelty to animals. The English portion of the
mob is just speechless at present through finding on the
business paper of the House of Commons a Bill for the
Suppression of Spurious Sports, and it is said that similar
Bills are in preparation in N.S.W. and Victoria. The
design of the proposed English Act is to suppress sport
that has degenerated into downright cruelty. No bird
or animal may be shot at, or hunted by either
animals (animals include birds) or human beings unless it
has been at large two months at least. This
knocks out in one act the repulsive business of
the carted stag, the bagged fox, the pigeon and sparrow
in the trap, and the pheasant that is turned out from the
hatchery the night before the shooting party, and walks
about in a perfectly tame state. Knowing that he has to
deal with a class to whom money is no object and fines no
determent, the author of the Bill has thrown in terms of
imprisonment among the penalties, and the millionaire
trembles in his rage and declares that the country is going
to the devil. In Australia the majority of the bird mur-
derers are a frowsy lot, mere whisky-soaked gamblers
who would die of scare if a pigeon turned on them. Their
shooting is of no value to the community ; at least none
of the gang that writer has met would be any good in an
invasion, except for picking the pockets of the dead. It
is to be hoped that all Australia will wipe out both them
and their horrible pastime. They are at large only owing
to an oversight of Parliament.
1, * w *
The Lacrosse colts were very much in evidence
in the Sydney parks and grounds on Saturday, and the
day being on the warm side, they found it about as
pleasant as navvying. The Canadian game doesn’t look
as ferocious as football, but it is just as hard work, and
the young fellows who were out of condition were
ready to drop. This game seems to have suddenly caught
on, and some one has apparently put in a lot of time in-
structing the colts how to wield their sticks, for even the
colts seemed to handle them with an ease and a cheerful
fluency of motion that were very absent at the beginning
of last season.

Sydney City Council proposes to erect spacious
swimming baths in Woolloomooloo Bay—an offer from
the Sydney Harbor Trust to lease the water frontage for
42 years being accepted. Provided the plans already con-
sidered are finally carried out, the straight-away stretch
of water will be over 100yds., and a long-murmured
grievance about the want of a sensible championship
course will be remedied. Some fast times should be put
up in Woolloomooloo Bay. No sane swimmer would stay
in that water a fraction of a second longer than necessary.
The N.S. W. Pony and Galloway Association
has defiantly replied to the sentence of sporting outlawry
passed on it by the Associated Clubs by declaring that it
will not race against any owner, trainer, jockey or horse
competing at any of the ring’s courses. Everybody
knew before that the Pony Association was behind the
Ascot Club, but hitherto the Ascot course was open to
any man who liked to leave the Associated Clubs. That
period of grace expires at the close of the present week
and after that it is war to the knife, and the devil take the
weakest.
* * * *
Bookie Colman’s action against the V.R.C.j
questioning its right to charge him £75 instead of the
former £5O for the privilege of betting “inside” at
Flemington, was last week decided in the club’s favor by
Justice A’Beckett. His Honor reckoned the committee
had control of and was responsible for the proper
management of the course, and could make regulations
to that intent, subject to the veto of the Governor in
Council; and that, racing being possible without
betting, and betting being possibly a nuisance, the V.R.C.
had just as much right to prohibit it, if it felt so dis-
posed, as to prohibit the hawking of commodities.
Colman now threatens an appeal to the High Court, but
his chance of success doesn’t look too rosy ; A’Beckett’s
judgments have a way of sustaining themselves. Anyhow,
the dictum that the raucous yelling of Ikey Noses can be
considered a nuisance is distinctly soothing.
* * * *
At Melbourne Exhibition Building, last Satur-
day, a sensational hammerdrama, entitled “Squiresand-
murphy,” was performed under the management of
Citizen Wren. Prior to the punch de resistance, seven
couples of light-weight young men took it in turns to
assault one another in a harmless manner, thesepreliminary
proceedings lasting until 9.30. Then came an interval for
suppressed excitement, after which Bill Squires and Tim
Murphy took the stage, and the vast audience settled
down to- enjoy a roaring time. Bill’s intentions were
understood to be strictly violent, whereas Tim was ex-
pected to dodge his adversary at the outset, with a view
to taking the offensive later on. But Tim—the taller and
lighter actor of the two—had no distant future before
him. The moment the prompter cried “ Go,” poor
Timothy’s plan of campaign was ruthlessly shattered by
the awful Bill. Bill smote him on the “jor.” He
biffed him on the body and bruised him outside the
soul. Within the first three minutes Tim suffered five
knock downs, and was palpably knocked-up. In the second
act the unhappy man fell only once. He even distin-
guished himself on one or two occasions by biffing Bill
but as a rule he was getting severely punished for his
rashness in being present. In the third act Bill’s task
was easy. He hurricaned into Tim, and that declining
pugilist came to grief as before. The referee went to him
and suggested that he had had enough, but seemingly he
wanted a little more. So Bill gave him a little more, and
down he went for the eighth and last time. Whilst Tim
was trying to collect his thoughts the referee spared Bill
any further exertion by declaring him the hero of the
entertainment. It had lasted eight minutes, exclusive of
the pauses for breath. Then the spectators (some of
whom had paid at the rate of fifteenpence a minute for
their joy and rapture) went away declaring that Bill
Squires was the noblest work of God.

According to Mr. “Barney” Reynolds, who is
supposed to be Mr. John Wren’s conscience, Mr. Wren has
noble aspirations regarding boxing. In his opinion ex-
pressed to a Melbourne Herald man, too many of Aus-
tralia, s best boxers have been compelled to leave Australia
in order to find new countenances to hit. So Mr. Wren
decided to come to the rescue. Bill Squires, as the best of
the present Australian bunch, is to be kept in his native
land. American boxers are to be induced to come to Aus-
tralia to hit Bill Squires for the fat purses Mr. Wren will
offer. The Californian earthquake is a special arrange-
ment between Providence and Mr. Wren in order to
further these patriotic efforts. San Francisco, the home
of the pug, is in ruins. Why, therefore, should not the

Bob Fraser, middle weight, and Bill Lang,
heavy weight, rival Melbourne footballers, fought 20
rounds under the Olympic club’s management at the
Cyclorama, Eastern Hill, on April 20. The fight was a
repetition of traser versus Nelson of a week earlier, with
the difference that on the previous occasion Fraser was
the man who took the battery ; on this occasion Fraser
was the man who dealt it out. Bob was just about as
much too clever for Bill on the 20th, as Ted was for Bob
PPJL- , a hhough the big man fought a game,
pushing fight till the last stroke of the gong, he never
looked nke getting ahead of his shifty and more scientific
enemy. Meanwhile Fraser was busy scoring points, and
-Liang s face was the scoring board. When time expired
freely marked, but not badly damaged, and
referee St.okesbury had an easy task in summing, up and
passing ludgment—“Fraser on points.”
