A Wyalong (N.S.W.) publican has success-
fully appealed against a conviction under the
Liquor Act, whereby he was fined 20s. because
live persons were found on his licensed premises
on a Sunday. Mr. Public-house had gone to
church, carrying the key of the bar in his pocket,
and he fell in despite this precaution. Fortu-
nately for the race of church-going publicans, the
conviction was upset. If it had stood, hotel-
keepers would have to spend the blessed Sabbath
defending the bar door with a battle-axe to con-
vince the law that their intentions were honorable.
Says an angry Argus correspondent, re-
ferring to the Natal insurrection :
Christianity (Ethiopianism) and sedition go hand in
glove, and it would have been better for South Africa if
the first disciples of the Ethiopian Church from America
had never been permitted to land in the country ; if they
did land, then not to exist. The creed of the Ethiopian
Church is “Africa for the Black Races.”
The only God recognised by John Bull is the one
that bears the special Imperial brand. No sedi-
tious deities need apply.
Last week it was announced: (1) That the
Garruthers Government had decided on its course
of action as regards the N.S.W. Railway Com-
missioners ; and (2) that it hadn’t yet fully con-
sidered the l eport of the committee of inquiry re
the astounding coal contract entered into by
Kirkcaldie and Fehon against the wish of Chief
Commissioner Oliver. That coal contract was
the cause of the big disagreement which brought
things to a head. It represented a ridiculously
bad bargain made by the two junior Commis-
sioners ; and any Ministry which has decided
what to do with the Commissioners without con-
sidering this principal item of evidence is a fool of
a Government.
One of the mysteries of modern commercial
life is the “ agent ’’—the person with no capital,
no stock, and very little furniture in a very small
office, who somehow makes a precarious living by
hanging on to the tail-end of Commerce and skir-
mishing at the rear of Trade. The Bulletin
thought it had heard of all the different kinds of
agent, but it finds it was wrong ; a perfectly new
kind of middleman or go-between was casually
mentioned last week, when the White case was
being heard in Sydney. White, a Gentleman
One, was imprisoned and removed from the roll
of solicitors, some time ago, on a charge of stealing
certain documents. Shortly after his release the
authorities in Sydney received a letter fromMor-
sen saying that he, and not White, had taken the
documents. Then the law fell on White with
the new charge of having invented an imaginary
Morsen with a view to deceiving the Law and,
presumably, of getting back on the roll of solicitors,
and, in the course of the proceedings, someone
mentioned the alleged Morsen as an alleged person
who made his humble but unblemished crust by
being “a private law and engineering agent.”
There is no reason why he shouldn’t do so, for
there are plenty of agents who sound more absurd
than tha,t, but The Bulletin is beginning to
wonder if there is any room in Sydney for a
theological agent. There seems to be already
every other kind of agent.

and Arg us-sanctioned Eight-Hours movement,
celebrated m Melbourne April 21st, recalls that
the stodgy lory of 1856 was just as blatant and
wearisome an ass as the anti-Socialist of to-day.
W hen, fatty years ago, the first procession carried
the now worn and faded blue-and-red banner
through Melbourne streets, the Eat party
moaned;—
Every industry would be ruined, the working classes in
Apparently the question whether Cap-
tain Crouch did or did not surrender during
the recent Victorian military manoeuvres is a
serious one, and not the joke it at first appeared.
The original idea in many quarters was that
Captain Crouch was mentioned as having been
captured by way of a hilarious witticism, and
that his denial published in the Age was part of
the mirth and comic opera, and that the whole
thing ranked with the girls who wave their legs
in the air at Rickards’ show. But it now appears
that the alleged capture was a serious military
proceeding, and that Crouch’s denial of the story
throws a blot on the other fellows’joint and several
escutcheon. So a military inquiry has been
ordered, and a barrel of sworn declarations has
been prepared, and a shocking row may be looked
for at any moment.
Melbourne, last week, had to swallow a
heavy overdose of University skite, and anv
public or private mention of the word “ Uni-
versity ” for the next six months should be
treated as a misdemeanor. Every sort of gather-
ing of caps and gowns has something of pretentious
puerility in it. The wearers have dressed them-
selves in a little brief importance of their own
making. Pedants demand reverence for their
mere scholastic acquirements, and students manv
The rowdyism which pretends to be just an
outbreak of high spirits in a Brotherhood
of P rain3 13 really a display of vanity, like the
a P^ lcs of a child who is “showing off” before
v . lslfc ors. After a whole week or more of Univer-
sltY funetioneering, one begins to ask whether it
w °uldn t be advisable to turn the hose of common-
®ohse upon such conflagrations. Too much Alma
Mater finds its way into the newspapers,
a . University education is too largely an
education in snobbishness. At the annual Com-
held in Melbourne Exhibition-
building last Thursday, a learned professor de-
glared, m other words, that the prosperity of a
radiated from its University. When a
schoolmaster talks in that strain, it is to he
expected that his scholars will have some trouble
their caps without the aid of a
shoehorn.

MY PAL HOMER
I HAVE a pal called Homer—an old Greek bard
is he;
The most poetic roamer this world did ever see !
I stride the promontories with Homer by my side,
And feast upon the glories of sky and cliff and tide !
I love the roaring ocean—old Homer loved it, too ;
The fretted white commotion his soul unto it drew.
It set his song a-rolling—it strung his lyre with joy—
It set old Homer trolling in the restless pubs, of Troy !
I love the good old Homer—the ghost who walks with
mje,
What time the long, grey comber rolls inward from the
sea.
The salt spray stings our faces, big winds chant in the
West,
When lordly Homer paces with me on mountain crest!
We talk of young Ulysses and Nestor brave and bold—
Of Helen’s honeyed kisses in the gay days of old !
We talk of old King Priam—a good old king was he —
The sort of king that I am in hours of reverie !
We talk of sacks and battles—of Ajax’ rolling car ;
Methinlts his chariot rattles where storms and thunders
are !
Methinks the Trojan forces still wrestle with the
Greeks —
Above the foaming horses the dust of combat reeks.
The Greek ships float in fancy upon the sunset tide —
O could but modern man see those ancient days of pride !
They were the days of daring, as Menelaus observed,
When sea-port towns were flaring and any reason served !
Pallas Athene slumbers, and Hector’s head lies low,
But when I read those numbers my pal penned long ago
I seem to see fair women stand on the walls of Troy,-
With eyes the tears are dim, in for mingled fear and joy !
I seem to hear them weeping, I feel their hot, salt tears—
Their agony comes sweeping across the wrack of years !
The sack of Troy sends shivers adown my modern spine,
( And Hector’s head still quivers in mingled blood and
wine!
I have a pal called Homer—he tells these things to me,
What time the long, grey comber rolls inward from the
sea.
We walk upon the headlands that face the salty south,
And in those misty, dead lands I still see Helen’s mouth !
Good luck to every Paris who strips another’s field ;
Alas, the day afar is of sword and spear and shield !
I like those pagan roamers—they fill my heart with joy—
Those drinky men of Homer’s who stormed the pubs, of
Troy.
Vic.
GRANT HERVEY.
The raking up of records of Melbourne
University, consequent on its jubilee celebrations,
has disclosed the fact that in 1887 some feminine
snob, who naturally desired her name to be kept
secret, offered a scholarship of £4O yearly to women
medical students, open only to those who had
“ never attended a State school.” It is distinctly;
gratifying to learn that the University Council of
the day promptly declined the endowment on
those terms.
The trustees of the rich Berry estate, now
in the hands of the Hay family, have offered to
hand over to the State 16,500 ft. of water frontage
skirting Berry’s Bay, North Sydney, and extend-
ing round to Ball’s Head, conditionally on the
Government undertaking to maintain for ever a
hospital for the use of residents in the Shoalhaven
district. Seeing that the duty of running the
hospitals of the State falls on the Government
in any case to the extent.erf at least half the cost,
the stipulation that an up-to-date institution must
be maintained near Berry looks comparatively
easy, and the State may be considered lucky in
getting its own again on such terms. The old Berry
grant, which is beginning to rival the Cooper estate
in the vastness of the.unearned increment, did
not include the usual 100 ft. reservation on the
foreshores of the harbor, and the estate is now
handing back the foreshores on the basis of a £6
per foot valuation. The property is certainly of
considerable value ; still, any person who tried to
settle on Ball’s Head and other precipitous parts
of the frontage would have to spend a heap of
money to make his water frontage accessible, or
else reach it by ladder or by airship, or else be
content to fall into it from a great height and be
drowned.
“ Vica ” writes :
The strut of the gaudy soldier-man is as nothing beside
that of the University doctor swaddled in garish scarlet
and a scream of yellow. Last week I watched a Uni-
versity reception in Melbourne, where Doctors of Law,
Philosophy, Literature, Medicine, Divinity and Lord,
knows-what were preening themselves on a platform,
W. Anderson’s pantomime was nowhere for barbaric color.
And these are the gentlemen who think themselves the
intellectual salt of the earth, and who laughed with
scorn, when gorged with their University dinner, at the
endeavor of outsiders to bring their barren “culchaw”
into line with modern thought. There is no conceit like
that of the University professor, and when he puts on his
vermilion gown, and his red tassel, and his yellow-striped
stomacher to parade in front of his adoring clique, his
vanity becomes taller than Belshazzar’s palace.
Military discipline, esprit cle corps, pipe-
clay, and several other senses of martial dignity
were violated in Sydney Water Police Court one
day last week. A valiant draper, who omitted to
attend drill on account of an extra rush on his
emporium for something in the nature of sheeting,
was hauled before a minor magistrate to enforce a
fine of £2 for an offence described in the informa-
tion as “ conduct to the prejudice and good order
of military discipline.” The panjandrum who
had fined the draper but couldn’t collect the fine
was quoted as “Reginald Selwyn Smith, officer
commanding the Australian Army Service Corps
Militia, New South Wales.” The draper simply
pleaded that there was a rush for sheeting, or
something like that, and that he had no intention
of paying any fine, and the magistrate threw the
Case, affidavits and all, out of the court. It doesn’t
matter much, perhaps, in time of peace, but in
war time it may be necessary to hang military
drapers who don’t turn up.
If, as seems pos-
sible, Britain has to go to
war with Turkey over
the Sinai Peninsula, Tur-
key should have all the
best of the fight on land.
A nation whose military
resources were strained
to the utmost to meet the
Boer army of 40,000 or
50,000, would have little
chance to withstand Tur-
key’s force of nearly a
million. Victorious on
land, the Turks might,
in reply to a ruined Con-
stantinople, destroy the
Suez Canal. It’s a dis-
mal commentary on Eng- i
land’s military system I
that even against the
Siok Man of Europe it
could put no adequate
army in the field.
Germany is blamed
in England for stirring
up Turkey to make
trouble over the Sinai
Peninsula. It’s the En-
glish fashion now to
blame Germany for every-
thing. A couple of gener-
ations ago the German
was the person who, so i
to speak, swept out offices
and cleaned up drains for
England. He was the
abject in the house of
Europe whose armies
were seldom patriotic
unless they were paid by
foreign gold, and whose
industrial position was
contemptible. Now, by
high Protection and effec-
tive industrial organisa-
tion, Germany has raised
itself up until it is the
foremost military and in-
dustrial powerof Europe, j
and is even preparing to
talk back at England on
the sea. The English- j
man, who has still the
habit of regarding the
German as an office-
sweeping person, can’t
stomach this, and anger
with Germany shows
itself in a disposition to
blame the Teuton for
everything. If King
Edward went out- i
rageously wrong in set-
ting the fashion for the ■
new season’s neck-wear, !
probably it W'ould be at-
tributed to the pernicious
machinations of the
Kaiser. j
The Law had another
bout lately with Percy
Ram age, the rampageous ;
youth who periodically j
plays ninepins with the
Melbourne police. This 1
time it took only four con-
stables to sit on Percy
while he was handcuffed.
Magistrate Panton re-
cognised the inefficacy
of the Law to restrain
Ramage, and merely im-
posed three £1 fines,
remarking that Ramage,
if he wasn’t careful,
would ultimately dance
at the end of a rope for
slaying a policeman.
That is the beauty of the
Law; its “remedy” is
sudden and-sure enough
when the Ramage—or
rather the damage—is
done, but for devising any
sensible plan to prevent
damage—which is the
same thing as preventing
Ramage—it is as hope-
lessly incompetent as
George Reid trying to
define anti-Socialism.
There is wailing in
the camp of the Deform
Leaguers, that curious
crowd of self-satisfied
humbugs which was to
bring about a sort of
Kyabramapootra move-
ment in N.S.W. Every-
where are to be found
branches of this precious
league, and its numbers
are said to be as the
sand on the sea shore;
but the only energetic
members seem to be
those who write to the
press complaining of the
“apathy” of the other members. Last week
“Kernel” Bell, the ancient and rather humorous
U.S.A. Consul, was advertised to speak in
favor of individualism, or against Socialism, or
to expose the fallacies of something or other, and
out of 7500 allegedly paid-up metropolitan mem-
bers of the league only 40 turned out to hear the
aged prophet slay the enemy and drag his remains
round the city till they were even deader than
before.
While the Victorian ’Pootras were listen-
ing to Yes-No babbling about the shameful han-
dicaps that Socialism would put upon the farmers,
one of them had something lying heavy in his
pocket that made him chuckle. He was a Carrutn
farmer, and when Dr. Cherry, of the Socialistic
Agricultural Department, was down that way last
year he strongly advised maize-growing. The
farmers smiled; maize wouldn’t do there, they
said. _ But one of them took the advice of the
Socialistic expert, and chanced 45 acres. It
yielded him 90 bushels per acre, of such fine
tjuality that the Department promptly offered
him 4s. a bushel for his crop, for seed purposes.
That sort of man doesn’t worry about ’Pootra
leagues ; State Socialism, as far as it has gone, has
paid him all right.

THE TWENTY-SIXTH REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS OF
The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
1. The Balance Sheet of the Bank as on 31st March last, and the Profit and Loss Account for the
half year which then terminated, are now submitted to the shareholders.

3. During the half year branches have been opened in Victoria at Macarthur, Stratford and
Wangerrip, and agencies at Briagolong, Laver’s Hill, Myrniong and Rivernook. Branches have also
been opened at Wallaroo, in South Australia, and at Broome Hill, Kojonup and Moora, in Western
4. The Ordinary General Annual Meeting will be held at the Head Office of the Company,
Collins-street, Melbourne, on Wednesday, 2nd May, at noon. .
5. The shareholders will be called on to elect two directors on that occasion in lieu of John Grice,
Esq., and Edward Trenchard, Esq., who retire by rotation. There are no other candidates. They
will also be required to appoint two auditors.
By order of the Board of Directors,
JOHN G. ADDISON, Chief Manager.

“P.N.” : Melbourne University has been
inquiring anxiously about its lost foundation stone,
and at last, by the aid of a very ancient person
who helped to build the halls of learning m the
’50’s., it has been found, obliterated under the soil
and vegetation of half a century. Australia is
dotted over with lost, disused and forgotten founda-
tion stones. Where, by the way, are all the
foundation stones of Sydney’s new Parliament
House —the one that never happened ? And
where are the men who laid them ? And where is
the money that was supposed to be put under
them, and the liquor that was consumed over
them?
“ T.D.” : I met the new arrival from Aus-
tralia at a convivial gathering in London just
after the Keir Hardie crowd had made Britain
recognise that Giles and Hodge and Bill and plain
John were at last to be reckoned with. He held
the fort, as the new-chum Australian generally
does until he discovers that a loose tongue collects
no gold. “ I told people out there what it would
be if they let the blessed Labor party get hold,”
he remarked. “I said everything would go to
the dogs, and Australia wouldn’t be fit to live in,
and my word, it’s about the rottenest hole under
the sun now Watson and all those fellows have
taken things in hand.” I’ve heard so much of
that kind of thing from alleged Australians in
England that I just let things rip, until a few
minutes afterwards I heard him relating with
great gusto how he had taken the Victorian
Government down in the early nineties by a deal
in land, which was required for public purposes,
and which he sold to one of the departments for
four times what it was really worth. He had been
rioting on the profits of that transaction ever since.
Then came my turn, and I asked the gentleman
whether the Labor party had ever been known to
connive at or tolerate land swindles, also whether
any big land-swindlers and land-boomers had ever
belonged to the working man element which was
now ruining Australia. Likewise I inquired
whether he was going to pay back his illicit
gains. from that land transaction. The listening
Britishers merely gaped when another Australian
“ patriot ” subsided, and sat miserably in the
corner by himself, and wondered why he hadn’t
held his tongue.

Liver Disease Cao Be CCired
From Mr. Charles Newson, 7 Taylor-street, Surry Hills, Sydney, 10th March, 1905.
“For three years I suffered from liver complaint, dizziness in the head, spasmodic pain in the
upper part of the abdomen, sick headache, frequent vomiting of bitter matter, accompanied by a feel-
ing of depression and a disinclination for exertion. I was induced to try Warner’s Safe Cure, and,
after taking that medicine, 1 was promptly relieved from all pain. Since then, my health has been
most satisfactory.
From Mrs. Margaret Doig, 60 Helen-street, Northcote, Vic., 25th February, 1905.
“For nearly three years, previous to a few months ago, I was afflicted with liver and kidney
trouble in a very severe form. I suffered intense pain in the back and under the shoulder blades, and
there was a sense of weight and pain in the region of the liver. I also had indigestion and other
symptoms of general ill-health. Nothing seemed to give me relief until I took Warner’s Safe Cure,
and then I got relief from the first bottle I took. I continued to take Warner’s Safe Cure and
Warner’s Safe Pills for some time longer, when all traces of the complaint left me, and there has been
no return of it up to the present; in fact, my health has been most satisfactory in every respect.”
From Mr. Robert White, Fitzroy-street, Rockhampton, Q., 11th March, 1905.
“My wife suffered from very severe bilious attacks. She had medical advice, but derived no
benefit from the treatment. Having myself had experience of the value of Warner’s Safe Cure, I
persuaded her to try that medicine. After taking two bottles of Warner’s Safe Cure and a phial of
Warner’s Safe Pills she was completely restored to health. I can heartily recommend Warner’s Safe
Cure and Warner’s Safe Pills to anyone suffering from biliousness.”
From Mr. Cecil Thompson, 17 Womerah Avenue, Rusheutters’ Bay, Sydney, N.S. W., 9th March, 19C5*
“Two years ago I had serious disease of the liver, indigestion, dizzy headaches, and great pain in
my chest and stomach. I tried various remedies to obtain relief, but without succeeding. I was
nearly in despair, when I happened to see a pamphlet which was left at my house, containing a num-
ber of letters from people, who, after suffering in a similar way to myself, had been cured by taking
Warner’s Safe Cure. So I thought if it would cure others, why should it not cure me, and resolved to
give it a trial. I did so, with the happy result that, in a few weeks, I was quite cured and in the
enjoyment of good health. Since that time I have been quite free from the complaint.”

From Mrs. A. M‘Donald, 52 Comber-street Paddington, Sydney, N.S.W., 7th March, 1905.

and under the shoulder-blades. I was reduced to a condition of great weakness ; I dreaded to stoop
to pick up anything, as stooping made me feel quite faint and dizzy. For years I suffered in this
way, and could get no relief till Warner’s Safe Cure was brought to my notice. A course of that

WORK AND TRADE.
[For The Bulletin.]
It was just a little hut, where well-tended fruit tree
grew,
And the hot winds from the west,
Or the cold winds from the crest
Of the iceberg’s in the South Sea seldom made a passage
through ;
It was many kinds of wood,
Bad, indifferent, and good,
And the roof of bark was pieced and patched to please
the owner’s mood.
But the man who lived within was more happy than a
king,
For he feared no traitor foe
Plotting for his overthrow,
Or capricious subjects grudging or revoking anj'thing.
He had what sufficed for one,
Loving labor in the sun,
And he dreamed of bliss hereafter when his working days
were done.
And beside the city’s harbor where big firms have taken
root,
There’s an agent rich and fat,
With a costly, shining hat,
Who receives the old man’s cases and disposes of his
fruit,
He sends up accounts which show
That the fruit fetched so-and-so—
Just a half of what it truly fetched, as many people
know.
And the agent’s growing richer and more pious with the
y ears,
And he regularly prays
For the old man length of days,
For he likes to deal with people who have no suspicious
fears.
Each is happy in his way,
One’s a rogue, and one’s a jay,
And ’tis combinations such as this that make our com-
merce pay.
N.S.W. Oby.

“ J.F.D.” : A knacker’s yard is a cheerful-
looking place. Was at one on Dead Horse-road, Botany,
recently, and the boss was swearing in large capitals.
“What I can never make out,” he said, “is why the
horses die in fits and starts. We hadn’t one in yester-
day, and here’s seven in this morning, and the ’earse
gone for another.” A poultry farmer loading up with
boiled, horse started to talk about ducks. “A duck is
the biggest cannibal on earth,” he said. “ He’d eat his
father. S’help me, my crowd hears this cart coming-
before I get within a mile of ’ome, and they get up at the
gate, kicking up ahorfulrow, and nudging one another in
the ribs with their bills. If I leave the cart for a moment,
they climb up the shafts and scoff everything in it. I
reckon a duck can eat about four pounds of ’orse at one go.
Two old drakes got a long sinew, the other day, and they
started on it from different ends, and there they were,
walking round the yard, ’ooked together for ’arf a day,
until the old woman cut it in two. Then each one
thought ’e beat the other, and went round telling all the
ducks about it. I heard a parson say all flesh is grass,
and ducks is of that opinion.” The “ hearse ” arrived
then, and the duck-feeder’s horse bucked at the sight of
his deceased brother, and upset all the boiled horse in the
sand. The boss knacker said he was going to dinner, so
I departed, leaving the poultry-farmer shovelling up sand
and horse in equal quantities and throwing them into the
cart. “ That’s one thing about ducks,” he said, “ they’re
not a bit pertikler, and this sand ’ll do their digesters
good.”
• • * *
“ Flabby Paul ” : Reading some remarks about
nerve specialists in previous issues of The Bulletin, I
thought I’d write to one and await results. Got back a
pamphlet with a list of symptoms, and instructions,
if I had one or more of them, to immediately send £1 and
live. There were 96 symptoms mentioned, of which I had
had 91 during the week. I promptly wrote a cheque for
a pound, with a forlorn hope of getting a reply from the
specialist in time. The bank wouldn’t cash the cheque,
so I sat back and awaited my doom. While I waited the
other li\ e symptoms crept slowly over me and I gave my-
self up for lost. The last five symptoms were a craving
for strong drink, irritation (a man just having asked me
to pay what I owed him), elephantiasis, appendicitis and
hydrophobia. This statement is purely voluntary. I
received no money or other consideration and to the
best of my belief it is not true. (I may have had heart
disease but I don’t think so.) Anyhow, I’m prepared to
give an assurance to anyone who cares to write to me,
that I’m still alive and still possess some of the symptoms.
Natural modesty alone prevents me sending you my
photo, for publication.
* * * *
“I was never yet beaten down in my prices,”
said the smart grocer, flicking a fly with the corner of his
apron. “ Oh, yes, it looks as if I was, I allow. They will
want a ha’penny off this and a penny off that, and so on,
and you think they get it because I agree. But they
don’t get it. They never got it once. Unless they' are
buying other things I never come down, and if they buy
other things I simply add to something else what I took
off. Most of us do that now. Easy ? It’s ever so easy.
The party’s so proud at having bested us for a ha’penny
or penny that the increase in another article isn’t noticed.
If I’m very closely watched I shove it on as an error in
counting up the total. I never yet gave in to a customer,
and never will, and I don’t know a man in the trade that
does. Anyway, if there is such a man he needn’t come to
me for a job.”

“Elmore” : In a N.E. Vic. tannery, t’other
day, was shown piles of leather ready for market. Huge
letters and figures, the remnants of old brands, were
scattered on nearly every piece ; and each brand repte.
sented a loss of from 2s. to 4s. a hide. Surely' some cattle-
man can invent a way of identifying his beast without
spoiling the hide. Or if he' must brand, why hot stick
to the less valuable neck ?

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After a Bush-Fire.
The garden fair, restored in calm, reposes,
The mended trellis smiles in the embrace
Of Afric’s ivy; and some autumn roses
Cling to the covert with a girlish grace,
Unharm’d’mid all the storm ; each bloom discloses
A heart of gold ; next which the eye may trace
The errant, amorous bee, an ardent suitor,
AVho’d learned his lesson well without a tutor.
The birds return. . . . Rosellas golden-breasted
Dive in the garden-paths, and softly flute ;
The cockatoo, all snow, and sulphur-crested,
Screams as lie skims the field he used to loot,
While the bronzed dove, her gentle foot-sole
rested,
Cooes on the winding path, where coral fruit
Stud the green train, the strawberry trails along,
As magnie-carols fill the air with song.
And blades of grass, like emerald needles thrust-
ing,
Keen-pointed, thro’ Earth’s mantle, black as
night,
Give earnest of green turf where charcoal-crusting
Shuts all the coming verdure from the light,
Much as some envious cloud of salt-sea-rusting
Obscures the glories of a sabre bright.
The fields are green, all Nature smiles again,
And our slab castle here might be in Spain.
VIO.
G.G.McC.
Welly Hot Insi’.
Li Ho conducts a doctor’s trade
AVay out at Bullabri;
His only medicine is made
Of schnapps and seaweed dry,
No matter what
Disease you’ve got,
One diagnosis meets the lot—
AVithout demur,
Li says : “ My wor’,
You welly hot insi’! ”
And mostly, too, he feels you where
The heat’s supposed to lie,
What time he sagely shakes his bare
Old pate, and shuts an eye ;
So Simon Farr,
Of Talbragar,
One day held there a live cigar,
And Li Ho swore,
And said : “ Whaffor?
You plully hot ou’si’! ”
Once unto Li some fellows brought
A swagman, bitten by
A carpet snake, who really thought
His time had come to die.
He’d slowly sunk,
Until he’d drunk
A case of schnapps to cure his lunk—
The cunning Chow
Shuns snakebite now :
“ Too muchee hot insi’ ! ”
You bet, the little township takes
An honest pride.in Li,
And better physic than lie makes
It doesn’t wish to try;
The seaweed tack
Supplies the lack,
Maybe, of greener food out back,
Or else, perhaps,
It is the schnapps
Suits Bullabri’s insi’ !

HENRY E. HORNE.
To an Old Playfellow.
I remember the far green hill-top,
Where we clung to the rata vines,
And you climbed to the nesting parrots
In the boughs of the kauri pines;
And the scent of the tutu bushes,
By the bend in the path o’ergrown,
AV here you wove me a necklet of rushes,
As we sat on the “ Bunyip Stone ” ;
And the day that we roamed the terrace,
’Mid the tangle of supple vines,
And the tuis sang on the meros
To the locust’s hum on the pines.
But the wind came out of the forest,
Like a lost soul’s moan in the air,
Till we thought ’twas the great Bush Spirit
AVho would draw us and hold us there.
And the might of our child-hearts failed us,
As we fled from the forest door,
AVith the roar of the pines behind us,
And the known green flats before.
Shrivelled now are the tutu bushes
That we climbed with our light limbs then ;
And the shrine of the hillside echoes
To the clang of the boots of men.
But the mystical pines lean over,
; And their shadows are falling black
'Between one on the trampled highway
i And a chum on an old bush track.

which will be drawn in Melbourne on May 14,
and £lOOO awaits the person whose ticket bears
the winning number. There are 129 consolation
prizes, ranging in value from £l5O to a modest
“quid.” Also, a sure and certain profit can be
achieved by anyone who buys 22 tickets for £l,
and retails them to his friends at a shilling each,
but in order to get this reduction on a quantity

chance to retrieve its character.*
“ Hiphen,” whom The Bulletin knows as
a mighty good writer of tales, sends this :
The S.M. Herald criticises Australian literature. It
says sweepingly that there is none, and that the fragment
of literature so-called bears the impress of English and
American writers. Take it for the love of argument that
the Herald is right. What is the Herald going to do
about it? It is easy to criticise. Talk is cheap. What
does literature (always a hard mistress) offer a young man
in Australia? Take an instance—a case on which I can
speak authoritatively—one that has come under my
notice. Take myself.
lam a young Australian—also a clerk in the city. I
feel I can write. I know I can write. I would rather
write than eat my dinner. But to live I must eat my
dinner. And that dinner has to be earned. To earn it I
work nine or 10 hours a day at a desk in the city. A
modern galley slave am I. In my evenings I write.
Now if I throw up my billet as galley slave which
gets me my dinner, what has the Herald to offer me?
There is only one paper that pays for Australian matter
at fair living prices. The rest have the importing craze.
Will the Herald patronise my wares ? Will it give us
young Australians a chance ? Will it publish my humble
yarns which deal with what I know—Australia ? Yarns
of the seamy side ; of the rough out-back ; of the cheap,
tawdry suburb. Will it leave off importing the refined
English literature which deals with the moral lapses of
English high society ?
Every 10 years—every ten years—every ten years—the
Sydney Mail offers a £lOO prize for an Australian story.
This is encouraging ! It is munificent, charitable,
inspiring ! The S.M. Herald does perhaps less than this,
but in its lofty and cobwebbed bed it turns in its sleep and
says: “ Let there be an Australian Literature; and God
help ye who go to help to make it—for we won’t.”


Legal Notices.
PURSUANT to an order of the Court of
Chancery of the County Palatine of
Lancaster, made in the matter of the estate of
JAMES BUCKLEY deceased LOWE v.
SWIRE 1902. Letter L No. 155 whereby an
enquiry was directed what grandchildren of the
Testator issue of his four children MARY
BUCKLEY, FRANCES BUCKLEY, SARAH
ANN BUCKLEY, and JAMES THOMAS
BUCKLEY were living at the decease of the last
survivor of the said four children and whether any
and which of such grandchildren have since died and
if so who are the legal personal representatives of
the one or more so dying and whereby a further
enquiry was directed whether any grandchild
or grandchildren of the Testator issue of his said
four children had died prior to the decease of the
last survivor of the said Testator’s said four
children leaving issue and if so who were the
issue of such children so dying before such date
leaving issue and whether any one or more of such
issue are now dead and if so who are their re-
spective legal personal representatives. All
persons claiming to be any of the persons referred
to in either of the said enquiries are by their soli-
citors on or before the 2nd day of July 1906 to
come in and prove his her or their claim before
the Registrar of the Manchester District of the
said Court at his Chambers situate in Duchy
Chambers 4 Clarence-street, Manchester, Eng-
land, or in default thereof they will be peremp-
torily excluded from the benefit of the said Order.
THURSDAY, the 12th day of July, 1906, at 11
o’clock in the forenoon, at the said chambers, is
appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
NOTE. —The survivor of the Testator’s said four
children was JAMES THOMAS BUCKLEY,
who died on the 31st of October, 1879. Two of
the children of the Testator’s said daughter
MARY (afterwards MARY SWIRE), namely,
THOMAS SWIRE and HENRY SWIRE, were
last heard of about 40 years ago, when they
respectively went to Australia, and afterwards:
they or one of them went to America.
Any person having knowledge of the where-
abouts of the said THOMAS SWIRE and;
HENRY SWIRE, or either of them if living, or
of their respective children if living, or of the
place or places of their respective decease if dead,
is requested to communicate with Messrs.
WATSON and SON, of 11 Church Lane, Oldham,
England.
Dated this 13th day of March, 1906.
HUBERT WINSTANLEY,
Registrar.
WATSON and SON,
of 11 Church Lane, Oldham, England,
Plaintiff’s Solicitors.

Mr. W. G. Heame.
Dear Sir, —In 1898,. I was lor four years previously in
Queensland travelling representative for a mercantile
house. Having contracted a heavy cold, I placed myself
under a medical man, and used all kinds of chemists’
prescriptions without avail. I was then staying at Hayes’
Terminus Hotel, Brisbane. The manager did all possible
for me, for which I shall always feel grateful. Mr. Duncan
Currie, at that time acting manager for the New York
Life Insurance Co., came to see me daily, and he advised
me to try your Bronchitis Cure. I was about done for,
could not eat, or scarcely swallow ; in fact, used to nearly
suffocate a dozen times a day, suffering intensely. Guess
of my thankfulness, when Mr. Currie brought me a bottle
of your Bronchitis Cure. A few doses actually cured me.
I took another bottle away from the city with me, and

The Peak in Darien.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
~wr jr-JS stared on the Pacific, and all his men
/■ -/ Gazed at each other with a wild sur-
t M raise,
Silent upon a Peak in Darien.
The world went well, and golden was the weather,
The skies without a frown,
When he and I walked up the hill together
To where the sun goes down.
We made the journey short with song and jesting,
My friend with heart so light;
We had no time for loitering or resting,
No fear of coming night.
The years to us were days, the days were hours,
then,
No carking cares had we;
The staff he bore was all abloom with flowers then,
The flowers of Poetry.
So we pressed on towards the Sunset’s porches,
Still higher and still higher;
In his lean cheeks there were two burning torches,
And in his heart red fire.
At times we strode along with little speaking,
Although our eyes were fond,
’Twas not the Rainbow Key that we were seeking,
But something far beyond.
We passed some pilgrims who with tears had salted
The path they trod in pain;
And at the Inn called Last of Luck we halted,
And heard a gay refrain.
I stayed and drank awhile with those good
fellows—
We all joined in a glee ;
Their raiment’s hues were mostly reds and yellows,
But what was that to me ?
But, ah my friend, he went away before me,
And left me all alone,
And so I live with old, grey memories o’er me,
Dark as a Druid stone.
I saw him standing on the Mount of Vision,
Transfigured, weak of breath ;
“ And can you truly see the Isles Elysian,
For you there is no Death.”
“ This is,” he said, “the Peak of Darien
Whatever may befall”...
I left my friend with other hopeful men
With dead eyes shining in the hospital.
V. J. DALEY.
The Riding of Slinger.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DULLA was one of the oldest squat-
tages on the Logan. Its patchwork
buildings, and the collection of wretched
vehicles about the blacksmith’s shop, were evi-
dence of the fact. There were bullock waggons,
horse drays, spring carts, buggies and wag-
gonettes; some were mere frames of splintered
wood and bent iron, and some had wheels without
tires, or wheels that had lost a number of spokes
and felloes. Toby Carson, the young manager,
had brought them in from out-camps and else-
where, and Jim Scully and Bill Tarkalson had
taken the contract of making them into service-
able rolling stock.
Scully was the blacksmith. He stood at the
anvil, artistically decked in a doughbanger’s cap
and a leather apron, flogging iron and making
sparks fly. Tarkalson, who modestly described
himself as a rough carpenter, sat chopping out
spokes with a tomahawk, and piling up chips.
Lolling on the bench was Matthew Conyers, who
had just arrived from the Richmond, looking for
droving. He was interrupting the work by re-
counting how he had broken in 40 colts at Wyan-
garie at a pound a head.
“ That’s a good price, ain’t it ? ” asked Tarkal-
son.
“For ordinary horses it’s a fair thing,” said
Conyers, * ‘ but it was little enough for that lot.
Ripsnorters they were, every one of ’em. Buck
a house down.”
“Didn’t buck you down, though?” remarked
Scully, blowing the bellows.
Conyers smiled as though such a contingency
was among the impossibles. “They had an out-
law there that’d thrown all th’ crack riders on
Wyangarie,” he went on. “Th’ boss offered me
a pound one mornin’ to ride him. I snapped; an’
he set me about the roughest go I’ve had since
th’ black mare bucked over th’ precipice with me
on Gordon Brook.”
“Thought it was McPherson who rode that
mare over the precipice ? ” questioned Scully,
leaning on the pole of the bellows.
“It was me,” Conyers informed him with deci-
sion.
“It said McPherson in th’ paper,” Scully per-
sisted.
“I contradicted that report,” said Conyers.
“ But they mislaid my letter till it was too late.
McPherson was a subscriber. I wasn’t.”
Toby Carson came to the door with a bridle on
his arm. A couple of stockmen were catching
horses in the yard.
“Anybody ridin’ Slinger to-day?” asked
Tarkalson.
“ No,” said Toby. “ Want a seat ? ”
Tarkalson, with a jerk of the tomahawk over
his shoulder, drew attention to the interested
person on the bench.
“ This chap’s fit for an argyment with anything
yer ken produce—providin’ yer make it worth his
while. Crack rider from Wyangarie.”
“ Well, if he can ride Slinger I’ll give him the
brute,” said Toby.
“ There you are, Mat,” said Tarkalson. “Th’
finest-lookin’ ’orse on Dulla.”
“’N animal with a bit o’ spirit in him,” added
Scully with a grin.
Conyers slipped off the bench and limped across
to the forge for a light. “I’d like to snap that,”
he said, limping back. “I’d like to—but I’m
afraid me leer won’t stand it.”
“Wot’s th’ matter with yer leg?” asked
Tarkalson, surprised.
“ Horse fell on it crossin’ the range. Me back’s
been troublin’ me a bit too,” said Conyers,
indicating the spot with his thumb.
“Yer seemed all right when yer came in,”
Tarkalson remarked, aggressively.
“ Felt all right too,” Conyers admitted. “Comes
on me after I sit down awhile.”
Tarkalson and Scully exchanged glances.
“ Give us a blow, Bill,” said the latter, and the
matter would have dropped there but that Toby
Carson scented fun.
“ There’ll be some cattle going away from here
next week, and I’ll want another hand,” he said.
“Have a camp, and you can ride Slinger some
afternoon before we start.”
Conyers didn’t look as pleased as the occasion
seemed to call for, but he consented, remarking
that he would be quite fit for the droving at any
time, and hoped that gammy leg of his would not
deprive him of the pleasure of a seat on the out-
law. He wanted a horse of Slinger’s reputed
calibre for a buckjumping contest at the Casino
show.
“I remember you entered for the Grafton show
one time,” said Scully. “Yer didn’t go any
further, though.”
“ Th’ blamed horse got out into the bush th’ day
before,” Conyers explained.
“Heard ’em say th’ fence was cut where he got
through, an’ there was tracks of a lot o’ gallopin’
thereabout,” said Scully, tapping the anvil busily.
“I heard about that too,” said Conyers, twist-
ing uneasily. “ Believe that fellow as won the
prize did it. I know he was a bit frightened o’
me an’ my nag.”
Tarkalson was seized with a fit of coughing.
* * * *
When Conyers had been a couple of days on
Dulla an event happened that precipitated
matters. A sheep had got out of the killing pen,
and Toby Carson and a rouseabout were trying to
catch it near the yard. Tarkalson and Scully
went out to give a hand, the yard being close to
the shop. Conyers followed. He was a good
runner, was Conyers. That is, he could run
away from the blacksmith and the carpenter. He
flew around, darting this way and streaking that
way; he dodged and danced and leaped in the
air, knocking his heels together and throwing his
arms about to show his agility. When the sheep
had been caught, Carson said :
“Leg seems pretty right now, Conyers. What
about a ride this afternoon?”
Conyers’ expression was that of a man who had
been taken an unfair advantage of. He ran his
hand down the afflicted limb and began to limp
again. “ I’m afraid that twistin’ about didn’t do
my leg any good,” he said. Then his thumb
dived into his spine. “ Back’s th’ worst. Caught
me up pretty sharp once or twice.”
Carson grinned and walked away.
“Look ’ere, Conyers,” said Tarkalson when
they had got back to the shop. “ I bet th’ boss
two quid this mornin’ that you’d cross Slinger an’
sit him. Seems I’m goin’ to lose.”
“You wouldn’t if I was right,” said Conyers.
“ Eggs-ackly,” assented Scully. “ But it looks
’s if them ailments you’ve picked up are goin’ to
be chronic. Often taken that way ? ”
“If yer don’t mount him, I’ve got to forfeit,”
Tarkalson continued. “If he slings yer I lose;
but if yer stick to him half th’ wager’s yours.”
“You oughtn’t ’ave bet that way, knowin’ how
crippled up I am,” said Conyers.
“I thought I could depend on an old mate,
who’s not m th’ habit o’ sayin’ he ken do things
he carn’t, an’ who’s gorra wholesome contempt
for a person who has no pluck in him.”
Conyers left the shop offended, and strode off
down the paddock. At dinner-time he came back
—a new man. He joked about Slinger, and
smiled all over his face.
“ Your two quid’s safe, William,” he said, with
a confidence that surprised Bill. “I’m goin’ to
ride the outlaw this afternoon. Pass the mus-
tard.”
They couldn’t account for his high spirits and
sudden change of manner.
“ Must’ave let him out,” Tarkalson whispered
as they left the table.
“ Eggs-ackly,” said Scully.
But Slinger wasn’t let out. He appeared in
the yard an hour later, and Conyers at once went
over to fulfil his promise, carrying his saddle and
bridle on one arm, and a long bolster cm the other.
Slinger was a big black horse, with the sleek
coat and carriage of a stallion. He looked power-
ful and active, and he was certainly a snorter.
He snorted every time Conyers moved.
The others sat on the caps, recalling the men
Slinger had maimed or killed in his time. Con-
yers looked pale. He saddled the murderer in
the crush, with a lot of trouble. Then he secured
the bolster by the middle to the crupper staple,
hooked the reins under the stirrup leathers, and
let Slinger loose in the yard. He bucked like a
demon, bucked, grunted, and squealed; and at
every prop the ends of the bolster swung out and
slapped him across the flanks, which made him
kick and buck all the more. Conyers kept him
going round the yard for an hour, till he was too
tired to buck any more—till he would hardly even
kick at the bran bags and kerosene tins Conyers
threw at his heels. He was in a lather of sweat
when Conyers caught him and relieved him of the
bolster.
“ You’re not going to get on him yet?” Toby
inquired with sarcasm.
“ I’d try him with th’ fam’ly bed-tick an’ a few
pillers fust,” Tarkalson suggested.
“Do yer want a landin’-net ? ” asked Scully.
Conyers didn’t hear these opprobrious remarks.
He led Slinger out of the yard, and started across
the paddock, watching the snortful beast across
his shoulder as he went.
“Means to have clear ground,” Toby remarked
as the three followed him. They followed him
for half a mile. Conyers still led tbe horse.
“ Showin’ th’ animal some scenery,” said
Scully.
When they had tramped about a mile Toby began
to grumble, and to make caustic allusions to
Matthew’s appearance and horsemanship. Tarkal-
son, having blown the trumpet for him, felt in-
clined to kick that person.
“ Don’t think th’ skiter ken sit a kick-up,” he
confided savagely to Scully. “ Where’s he travel-
lin’ to, anyway ? ” *
It was soon clear where Conyers was travelling
to. A mile and a-half from the homestead was a
boggy spring, and into this he led Slinger till the
horse was bogged up to his knees, and he began
to bog himself. Then he prepared to mount. He
had a monkey-hold rigged on the off-side of the
pommel, which he took a firm grip of before he
left the ground. Slinger stood pretty quiet.
He couldn’t do anything else; he was too
well hobbled with black mud. Still, when he
felt the weight on him, he made a good
effort to get rid of it, plunging desperately through
mud and slush; but he grunted and stumbled
more than he bucked. Conyers went twice over
the pommel, once behind the saddle, and several
times he lost his stirrup irons and stuck a foot
down into the quagmire, to the jeering yells of
Toby and company. But he wasn’t slung ; there
was always some of him on the horse.
Below the bog was a big dam, where the stock
watered. Conyers rode Slinger into it, and swam
him round in it; he flanked him, caught him by
the ears, stood up in the saddle, and took other
liberties with that outlaw. He rode him back
into the bog, back again into the water to wash
him, and so on till sundown ; while Carson and
Scully chiacked him, and Tarkalson threw mud
over him when his back was turned, and called
him things unprintable.
Suddenly Conyers left the water on the dry
side, and headed for the yard at a good jog, the
horse going like an old stager. He was dead
beat. With a yell, Tarkalson rushed after him,
picking up sticks. Scully remonstrated.
“ Let him go, you fool! If he gets to th’ yard
safe, yer’ll win th’ wager.”
“ Win th’ wager be darned !” cried Tarkalson.
“ I never made no wager, ’n’ if that blamed yahoo
don’t fall off I’ll ’ave to pay him a pound. A
pound for that l ” And Bill made renewed efforts
to get near enough to throw a stick at Slinger’s
heels; but, just as there was a prospect of success,
his foot caught in a creeper, and he lost some skin
off his nose in the resultant fall.
When they reached the yard Conyers had un-
saddled, and was sitting on the erate-post, smoking
a cigarette. He looked pleased.
“ I’m goin’ to make a pack-horse of him,” he
informed Tarkalson, with a nod towards the
depreciated outlaw. “Wouldn’t mind gettin’ a
few more at the same price.”- He jumped down,
laughing. “ You might let me have that quid to-
night, Bill, if it’s convenient.”
Tarkalson, dabbing a bleeding nose with a
handkerchief, swore under his breath, and went
and sat down on the anvil.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
What for 35 years has been “ The Aus-
tralian Handbook” becomes now “The Austral-
asian Handbook ”; but inside the covers it
remains still the same old reliable guide to most
things that concern the Commonwealth and
Maoriland. In especial it contains what, in spite
of blemishes, is the finest lot of Australian maps
published in one volume —general maps, railway
maps, mineral and other maps of that sort, news-
paper maps, and invaluable maps of the cities and
suburbs, the only blot on which, is the announce-
ment in the corner that they are printed in Edin-
burgh. But for being printed in Edinburgh, per-
haps, the Benevolent Asylum would not still be a
vast pile of buildings standing where the new
Sydney railway station is. Gordon and Gotch
publish this volume—which is a large guarantee
in itself.
The Sport.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MET Bill S , a sporting crank, going
to his “ club,” the other night. He
asked me to go along with him. Out of
curiosity, I went. Afterwards, I was glad I did.
Bill was down to spout. He spouted :
“ Look here, mates, I tell ’e sport is the main
thing in this ’ere world. Put all yer money in
sport. Every blanky cent. Don’t pay yer butcher
or baker if yer have to neglect sport. What we
want is a race of true sportsmen.”
I left the club after Bill’s famous speech.
Two days later I saw Bill at a football match.
Bill’s fancy club was playing in the final.
“ ’Eie y’ are,” roared Bill, “4 to 1 on our
fellahs ! ”
Bets were numerous. Bill had all his money on.
The game started. Bill’s team was going down
badly.
He raved ; he wildly clutched his hair; he
swore; he cursed.
# “ Umpire’s a damned cheat! ”he yelled. “You
rotten ,” he roared, and rushed madly at
the goal-umpire for “ robbing ” a goal. He banged
a player, and pulled off his coat with an oath.
“ Fight any man on the ground,” he shouted.
This was too much for me, remembering Bill’s
famous speech.
1 could stand it no longer.
“Be a true sportsman, Bill,” I cried. “We
want a race of true sportsmen.”
“Ain’t I a true sportsman?” hollered Bill.
“Ain’t I got all my money on this match? I’ll
fight any man on this ground ! The umpire’s a
damned cheat—a rot ”
I laughed. Bill spotted me. I had to scoot for
That was the last I saw of Bill. Bill was a true
sportsman. He put all his money on sport. He
lost. He went mad over it.

Margaret.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
** ~W~ ~T"P in the morchary, sir, she lies,”
m / Said thehospital porter, with weary eyes;
“ You orter look at her, cold art clean,
Most beautiful woman ever I seen.”
Still sweet loveliness lying there,
With your white hands crossed on your marble
breast,
Is life so good—with a death so fair ?
Is joy so kind —with so cool a rest ?
What peace of the spirit your pale face shows,
Rare dead woman whom no man knows !
Pure proud oval of face and chin,
Perfectly framed in the full hair’s brown !
Were there kisses and laughter for love to win
On those cold carved lips where my eyes look
down ?
Small white hand with never a stain,
Did it clasp in bliss to be clasped again ?
“ She fell in th ’ street,” spoke the porter low,
“ Art she died in th’ keb four hour ago.
She weren’t ill-fed, art ’er ’ands ain’t rough,
But the clothes she was wearirt was pore enough.
The p’leece found notliink at all, I b’leef,
’Cept ‘ Margret,’ wrote on a ’angkercheef.”
Was there sorrow, girl, in your life, or shame ?
Your heart, whose blood is now choked and thick,
Was it broken and torn when the death-grip came,
Or was joy still urging it warm and quick ?
Margaret, girl with the frozen brow,
Did they love you then as I love you now ?
Though life be dear, it were dearer yet
To-day like you on the stone to lie,
And clasped to the bosom of Death, forget
The blossoming trees and the warm blue sky,
To sink with you in the quiet grave
With the crown of beauty his cold hand gave.
Shall I do you wrong if I touch you—so ?
Will your dead hands stir if I touch them, dear ?
I draw the veil o’er your face of snow,
And say good-bye ; you are fairer here,
I ween, than you were in those days of mirth,
When you smiled in joy on the good, glad earth.
“ There’s some looks awful,” the porter said,
“ But some looks beautiful la,yin’ dead.”
VIC.
PRIAM.
Mr. Barnes in Society.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ XT’s good for a man to be married,”
remarked Mr. Barnes, sadly and thought-
fully. “It keeps ’im from broodin’ on ’is
other troubles.”
He kicked the attenuated fire resentfully.
“Booh!” he shivered, “it’s c-cold. There’ll
be icebergs down south, I’m thinkin’. The
trouble is, wimmin wears so badly after marriage.
Most on ’em gits ugly, and all on era’ gits kids.
Bah ! some on ’em gits nothin’ else—bar the
sassi-ty people. Rotters! I ’ates them ’igh-
clawss swells. Only ’ad to do with ’em once.
“ It wuz through a mate o’ mine—the Dook we
called ’im—’cos ’e come frum Ingland an’ dressed
an’ washed like sif every day wuz Sunday. Now,
though I’m a lib’ral-minded man meseif, I don’t
usually ’old with forriners an’ sich, but this ’ere
Dook wuz a white man —mighter been born
Australian.
“ ’E’d bin somethin’ high in his own country,
an*ad done somethin’, so come out ’ere.
“ That’s jist it. The wasters come out ’ere—
we make men of ’em, and then they go away
and curse us.
“ The Dook an’ me was in Melbourne where we
’ad gone to sell some ’igh -steppin’ pals of ’is a
little mine we ’ad been workin’ out past Mt.
Warrenheip. A splendid mine it were—only
wanted a little capital to develop it—full o’ beauti-
ful specimens. We knew—’cos we put ’em there.
“ The Dook raised some money—’imself an’ the
devil knows how —and we lived at the best pub.
an’ splashed round like kings. All went well at
first, and the Toorak mugs came round like lambs
and looked at our specimens an’ went away with
their mouths watering. Yus, all went well till
the Dook excepted an inveetation for me to
dinner. I kicked against it, but the Dook said it
was to the ’ouse of the brumby we wanted to ear-
mark. ’E dressed me out in an evenin’ suit till I
looked like that Italian waiter at Moriarty’s at
Coleraine. Mr. ’Erbert Rixon was the name of
our mark, an’ what a ’ouse ’e ’ad ! I uster think
Moriarty’s at Coleraine was clawss, but lor ! it was
a wash’ouse compared to Rixon’s.
“The door was opened by 6ft. of waiter in a
beautiful uniform, like at the the-atre plays, ’oo
took me breath an’ coat away an’ led us into a
room and called out in a loud voice, ‘Captain
Charles Claridge’ (which was the Dook), ‘Mr.
James Barnes’ (what was me). An’ole bird all
over nakidness an’ di-monds come forrard an’
shook our ’ands in a tired sorter way, an’ eyed
me up an’ down through glasses on a stick. ‘ This
is Mr. Barnes,’ said the Dook, ‘whom I was
telling Mr. Rixon about.’ ‘Ha ! ’ said th’ ole gal.
“‘Yer servant, mum,’ sed I, with a perlite
scrape.
“ ‘ A child o’ Natur’,’ whispered the Dook in
Jer ear. ‘ The back-bone of this great country.’
‘Ha!’ sed she agen, still quizzin’ me through
them glasses like I was the sword-swallerin’ cove
at the Dramboo cattle show. Made me pretty
nervous, ’specially as there was a roomful of
Eeople yapping like a dog-show. I thought I’d
etter say somethin’.’
“‘Very ’ot weather to-day; makes me feal
quite sweaty,’ I said.
“ ‘ Indeed,’ sed she (she spoke like an ice-chest)
‘ I find it rather cold.’
“ I looked at the nakedness of ’er. ‘ You would,’
sed I. She went away.
“Just then a white-whiskered ole cove come
bustlin’ up. ‘ Ah, Mr. Barnes, I presoom. ’Ow
d’yer do ? ”
“ ‘Nicely,’ sed I, *’ow’s yerself ?’
We re jist going to dinner. Let me inter-
dooce you Miss Grosvenor—Mr. Barnacles—no,
Barnes.’
“Gosh ! what a boshter gal that was ! She
stuck about two yards outer ’er clothes at the top,
but it was worth it—all the way.
‘“’Ow d’yer?’ I murmured feebly. I could
only look at ’er.
‘“Won’t y’u sit down, Mr.—er—Barnacles,’
she sed sweetly.
“ ‘ My oath,’ sed I, an’ in me nervousness nearly
spilt a table.
“‘You must tell me all about the bush,’she
sed. ‘I love it.’
, kl bush,’ sed I. She ’it me playful with
er fan. I was gettin’ on splendid with’er, and
was jist goin’ to ax ’er to come fer a stroll in the
Eitzroy Gardens the next night, when they started
in for dinner.
“ That dinner was the limit. Never, never did
I think that Jim Barnes would see the time when
’e didn’t know how to eat.
“But I confess. that when I set down and eyed
the ironmongery in front o’ me, a cold chill crept
to me vitals. There was about ten knives and
forks—all silver (I ’ave some on ’em still) set in
front o’ me, and as many spoons an’ forks. A cold
sweat o’ despair broke out on me brow, and I was
glad to see there was a bit of a towel in front
ov me.
“ I felt better when I’d mopped down. Then
a, big bloke of a waiter put a little plate in front
o’ me. There were two tiny green plums on it
wat y’u could ’ave put in yer eye. Olives I think
they was called. I wish I’d ’a’ put ’em there
rather than in me mouth. I was foolish, for I
mistrusted ’em at the start. They didn’t look
ripe. ’Owever, I s’lected a knife and fork from
the bundle and started. I never tasted nuthink
like it in me life. Bitter an’ sour all at oust. I
couldn’t stand it. I simply ’ad to spit it out in
me anguish. It ’it the ole gal opposite, an’ she
went away, amid great excitement. That rattled
me considerable, so that when the bloke come
round with a dish o’ soup an’ I took it from ’im,
me ’and wuz so trembly that I spilt it over the
lovely gal’s dress.
“ She looked sif she wuz goin’ to weep lots, but
pulled ’erself together, and kinder smiled on one
side of ’er face and reckoned it didn’t matter
nothink; it wuz an old dress, and she hated
it anyhow an’ wuz goin’ to give it away to ’er
washerwoman.
“ No one’ll ever know what I suffered during
that dinner. The Dook sed ’e suffered too. I’rn
jolly glad. It wuz all ’is fault. There wuz about
40 rounds of it, but lor, they wouldn’t ’a’ made a
square feed fer a sparrer.
“Talkin’ o’ them, there was some things called
sparrer-grass. I couldn’t touch ’em. My gal
sed, ‘ Don’t y’u like the sparrer-grass ? ’ ‘ No,’
sed I, ‘the blamed things is ’arf boiled.’ ‘ Y’u’re
bitin’ the wrong end,’ sed she, gigglin’. The
other end was certainly softer but they was rotten
eny’ow—not fit fer a white man. The drink wuz
the only thing I could understand. Dozens o’
different wines. I tried ’em all several times. It
made me feel ’appier—more confident. Still the
knives an’ forks an’ spoons an’ things worried
me considerable. I never knew which to use.
They was all sizes an’ shapes—big, little, an’
middlin’ sized. The knives was that sharp, I
near cut me jore every time I ventered a mouth-
ful. Half-way through I grew cunnin’. I’d wait
to see what ones me neighbor, the beautiful gal
with the few clothes on, would pick up, an’ then
I’d copy her. But even that didn’t work any too
well. At three-quarter time I found she was
five knives, three forks, an’ two spoons ahead o’
me. I was fair boxed up.
“But t’wards the finish I got a terrible fright.
’Twas when they brought the fruit on. The
waiter-bloke give me a plate with another knife,
fork an’ spoon, an’ a little bowl full o’ liquor wat
I took for gin. It was the first chance I ’ad ’ad to
git a decent drink —a man’s drink—so, bein’
thirsty, I tossed it off without a second thought.
It was ’orrible. It tasted like nuthin’.
“My Gawd, I’m pisened ! ” I spluttered,
jumpin’up. Then I see’d the waiter be’ind me
grinnin’ like a joss.
“ I’ll learn y’ to put up a joke on yer betters, y’
cow,” I yelled, an’ landed ’im. There was great
excitement. Ole ladies fainted, while the young
’ups wuz all ’air-pins an’ hystericks. Ole Rixon
grabbed the Dook an’ yelled : ‘ Take ’im away !
Take ’im an’ yer sumthink mine outer me ’ouse.”
Then they tole me it wuz on’y water to wash yer
fingers in. I ’ad fergot wot water tasted like.
“Ole man Rixon danced up very red about the
gills, an’ foamed an’ raved an’ cursed. ‘ Outer me
’ouse ! outer me ’ouse ! ’ was all he could splutter.
An’ all the rest was squealin’ an’ snortin’, an’ the
waiter was moppin’ his eye.
“To all the row I showed a dignified silence,
beyond tellin’. ’em to go to somewhere warm an’
bracin’. I thought ole Rixon was agoin’ to throw
a aperplectic fit, so I pushed ’is sinful ole head
into a dish of ice-cream to cool it.
“ Then the wine in me grew excited. I jumped
onto the table an’ told ’em in a few chosen words
what I thought of ’em, their manners, their
lack o’ clothin’, their style o’ feedm’ ; an’
touched on many other matters of interest. Then,
seein’ an army o’ flunkeys gettin’ ready for attack,
I bashed out as many of the lights as I could. I
filled me arms full o’apples, oranges, pines, an’
other vegetables, an’ took cover be’ind the floral
decorations, an’ gave battle.
“There was a wild stampede for the door.
Two fat ole gents got jammed ’ard, an’ blocked
the fairway. A pine-apple or two in the back
relieved the pressure, an’ soon the room wuz
empty but for about 15 flunkeys, the Dook an’
meseif. The waiters was town bred, and’ unused
to warfare, so that a few well-d’rected volleys of
peaches an’ forks dispersed them, an’ I turned me
attention to the Dook.
“ ‘E wanted me to come ’ome, an’ called me
names; but I was jist beginnin’ to enjoy the party,
so I let ’im ’ave a ’andful o’ nuts an’ a fistful or
two o’ strawberries to go on with, and struck ’im
in the weskit with a bottle. ’E didn’t wait for
more, but retreated hurriedly through the
window. So, havin’ dispersed all me enemies
single-’anded, I locked the doors an’ windows.
Then I emptied a decanter or two to counteract
the effects of the water.
“I woke late next afternoon, and found meseif
back in the hotel. The Dook was in the room
lookin’ very wild an’ worried. I felt very, very
ill. Muster bin that water I drunk. The stirrin’
events of the previous evenin’ was a blank at first.
My head was hazy.
“‘Well,’ I asked ’im, ‘did y’ sell the mine?
What was the result ? ’
“‘The result,’ sed’e, kinder sorrowful. ‘The
result is that y’ are a good enuf chap out back, but
hardly—well, hardly a success in ’lgh Sassi’ty.
It took me all me time ter keep yer out o’ gaol,
and the mine isn’t sold an’ never will be.’
“Well, they can keep all their fancy drinks,”
concluded the old gentleman in a loud voice. “If
y’ was to offer me—now —a bucketful o’ champine
or a pint o’ plain, ’onest beer, I know which I’d
’ave.
“ Thankee kindly.”
